### [1] [Self-Execution Simulation Improves Coding Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03253)
*Gallil Maimon,Ori Yoran,Felix Kreuk,Michael Hassid,Gal Cohen,Pierre Chambon,Yossi Adi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They train code-focused LLMs to explicitly simulate program execution step by step, then use this skill plus verifiable rewards to improve competitive programming performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Code LLMs often fail because they cannot reliably anticipate how their generated programs will actually run, leading to logical or edge‑case errors. Existing reasoning or planning methods do not fully fix this mismatch between generation and true execution, especially in competitive programming where correctness is binary and strict. The paper aims to close this gap by teaching LLMs to mentally ‘run’ code and use that to self‑check and repair their own solutions.

Method: They fine‑tune code LLMs with supervised learning on natural language execution traces that describe stepwise program behavior grounded in actual runs. On top of this, they apply reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (e.g., passing tests) and design two objectives: (1) predict program outputs given code and inputs by simulating execution, and (2) solve competitive programming problems while using either ground‑truth or model‑predicted execution traces as feedback. The model uses these capabilities for self‑verification of multiple candidate solutions and iterative self‑fixing by simulating test execution and revising code.

Result: Across several competitive programming benchmarks, models trained with execution simulation and verifiable RL achieve higher solution rates and outperform baselines that rely on standard chain‑of‑thought or other reasoning methods. Ablation studies show that the explicit execution‑simulation component is a key contributor to performance gains, and they analyze where this simulation helps and where it breaks down.

Conclusion: Explicitly training code LLMs to simulate program execution, combined with reinforcement learning from verifiable outcomes, improves their ability to solve competitive programming tasks. Execution‑based self‑verification and self‑fixing are effective mechanisms for increasing code correctness, though the paper also identifies limits to how far current models can faithfully simulate complex executions.

Abstract: A promising research direction in enabling LLMs to generate consistently correct code involves addressing their inability to properly estimate program execution, particularly for code they generate. In this work, we demonstrate that Code LLMs can be trained to simulate program execution in a step-by-step manner and that this capability can be leveraged to improve competitive programming performance. Our approach combines supervised fine-tuning on natural language execution traces, textual explanations grounded in true execution, with reinforcement learning using verifiable rewards. We introduce two complementary objectives: output prediction given code and inputs, and solving competitive programming tasks with either ground-truth or self-predicted execution feedback. These objectives enable models to perform self-verification over multiple candidate solutions, and iterative self-fixing by simulating test execution. Across multiple competitive programming benchmarks, our method yields consistent improvements over standard reasoning approaches. We further present ablations and analysis to elucidate the role of execution simulation and its limitations.

</details>


### [2] [Robust LLM Performance Certification via Constrained Maximum Likelihood Estimation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03257)
*Minghe Shen,Ananth Balashankar,Adam Fisch,David Madras,Miguel Rodrigues*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a constrained maximum-likelihood method to estimate LLM failure rates accurately and efficiently by combining a small human-labeled set, many LLM-judge labels, and domain constraints, outperforming existing approaches like PPI.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Safe deployment of LLMs requires accurate estimates of how often they fail. Human evaluation is accurate but expensive and small-scale; automatic schemes like using another LLM as a judge are cheap but biased and unreliable. Current methods don’t fully exploit side information about judge behavior and often yield high-variance or biased estimates of failure rates.

Method: Use constrained maximum-likelihood estimation that fuses three information sources: (i) a small human-labeled calibration dataset, (ii) a large dataset labeled by an automatic LLM judge, and (iii) domain-specific constraints that encode prior knowledge or bounds on the judge’s performance (e.g., bounds on sensitivity, specificity, or error rates). Formulate an MLE problem over model failure rates and judge performance parameters, then impose these constraints to regularize and disambiguate the estimation. Implement and solve this constrained MLE and compare against baselines such as Prediction-Powered Inference.

Result: Across a variety of simulated and/or real settings with different judge accuracies, calibration set sizes, and true failure rates, the constrained MLE method achieves more accurate (lower bias) and more stable (lower variance) estimates of LLM failure rates than Prediction-Powered Inference and other baselines.

Conclusion: By explicitly modeling and constraining the behavior of automatic judges, their framework turns noisy LLM-judge labels plus limited human ground truth into reliable failure-rate estimates. This provides a more principled, interpretable, and scalable path for certifying LLM reliability than treating automatic judges as inscrutable black boxes.

Abstract: The ability to rigorously estimate the failure rates of large language models (LLMs) is a prerequisite for their safe deployment. Currently, however, practitioners often face a tradeoff between expensive human gold standards and potentially severely-biased automatic annotation schemes such as "LLM-as-a-Judge" labeling. In this paper, we propose a new, practical, and efficient approach to LLM failure rate estimation based on constrained maximum-likelihood estimation (MLE). Our method integrates three distinct signal sources: (i) a small, high-quality human-labeled calibration set, (ii) a large corpus of LLM-judge annotations, and, most importantly, (iii) additional side information via domain-specific constraints derived from known bounds on judge performance statistics. We validate our approach through a comprehensive empirical study, benchmarking it against state-of-the-art baselines like Prediction-Powered Inference (PPI). Across diverse experimental regimes -- spanning varying judge accuracies, calibration set sizes, and LLM failure rates -- our constrained MLE consistently delivers more accurate and lower-variance estimates than existing methods. By moving beyond the "black-box" use of automated judges to a flexible framework, we provide a principled, interpretable, and scalable pathway towards LLM failure-rate certification.

</details>


### [3] [SoLA: Leveraging Soft Activation Sparsity and Low-Rank Decomposition for Large Language Model Compression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03258)
*Xinhao Huang,You-Liang Huang,Zeyi Wen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: SoLA is a training-free compression method for LLMs that exploits activation sparsity and low-rank decomposition to significantly shrink models while even improving perplexity and downstream accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models have billions of parameters, making them expensive and difficult to deploy. Existing compression and slimming approaches often need special hardware, fine-tuning, or costly post-training to keep performance acceptable. The paper is motivated by the need for an efficient, affordable, and training-free way to compress LLMs while preserving or improving their quality.

Method: The method, SoLA, analyzes activation patterns in the feed-forward networks of modern LLMs and observes soft activation sparsity. Based on this, it separates FFN components into a small subset that contributes strongly to inference and a large subset that can be compressed. The important minority of components are retained as-is, while the rest are compressed using low-rank decomposition. To reduce information loss from decomposition, SoLA introduces an adaptive component-wise low-rank allocation strategy that decides different truncation ranks/positions for different weight matrices instead of using a uniform rank. The entire procedure is training-free and applied directly to pretrained LLaMA-2 and Mistral models.

Result: On LLaMA-2 models with 7B, 13B, and 70B parameters and on Mistral-7B, SoLA achieves substantial compression while improving performance without any post-training. For instance, at a 30% compression rate on LLaMA-2-70B, SoLA lowers perplexity from 6.95 to 4.44 and increases downstream task accuracy by 10%, outperforming the previous state-of-the-art compression methods under similar compression ratios.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that SoLA provides an effective, training-free approach to compress large language models by exploiting soft activation sparsity and low-rank structure in FFNs. Its adaptive low-rank allocation mitigates decomposition loss, enabling significant parameter reduction while not only maintaining but often improving language modeling and downstream task performance, and doing so without specialized hardware or post-training costs.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have demonstrated impressive capabilities across various tasks, but the billion-scale parameters pose deployment challenges. Although existing methods attempt to reduce the scale of LLMs, they require either special hardware support or expensive post-training to maintain model quality. To facilitate efficient and affordable model slimming, we propose a novel training-free compression method for LLMs, named "SoLA", which leverages \textbf{So}ft activation sparsity and \textbf{L}ow-r\textbf{A}nk decomposition. SoLA can identify and retain a minority of components significantly contributing to inference, while compressing the majority through low-rank decomposition, based on our analysis of the activation pattern in the feed-forward network (FFN) of modern LLMs. To alleviate the decomposition loss, SoLA is equipped with an adaptive component-wise low-rank allocation strategy to assign appropriate truncation positions for different weight matrices. We conduct extensive experiments on LLaMA-2-7B/13B/70B and Mistral-7B models across a variety of benchmarks. SoLA exhibits remarkable improvement in both language modeling and downstream task accuracy without post-training. For example, with a 30\% compression rate on the LLaMA-2-70B model, SoLA surpasses the state-of-the-art method by reducing perplexity from 6.95 to 4.44 and enhancing downstream task accuracy by 10\%.

</details>


### [4] [Why Attend to Everything? Focus is the Key](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03260)
*Hengshuai Yao,Xing Chen,Ahmed Murtadha,Jin Li,Shuai Shao,Yasin Abbasi Yadkori,Guan Wang,Mingli Yuan,William Chen,Sen Song*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Focus is an additive, training-light attention modification that learns token-group centroids to sparsify distant attention while keeping base model weights frozen, improving perplexity and inference speed without hurting downstream performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Efficient attention methods typically approximate all token pairs or require retraining, often hurting downstream performance and alignment when retrofitted to existing models. There is a need for an attention mechanism that can be added on top of frozen LMs, improves domain performance, accelerates inference, and preserves alignment without custom kernels.

Method: Introduce learnable centroids that assign tokens to groups. Long-range (distant) attention is restricted to tokens within the same group, while local attention remains dense. Centroids are trained with all original model weights frozen, adding as few as ~148K parameters. During inference, each token is restricted to its top-k groups, converting soft routing to a hard sparsity pattern. This pattern is implemented via two standard FlashAttention calls, and Sinkhorn normalization is used to keep group sizes balanced. The method is tested from 124M to 70B parameters and across five attention architectures, and compared with LoRA and full attention baselines.

Result: Centroid-only training improves in-domain perplexity across scales (124M–70B) with no degradation on downstream tasks. At 124M parameters, Focus outperforms full attention (30.3 vs 31.4 perplexity). When trained from scratch at 7B on 2B tokens, Focus again beats full attention (13.82 vs 13.89 perplexity). With hard sparsity at inference, Focus attains 2x speedup while improving perplexity over the pretrained baseline (41.3 vs 42.8). Implemented via two FlashAttention calls, it reaches up to 8.6x wall-clock speedup on 1M-token sequences without custom kernels. Compared to LoRA, Focus preserves instruction-tuning alignment: TruthfulQA scores stay intact, whereas LoRA degrades them across learning rates and ranks. Learned groups exhibit interpretable linguistic structure.

Conclusion: Focus provides a retrofit-friendly, additive attention mechanism that improves domain perplexity, preserves or improves downstream and alignment performance, and yields substantial inference speedups by learning token-group centroids and restricting long-range attention within groups. It can be applied to existing models of various sizes and architectures without modifying or fine-tuning their original weights, and produces interpretable token groupings via balanced centroid routing.

Abstract: We introduce Focus, a method that learns which token pairs matter rather than approximating all of them. Learnable centroids assign tokens to groups; distant attention is restricted to same-group pairs while local attention operates at full resolution. Because all model weights stay frozen, Focus is purely additive: centroid-only training (as few as 148K parameters) improves domain perplexity with zero degradation on downstream benchmarks--from 124M to 70B parameters, across five attention architectures. No existing efficient attention method achieves this in the retrofit setting. At 124M, Focus surpasses full attention (30.3 vs 31.4 PPL); trained from scratch at 7B scale (2B tokens), Focus again beats full attention (13.82 vs 13.89 PPL). At inference, restricting each token to its top-k highest-scoring groups discretizes the soft routing into a hard sparsity pattern, yielding 2x speedup while beating the pretrained baseline (41.3 vs 42.8 PPL); decomposing this pattern into two standard FlashAttention calls reaches 8.6x wall-clock speedup at 1M tokens with no custom kernels. Unlike LoRA, centroid routing preserves alignment: instruction-tuned models retain TruthfulQA scores after adaptation, while LoRA degrades at every learning rate and rank. Sinkhorn normalization enforces balanced groups as a hard constraint, and the resulting groups discover interpretable linguistic categories without supervision.

</details>


### [5] [VIGIL: An Extensible System for Real-Time Detection and Mitigation of Cognitive Bias Triggers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03261)
*Bo Kang,Sander Noels,Tijl De Bie*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce VIGIL, a browser extension that detects and mitigates cognitive-bias triggers in online text in real time, using LLMs and an extensible plugin system.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Generative AI increases risks to information integrity and civic discourse, not only via outright misinformation but also via subtle persuasive manipulation that exploits human cognitive biases. Existing media literacy and transparency tools focus on factual accuracy and source reliability/ideology, but there are no tools that directly detect or mitigate cognitive-bias triggers themselves. The authors aim to fill this gap.

Method: They design and implement VIGIL, a browser extension that does: (1) real-time, scroll-synced detection of cognitive-bias triggers in online content; (2) LLM-powered text reformulation that mitigates these triggers while remaining reversible so the user can always see the original; (3) multi-tier privacy options, allowing inference from fully offline to cloud-based. The system is built as an extensible platform that supports third-party NLP plugins, several of which are included and validated on NLP benchmarks.

Result: They deliver a working open-source system (VIGIL) with multiple integrated plugins that have been rigorously evaluated against NLP benchmarks. The tool operates in real time in the browser and supports different privacy tiers and reformulation capabilities.

Conclusion: VIGIL demonstrates that it is feasible to provide real-time, in-situ detection and mitigation of cognitive-bias triggers in online information via a browser extension, going beyond traditional fact-checking and source analysis. By being extensible and open source, it establishes a platform for further research and development of cognitive bias–aware tools to protect online civic discourse.

Abstract: The rise of generative AI is posing increasing risks to online information integrity and civic discourse. Most concretely, such risks can materialise in the form of mis- and disinformation. As a mitigation, media-literacy and transparency tools have been developed to address factuality of information and the reliability and ideological leaning of information sources. However, a subtler but possibly no less harmful threat to civic discourse is to use of persuasion or manipulation by exploiting human cognitive biases and related cognitive limitations. To the best of our knowledge, no tools exist to directly detect and mitigate the presence of triggers of such cognitive biases in online information. We present VIGIL (VIrtual GuardIan angeL), the first browser extension for real-time cognitive bias trigger detection and mitigation, providing in-situ scroll-synced detection, LLM-powered reformulation with full reversibility, and privacy-tiered inference from fully offline to cloud. VIGIL is built to be extensible with third-party plugins, with several plugins that are rigorously validated against NLP benchmarks are already included. It is open-sourced at https://github.com/aida-ugent/vigil.

</details>


### [6] [Knowledge Packs: Zero-Token Knowledge Delivery via KV Cache Injection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03270)
*Andrey Pustovit*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Knowledge Packs, pre-computed KV caches that can replace RAG context tokens, delivering equivalent knowledge at near-zero token cost while also enabling new behavior steering capabilities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) consumes many prompt tokens by inserting retrieved documents into the context, which is costly and inefficient. The authors want to avoid this token overhead while still giving models access to external knowledge, and also explore whether the KV-cache interface can be used to steer model behavior in ways that RAG cannot.

Method: They precompute key–value (KV) caches from forward passes over knowledge text (F) and then later reuse these caches when answering queries (q). For causal transformers, they show that the KV cache produced by F alone is mathematically identical to the portion of the cache that would result from a single joint pass on F+q, as implied by the causal mask. They empirically validate this equivalence on Qwen3-8B and Llama-3.1-8B, carefully controlling chat template formatting to avoid artifacts. They then analyze how to manipulate cached values for behavioral steering: using properties of RoPE (which rotates keys but not values), they apply contrastive deltas in mid-layer value states, study their geometric structure (orthogonality, composability), and test joint use of knowledge KV and steering KV at various scaling factors (alpha).

Result: With correct prompt/chat-template formatting, they find no divergences between Knowledge Pack KV reuse and standard RAG-style token context over 700 questions on Qwen3-8B and Llama-3.1-8B, while achieving up to 95% token savings. Misformatted templates cause 6–7 percentage point performance drops, which they argue explains earlier reports that KV-based approaches outperform RAG. For behavioral steering, they show that value-only contrastive deltas in mid layers (roughly 33–66% depth) can reliably nudge model behavior without breaking coherence, whereas manipulating keys leads to degradation. The steering directions appear nearly orthogonal and can be composed, and both knowledge injection and behavioral steering can be run simultaneously at alpha ≤ 0.7 without mutual interference.

Conclusion: Knowledge Packs demonstrate that pre-computed KV caches can faithfully replace RAG-style textual retrieval for causal transformers, eliminating nearly all token overhead while preserving performance, provided that chat template formatting is correct. The work also shows that the KV-cache interface, particularly value states in mid layers, is a powerful handle for behavior steering that coexists with knowledge injection without retraining or weight changes. This suggests a practical path toward efficient, controllable LLM systems that operate via cache-level operations rather than continuously re-tokenizing large contexts.

Abstract: RAG wastes tokens. We propose Knowledge Packs: pre-computed KV caches that deliver the same knowledge at zero token cost. For causal transformers, the KV cache from a forward pass on text F is identical to what a joint pass on F+q would produce - this follows directly from the causal mask. The equivalence is exact but fragile: wrong chat template formatting causes 6-7pp degradation, which we believe explains prior claims of KV outperforming RAG. With correct formatting: zero divergences across 700 questions on Qwen3-8B and Llama-3.1-8B, up to 95% token savings. The KV interface also enables behavioral steering that RAG cannot do. Because RoPE rotates keys but leaves values untouched, contrastive deltas on cached values can nudge model behavior while key arithmetic destroys coherence. The effect sits in mid-layer values (33-66%), independent directions are nearly orthogonal (cos~0) and compose, and both channels - knowledge and steering - run simultaneously at alpha<=0.7 without interference. No training, no weight modification.

</details>


### [7] [CresOWLve: Benchmarking Creative Problem-Solving Over Real-World Knowledge](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03374)
*Mete Ismayilzada,Renqing Cuomao,Daniil Yurshevich,Anna Sotnikova,Lonneke van der Plas,Antoine Bosselut*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents CresOWLve, a benchmark that tests large language models on real-world, creativity-demanding puzzles that require integrating diverse knowledge and creative reasoning, revealing a substantial performance gap between factual recall and creative problem-solving.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM benchmarks typically assess narrow cognitive abilities (e.g., factual recall, logical reasoning) and many creativity tests use artificial puzzles that fail to capture real-world creative problem-solving. There is a need for an evaluation framework that reflects how creative thinking operates in practice, involving multiple strategies and real-world knowledge integration.

Method: The authors design CresOWLve, a benchmark composed of puzzles rooted in real-world knowledge that demand various creative thinking strategies (logical reasoning, lateral thinking, analogy, commonsense). They then evaluate several state-of-the-art LLMs, categorized as non-thinking and thinking models, on this benchmark and compare performance on creative versus purely factual questions, analyzing where models succeed or fail.

Result: Across evaluated models, CresOWLve is consistently challenging: models perform significantly worse on creative problems than on factual ones, with performance gaps up to a 17% drop. The analysis indicates that while LLMs can frequently retrieve relevant domain knowledge, their accuracy degrades when they must use that knowledge in non-obvious, integrative, and creative ways.

Conclusion: CresOWLve exposes a key limitation of current LLMs: they are relatively strong at factual retrieval but weak at forming the non-obvious connections required for creative problem-solving in realistic settings. This benchmark provides a more faithful test of creative reasoning and highlights the need for methods that enhance models’ ability to integrate diverse knowledge sources in flexible, innovative ways.

Abstract: Creative problem-solving requires combining multiple cognitive abilities, including logical reasoning, lateral thinking, analogy-making, and commonsense knowledge, to discover insights that connect seemingly unrelated pieces of information. However, most existing benchmarks for large language models (LLMs) evaluate only specific components of this process. Moreover, many creativity-oriented benchmarks rely on artificially constructed brainteasers or contrived scenarios that do not reflect how creative problem-solving occurs in real-world settings. To address this gap, we introduce CresOWLve, a benchmark for evaluating creative problem-solving using puzzles grounded in real-world knowledge. Problems in CresOWLve require employing multiple creative thinking strategies, retrieving facts from diverse domains, and creatively combining them to arrive at a solution. Evaluating several frontier non-thinking and thinking LLMs, we show that CresOWLve remains highly challenging. Our analysis reveals a consistent performance gap: models perform substantially better on factual questions than on creative ones (up to a -17% drop). While models can often retrieve the relevant knowledge, they struggle to form the non-obvious creative connections required to integrate this information and arrive at the correct answer.

</details>


### [8] [Noise Steering for Controlled Text Generation: Improving Diversity and Reading-Level Fidelity in Arabic Educational Story Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03380)
*Haziq Mohammad Khalid,Salsabeel Shapsough,Imran Zualkernan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They test adding controlled noise inside Arabic language models during inference to make early-grade reading assessment stories more diverse without retraining.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Arabic early-grade reading assessments need many short stories that strictly satisfy vocabulary, difficulty, and structural constraints. Standard sampling for diversity often breaks these constraints, raises reading level, or leads to repetitive plots, which harms assessment validity. The authors want a way to generate diverse but still pedagogically valid stories from existing small Arabic LMs without expensive retraining.

Method: They use “noise steering”: adding calibrated Gaussian noise to internal transformer representations at inference time as a training-free way to increase diversity. They experiment with five 7–9B-parameter Arabic-centric LMs and compare four internal-noise injection strategies, including residual stream noise and attention entropy noise injection (AENI), against high-temperature output sampling. They systematically evaluate outputs on narrative diversity, text quality, adherence to strict constraints, and estimated reading grade level.

Result: Residual stream noise consistently increases narrative diversity while maintaining output quality, constraint adherence, and early-grade reading level across all tested models. AENI makes attention-logit noise more stable and helps recover quality. In contrast, simply increasing sampling temperature at the output layer both raises the reading level and can cause catastrophic generation failures for several models.

Conclusion: Perturbing internal representations during decoding is a better strategy than traditional high-temperature output sampling for generating constrained educational content: it yields more diverse Arabic early-grade reading passages while preserving quality and pedagogy-relevant constraints, without needing model retraining.

Abstract: Generating diverse, pedagogically valid stories for Arabic early-grade reading assessments requires balancing tight constraints on vocabulary, reading level, and narrative structure against the need to avoid repetitive plots that undermine assessment validity. We investigate noise steering, injecting calibrated Gaussian perturbations into the internal representations of transformer models at inference time, as a training-free diversity method evaluated across five small Arabic-centric language models (7-9B parameters). We compare four injection strategies against high-temperature sampling baselines, measuring diversity, quality, constraint adherence, and reading grade level. Residual stream noise consistently improves narrative diversity with minimal quality or constraint cost and preserves early-grade reading level across all models. Attention entropy noise injection (AENI) stabilizes the otherwise unreliable attention-logit noise while recovering quality. High-temperature sampling inflates reading grade level and causes catastrophic collapse on several models. We find internal representation-level perturbation to be a more suitable diversity strategy than output-level stochasticity for constrained educational content generation.

</details>


### [9] [Are Arabic Benchmarks Reliable? QIMMA's Quality-First Approach to LLM Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03395)
*Leen AlQadi,Ahmed Alzubaidi,Mohammed Alyafeai,Hamza Alobeidli,Maitha Alhammadi,Shaikha Alsuwaidi,Omar Alkaabi,Basma El Amel Boussaha,Hakim Hacid*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: QIMMA is a curated, quality-assured leaderboard and benchmark suite for evaluating Arabic LLMs, emphasizing systematic validation of existing benchmarks before use.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Arabic LLM benchmarks often aggregate datasets without sufficient quality checks, leading to noisy, biased, or unreliable evaluations. The authors aim to provide a trustworthy, reproducible evaluation ecosystem tailored to Arabic, where benchmark quality is validated rather than assumed.

Method: They build a multi-model assessment pipeline that uses automated LLM-based judgment plus human review to inspect and clean existing Arabic benchmarks. Systematic issues are identified and resolved before inclusion. The final suite spans multiple domains and tasks, mostly from native Arabic sources, and is implemented transparently using frameworks like LightEval and EvalPlus, with public per-sample inference outputs.

Result: The outcome is QIMMA, a curated evaluation suite with over 52k samples across multiple domains and tasks, mostly in native Arabic, with code tasks as a language-agnostic exception. The infrastructure, data, and model outputs are released to enable reproducible comparisons of Arabic LLMs.

Conclusion: QIMMA provides a robust, extensible foundation for evaluating Arabic LLMs by centering benchmark validation and transparency. It improves trust in reported performance, supports community contributions, and can serve as a standard reference point for future Arabic NLP evaluation work.

Abstract: We present QIMMA, a quality-assured Arabic LLM leaderboard that places systematic benchmark validation at its core. Rather than aggregating existing resources as-is, QIMMA applies a multi-model assessment pipeline combining automated LLM judgment with human review to surface and resolve systematic quality issues in well-established Arabic benchmarks before evaluation. The result is a curated, multi-domain, multi-task evaluation suite of over 52k samples, grounded predominantly in native Arabic content; code evaluation tasks are the sole exception, as they are inherently language-agnostic. Transparent implementation via LightEval, EvalPlus and public release of per-sample inference outputs make QIMMA a reproducible and community-extensible foundation for Arabic NLP evaluation.

</details>


### [10] [Towards a theory of morphology-driven marking in the lexicon: The case of the state](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03422)
*Mohamed El Idrissi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Paper proposes a formal model (morphology-driven marking) to explain how noun categories are morphologically realized across languages, using Riffian as a core case and extending to other languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although all languages have nouns, how they are realized morphologically and syntactically varies widely. Existing accounts of markedness, state, and noun inflection do not fully capture cross-linguistic variation or systematically relate semantic, cognitive, and morphosyntactic aspects. The authors want a unified model that explains intra- and cross-linguistic differences in noun marking.

Method: Use Riffian (a Berber language) as a detailed reference system, analyzing its noun morphology and syntactic behavior. From this, abstract a formal model called morphology-driven marking, in which nouns are grouped into modular cognitive sets, each with a dedicated morphological template and an unmarked form. Apply and test this model comparatively on other languages to see how it captures differences in noun marking and state systems, and integrate it with syntactic functions to reassess markedness and state.

Result: The paper develops the morphology-driven marking model and shows that organizing nouns into cognitive sets with distinct morphological templates accounts for variation in noun marking within Riffian and across languages. It demonstrates that traditional notions of markedness and state are insufficient and that these patterns align systematically with syntactic functions when viewed through the new model.

Conclusion: Noun realization differences across languages can be systematically modeled via morphology-driven marking, where noun types belong to modular cognitive sets with specific morphological templates and unmarked forms. This framework clarifies how and why particular noun types show different marking patterns, and it motivates reconceptualizing state as a general, cross-linguistic, syntax-based inflectional category—on a par with agreement and case—applicable to all synthetic languages.

Abstract: All languages have a noun category, but its realisation varies considerably. Depending on the language, semantic and/or morphosyntactic differences may be more or less pronounced. This paper explores these variations, using Riffian as a reference point before extending the analysis to other languages. We propose a formal model termed morphology-driven marking. Nouns are organised into modular cognitive sets, each with its own morphological template and unmarked form. This approach helps explain differences in marking among noun types within and across languages. By situating these patterns within syntactic functions, we also reassess the notions of markedness and state. It is proposed that the concept of state be extended to all synthetic languages and analysed a novel subcategory of syntax-based inflection like agreement and grammatical case.

</details>


### [11] [The Tool Illusion: Rethinking Tool Use in Web Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03465)
*Renze Lou,Baolin Peng,Wenlin Yao,Qianhui Wu,Hao Cheng,Suman Nath,Wenpeng Yin,Jianfeng Gao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper empirically re-examines tool use for web agents via a large, controlled study, clarifying when and how tools help, and what side effects they introduce.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work suggests that equipping web agents with tools can make them more capable, but prior evidence is limited in scale, inconsistent in methodologies, and sometimes not directly comparable. This leaves open key questions about whether tool use reliably improves performance, what design choices make tools effective, and what unintended consequences may arise. The authors aim to build a stronger empirical foundation to guide future research and practical system design.

Method: The authors conduct an extensive, carefully controlled empirical study of web agents augmented with tools. They systematically vary and compare multiple dimensions: (1) different sources and types of tools; (2) different backbone language models powering the agents; (3) different tool-use frameworks or architectures; and (4) multiple evaluation benchmarks. By holding confounding factors constant where possible, they measure performance differences and analyze patterns to derive generalizable insights about tool effectiveness and side effects.

Result: The study reveals that some previously reported benefits of tool use do not generalize across settings, while other advantages are robust and reproducible. The authors identify conditions under which tools consistently help web agents, highlight design principles associated with more effective tools, and document notable side effects or trade-offs introduced by tool use. Overall, their results revise certain earlier claims and strengthen others using broader, more systematic experimental evidence.

Conclusion: Tool use can be beneficial for web agents, but its impact is nuanced and context-dependent. By providing a large-scale, controlled comparison across tools, models, frameworks, and benchmarks, the paper offers a more reliable empirical picture of when tools help, how they should be designed, and what trade-offs they entail. This work is intended to serve as a solid empirical reference and to guide future research on building and evaluating tool-using web agents.

Abstract: As web agents rapidly evolve, an increasing body of work has moved beyond conventional atomic browser interactions and explored tool use as a higher-level action paradigm. Although prior studies have shown the promise of tools, their conclusions are often drawn from limited experimental scales and sometimes non-comparable settings. As a result, several fundamental questions remain unclear: i) whether tools provide consistent gains for web agents, ii) what practical design principles characterize effective tools, and iii) what side effects tool use may introduce. To establish a stronger empirical foundation for future research, we revisit tool use in web agents through an extensive and carefully controlled study across diverse tool sources, backbone models, tool-use frameworks, and evaluation benchmarks. Our findings both revise some prior conclusions and complement others with broader evidence. We hope this study provides a more reliable empirical basis and inspires future research on tool-use web agents.

### [12] [Vocabulary Dropout for Curriculum Diversity in LLM Co-Evolution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03472)
*Jacob Dineen,Aswin RRV,Zhikun Xu,Ben Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes vocabulary dropout as a simple way to keep problem-generating language models diverse in co-evolutionary self-play, improving math reasoning performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In co-evolutionary self-play for language models, one model generates problems and another solves them, aiming for automatic curriculum learning. However, the problem proposer tends to collapse to a narrow set of problem types that satisfy the reward, reducing diversity and making the curriculum unhelpful. This diversity collapse stalls learning and limits the effectiveness of self-play methods like R-Zero for mathematical reasoning, motivating methods that can sustain diverse problem generation over training.

Method: The authors introduce vocabulary dropout: during both training of the proposer policy and during curriculum generation, they randomly mask parts of the vocabulary in the proposer’s output logits. This hard, non-stationary mask changes over time, preventing the proposer from overfitting to fixed token sequences and forcing exploration of different lexical patterns. They integrate this into an R-Zero self-play setup, using Qwen3-4B and Qwen3-8B as solver/proposer models on mathematical reasoning tasks, and measure diversity both lexically and semantically, as well as downstream solver performance.

Result: With vocabulary dropout, the proposer maintains higher diversity across lexical, semantic, and functional measures throughout training, instead of collapsing to a narrow distribution. For Qwen3-8B, this leads to average solver gains of about +4.4 points, with especially strong improvements on harder, competition-level math benchmarks. The method remains lightweight and does not require changes to the solver architecture or additional supervision.

Conclusion: Explicitly constraining the action space of the problem-generating model—here via vocabulary dropout—can play a role similar to game rules in classical self-play, helping sustain productive co-evolution between proposer and solver in language domains. Vocabulary dropout is presented as a simple yet effective instantiation of this principle, improving mathematical reasoning performance by preserving curriculum diversity in co-evolutionary training.

Abstract: Co-evolutionary self-play, where one language model generates problems and another solves them, promises autonomous curriculum learning without human supervision. In practice, the proposer quickly converges to a narrow distribution of problems that satisfy the reward function. This diversity collapse renders the curriculum uninformative for the solver, stalling the co-evolutionary loop. We introduce vocabulary dropout, a random mask applied to the proposer's output logits during both policy training and curriculum generation, as a lightweight mechanism to sustain diversity. The mask is hard and non-stationary, preventing the proposer from locking into fixed token sequences. Training Qwen3-4B and Qwen3-8B on mathematical reasoning via R-Zero, we find that vocabulary dropout sustains proposer diversity across lexical, semantic, and functional metrics throughout training, and yields solver improvements averaging +4.4 points at 8B, with the largest gains on competition-level benchmarks. Our findings suggest that explicit action-space constraints, analogous to the structural role that game rules play in classical self-play, can help sustain productive co-evolution in language. Vocabulary dropout is one simple instantiation of this principle.

### [13] [Evolutionary Search for Automated Design of Uncertainty Quantification Methods](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03473)
*Mikhail Seleznyov,Daniil Korbut,Viktor Moskvoretskii,Oleg Somov,Alexander Panchenko,Elena Tutubalina*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They use LLM-driven evolutionary search to automatically design Python-program-based uncertainty quantification methods for hallucination detection, beating hand-crafted baselines on claim verification benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing uncertainty quantification (UQ) methods for large language models are manually engineered using domain knowledge and heuristics. This manual design limits scalability, adaptability across tasks and models, and may miss high-performing or interpretable strategies. The authors want an automated, systematic way to discover strong UQ methods without heavy human hand-crafting.

Method: They represent UQ methods as small Python programs and use an LLM to power an evolutionary search over this program space. The LLM proposes, mutates, and recombines candidate UQ programs, which are evaluated on an atomic claim verification task. Fitness is based on ROC-AUC for distinguishing correct vs hallucinated outputs. They run this process across different LLMs as the evolutionary engine (e.g., Claude variants, Gpt-oss-120B) to see how search behaviors differ and to obtain high-performing unsupervised UQ methods.

Result: The automatically evolved UQ methods outperform strong manually designed baselines on atomic claim verification, with up to 6.7% relative improvement in ROC-AUC across 9 datasets and robust generalization to out-of-distribution settings. Analysis shows that Claude models tend to create high-feature-count linear estimators, whereas Gpt-oss-120B prefers simpler positional weighting schemes. Only Sonnet 4.5 and Opus 4.5 reliably translate increased method complexity into better performance, and Opus 4.6 unexpectedly performs worse than Opus 4.5 in this evolutionary design role.

Conclusion: LLM-powered evolutionary search can automatically discover effective and interpretable hallucination detectors, outperforming hand-crafted UQ baselines and revealing distinct inductive biases across different LLMs when used as search engines. This suggests a promising paradigm for automated design of uncertainty quantification methods for LLMs, potentially reducing reliance on manual heuristics while maintaining or improving performance and interpretability.

Abstract: Uncertainty quantification (UQ) methods for large language models are predominantly designed by hand based on domain knowledge and heuristics, limiting their scalability and generality. We apply LLM-powered evolutionary search to automatically discover unsupervised UQ methods represented as Python programs. On the task of atomic claim verification, our evolved methods outperform strong manually-designed baselines, achieving up to 6.7% relative ROC-AUC improvement across 9 datasets while generalizing robustly out-of-distribution. Qualitative analysis reveals that different LLMs employ qualitatively distinct evolutionary strategies: Claude models consistently design high-feature-count linear estimators, while Gpt-oss-120B gravitates toward simpler and more interpretable positional weighting schemes. Surprisingly, only Sonnet 4.5 and Opus 4.5 reliably leverage increased method complexity to improve performance -- Opus 4.6 shows an unexpected regression relative to its predecessor. Overall, our results indicate that LLM-powered evolutionary search is a promising paradigm for automated, interpretable hallucination detector design.

### [14] [Cultural Authenticity: Comparing LLM Cultural Representations to Native Human Expectations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03493)
*Erin MacMurray van Liemt,Aida Davani,Sinchana Kumbale,Neha Dixit,Sunipa Dev*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a human-centered framework to measure how well LLMs’ cultural outputs match how local people themselves prioritize aspects of their culture, rather than just checking diversity or factual correctness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of cultural representation in LLMs mainly look at whether different cultures appear (diversity) and whether the information is factually correct. This misses a key dimension: cultural alignment—whether the model’s emphasis and priorities actually reflect how people from that culture see their own society and values. The authors aim to fill this gap and provide a systematic way to measure whether LLMs reproduce Western-centric biases and mis-prioritize non-Western cultures’ values.

Method: 1) Collect open-ended survey responses in nine countries about what aspects of their culture matter most to them; induce a set of culturally significant facets and compute ‘Cultural Importance Vectors’ (CIVs) that encode the locally perceived importance of each facet. 2) Design a syntactically varied set of prompts to query LLMs (Gemini 2.5 Pro, GPT-4o, Claude 3.5 Haiku) about those cultures; from the generated content, derive ‘Cultural Representation Vectors’ (CRVs) that capture how strongly each model emphasizes each cultural facet. 3) Compare CIVs and CRVs across countries to quantify alignment, study how alignment changes with cultural distance from the US, and analyze systematic error patterns across models.

Result: The study finds that for some models, alignment between model-generated cultural representations and human-reported cultural priorities decreases as a country’s cultural distance from the US increases, indicating a Western-centric calibration. All three models show very similar systemic error patterns (correlation ρ > 0.97), consistently overemphasizing some surface-level cultural markers while underrepresenting deeper social and value-based priorities reported by local populations.

Conclusion: The proposed framework demonstrates that LLM evaluation must go beyond diversity counts and factual checks to examine how well models reproduce the nuanced priority structures that people within each culture report about themselves. The evidence of Western-centric drift and highly correlated error patterns across models suggests current LLMs share structural biases in representing non-Western cultures. The framework can serve as a basis for more authentic, human-grounded assessment and future debiasing of cultural representations in AI systems.

Abstract: Cultural representation in Large Language Model (LLM) outputs has primarily been evaluated through the proxies of cultural diversity and factual accuracy. However, a crucial gap remains in assessing cultural alignment: the degree to which generated content mirrors how native populations perceive and prioritize their own cultural facets. In this paper, we introduce a human-centered framework to evaluate the alignment of LLM generations with local expectations. First, we establish a human-derived ground-truth baseline of importance vectors, called Cultural Importance Vectors based on an induced set of culturally significant facets from open-ended survey responses collected across nine countries. Next, we introduce a method to compute model-derived Cultural Representation Vectors of an LLM based on a syntactically diversified prompt-set and apply it to three frontier LLMs (Gemini 2.5 Pro, GPT-4o, and Claude 3.5 Haiku). Our investigation of the alignment between the human-derived Cultural Importance and model-derived Cultural Representations reveals a Western-centric calibration for some of the models where alignment decreases as a country's cultural distance from the US increases. Furthermore, we identify highly correlated, systemic error signatures ($ρ> 0.97$) across all models, which over-index on some cultural markers while neglecting the deep-seated social and value-based priorities of users. Our approach moves beyond simple diversity metrics toward evaluating the fidelity of AI-generated content in authentically capturing the nuanced hierarchies of global cultures.

### [15] [LangFIR: Discovering Sparse Language-Specific Features from Monolingual Data for Language Steering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03532)
*Sing Hieng Wong,Hassan Sajjad,A. B. Siddique*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose LangFIR, a method that finds language-specific directions in LLM activations using sparse autoencoders and only monolingual plus random-token data, enabling more accurate multilingual steering without parallel corpora.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Controlling which language an LLM uses is still unreliable, even though LLMs are highly multilingual. Existing representation-steering methods need multilingual or parallel data to find language-specific directions in the residual stream, which is costly and not always available. Sparse autoencoders offer interpretable directions in activation space, but prior SAE-based methods inherit the same data requirements. The authors want a cheaper, scalable way to locate language-identity features without parallel data.

Method: They train or use sparse autoencoders on residual activations so that each SAE feature corresponds to an interpretable, sparse direction. For a target language, they inspect which SAE features are consistently activated by that language’s monolingual data. However many of these features are language-agnostic (e.g., syntax, formatting). To filter those out, they generate random-token sequences, which activate general features but not those encoding coherent language identity. LangFIR retains features that are strongly active for true language text but not for random tokens, yielding a small set of language-specific SAE features. These features are then combined into steering vectors that can be added to or subtracted from the residual stream to control output language during generation. They test this on several LLMs and languages.

Result: The discovered features are extremely sparse and highly selective for individual languages. Causal tests via directional ablation show that removing them selectively increases cross-entropy loss for the corresponding language, confirming their importance for representing language identity. When used as steering vectors for multilingual generation, LangFIR achieves the best average BLEU-based language control across Gemma 3 1B, Gemma 3 4B, and Llama 3.1 8B, over three datasets and twelve languages. It outperforms strong monolingual baselines and even approaches or surpasses methods that depend on parallel data.

Conclusion: Language identity in multilingual LLMs appears to be localized in a very sparse set of feature directions in the residual stream. These directions can be reliably discovered using only small monolingual datasets plus random-token filtering via SAEs, avoiding the need for parallel corpora. LangFIR thus offers a practical and effective approach for controllable multilingual generation and supports the broader view that sparse feature directions can capture high-level attributes like language identity.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) show strong multilingual capabilities, yet reliably controlling the language of their outputs remains difficult. Representation-level steering addresses this by adding language-specific vectors to model activations at inference time, but identifying language-specific directions in the residual stream often relies on multilingual or parallel data that can be expensive to obtain. Sparse autoencoders (SAEs) decompose residual activations into interpretable, sparse feature directions and offer a natural basis for this search, yet existing SAE-based approaches face the same data constraint. We introduce LangFIR (Language Feature Identification via Random-token Filtering), a method that discovers language-specific SAE features using only a small amount of monolingual data and random-token sequences. Many SAE features consistently activated by target-language inputs do not encode language identity. Random-token sequences surface these language-agnostic features, allowing LangFIR to filter them out and isolate a sparse set of language-specific features. We show that these features are extremely sparse, highly selective for their target language, and causally important: directional ablation increases cross-entropy loss only for the corresponding language. Using these features to construct steering vectors for multilingual generation control, LangFIR achieves the best average accuracy BLEU across three models (Gemma 3 1B, Gemma 3 4B, and Llama 3.1 8B), three datasets, and twelve target languages, outperforming the strongest monolingual baseline by up to and surpassing methods that rely on parallel data. Our results suggest that language identity in multilingual LLMs is localized in a sparse set of feature directions discoverable with monolingual data. Code is available at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/LangFIR-C0F5/.

</details>


### [16] [Rethinking Token Prediction: Tree-Structured Diffusion Language Model](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03537)
*Zihao Wu,Haoming Yang,Juncheng Dong,Vahid Tarokh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a tree-structured diffusion language model that replaces full-vocabulary token prediction with a hierarchical vocabulary tree, cutting memory use in half while keeping perplexity comparable to existing methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Discrete diffusion language models are promising competitors to auto-regressive LMs, but they are hard to train efficiently when parameters and GPU memory are limited. A major bottleneck is the full-vocabulary prediction head, which uses a large fraction of parameters and dominates peak memory usage, leading to poor parameter and memory efficiency under constrained resources.

Method: Replace the standard full-vocabulary classification layer with a tree-structured factorization of the vocabulary. Construct a vocabulary tree and model the diffusion process through intermediate latent states that correspond to ancestor nodes of each token in this tree. This hierarchical factorization drastically reduces the effective classification dimensionality and shrinks the prediction head, allowing the freed parameter and memory budget to be used to deepen attention blocks.

Result: Under a fixed total parameter budget, the proposed tree-structured diffusion language model cuts peak GPU memory consumption by about 50% compared to standard discrete diffusion LMs, while achieving comparable perplexity to state-of-the-art baselines.

Conclusion: Explicit full-vocabulary prediction layers are not necessary for competitive discrete diffusion language modeling. Exploiting token structure via a vocabulary tree yields a much smaller prediction head, substantially lower memory usage, and enables reallocating capacity to the backbone network without sacrificing perplexity performance.

Abstract: Discrete diffusion language models have emerged as a competitive alternative to auto-regressive language models, but training them efficiently under limited parameter and memory budgets remains challenging. Modern architectures are predominantly based on a full-vocabulary token prediction layer, which accounts for a substantial fraction of model parameters (e.g., more than 20% in small scale DiT-style designs) and often dominates peak GPU memory usage. This leads to inefficient use of both parameters and memory under constrained training resources. To address this issue, we revisit the necessity of explicit full-vocabulary prediction, and instead exploit the inherent structure among tokens to build a tree-structured diffusion language model. Specifically, we model the diffusion process with intermediate latent states corresponding to a token's ancestor nodes in a pre-constructed vocabulary tree. This tree-structured factorization exponentially reduces the classification dimensionality, makes the prediction head negligible in size, and enables reallocation of parameters to deepen the attention blocks. Empirically, under the same parameter budget, our method reduces peak GPU memory usage by half while matching the perplexity performance of state-of-the-art discrete diffusion language models.

Subsequently, we used a simple Multi-layer Perceptron architecture, which improved the results in our proposed model on our primary dataset, CNN/DM. Additionally, we annotated XSum dataset with RST graph information, establishing a benchmark for future graph-based summarization models. This secondary dataset posed multiple challenges, revealing both the merits and limitations of our models.

They further introduce a reward-driven iterative optimization mechanism that refines agents based on performance feedback. Existing methods often process modalities independently or employ simplistic fusion strategies, limiting their ability to capture complex cross-modal interactions and leverage external knowledge. To overcome these limitations, we propose MultiPress, a novel three-stage multi-agent framework for multimodal news classification. MultiPress integrates specialized agents for multimodal perception, retrieval-augmented reasoning, and gated fusion scoring, followed by a reward-driven iterative optimization mechanism. We validate MultiPress on a newly constructed large-scale multimodal news dataset, demonstrating significant improvements over strong baselines and highlighting the effectiveness of modular multi-agent collaboration and retrieval-augmented reasoning in enhancing classification accuracy and interpretability.

### [19] [Unveiling Language Routing Isolation in Multilingual MoE Models for Interpretable Subnetwork Adaptation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03592)
*Kening Zheng,Wei-Chieh Huang,Jiahao Huo,Zhonghao Li,Henry Peng Zou,Yibo Yan,Xin Zou,Jungang Li,Junzhuo Li,Hanrong Zhang,Xuming Hu,Philip S. Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how Mixture-of-Experts language models route different languages to experts, discovers that high- and low-resource languages use mostly disjoint experts (Language Routing Isolation), and introduces RISE, a method that selectively fine-tunes language-specific expert subnetworks to boost low-resource performance with little harm to other languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: MoE models perform unevenly across languages, with low-resource languages often lagging behind. Existing work improves MoEs but doesn’t clearly explain why such cross-lingual gaps emerge or how internal routing decisions differ among languages. The authors are motivated to (1) understand the routing behavior of MoE models across languages and layers, and (2) leverage this understanding to design an efficient adaptation method that helps low-resource languages without retraining or degrading performance in other languages.

Method: 1) Analyze routing logs from an MoE model across multiple languages and layers to quantify which experts are activated per language and how similar these expert distributions are between high- and low-resource languages. Identify the phenomenon of Language Routing Isolation and a layer-wise convergence–divergence pattern in routing.
2) Propose RISE, which builds a language-specific subnetwork by selecting experts with: (a) high language-specificity scores in shallow layers, (b) high overlap scores (shared across languages) in middle layers, and (c) high specificity again in deep layers. 3) Freeze all non-selected parameters and fine-tune only the chosen experts and related components for the target language. 4) Evaluate the approach on 10 languages using F1 as the main metric and compare against baselines.

Result: The analysis shows that high-resource and low-resource languages tend to activate largely non-overlapping experts, confirming Language Routing Isolation, and that routing similarity between languages varies systematically with depth (more similar in some middle layers, more divergent at shallow and deep layers). Using this insight, RISE is able to significantly improve performance on low-resource target languages—reporting up to 10.85% absolute F1 gain—while inducing only minimal performance loss, if any, on other languages.

Conclusion: Language-specific performance disparities in MoE models are closely tied to how experts are routed for different languages. The discovered Language Routing Isolation and layer-wise routing patterns explain why low-resource languages underperform and provide a handle for targeted adaptation. By exploiting these patterns, RISE can efficiently fine-tune a small, language-relevant subnetwork within a large MoE model to substantially improve low-resource language performance, all while largely preserving cross-lingual abilities. This suggests that routing-aware, subnetwork-level interventions are a promising direction for multilingual MoE optimization.

Abstract: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) models exhibit striking performance disparities across languages, yet the internal mechanisms driving these gaps remain poorly understood. In this work, we conduct a systematic analysis of expert routing patterns in MoE models, revealing a phenomenon we term Language Routing Isolation, in which high- and low-resource languages tend to activate largely disjoint expert sets. Through layer-stratified analysis, we further show that routing patterns exhibit a layer-wise convergence-divergence pattern across model depth. Building on these findings, we propose RISE (Routing Isolation-guided Subnetwork Enhancement), a framework that exploits routing isolation to identify and adapt language-specific expert subnetworks. RISE applies a tripartite selection strategy, using specificity scores to identify language-specific experts in shallow and deep layers and overlap scores to select universal experts in middle layers. By training only the selected subnetwork while freezing all other parameters, RISE substantially improves low-resource language performance while preserving capabilities in other languages. Experiments on 10 languages demonstrate that RISE achieves target-language F1 gains of up to 10.85% with minimal cross-lingual degradation.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Its strong empirical performance on multiple benchmarks validates the advantages of explicit temporal ordering and controllable modality fusion, positioning it as a promising architecture for dialogue-based multimodal sentiment analysis.

Abstract: Multimodal Sentiment Analysis (MSA) requires effective modeling of cross-modal interactions and contextual dependencies while remaining computationally efficient. Existing fusion approaches predominantly rely on Transformer-based cross-modal attention, which incurs quadratic complexity with respect to sequence length and limits scalability. Moreover, contextual information from preceding utterances is often incorporated through concatenation or independent fusion, without explicit temporal modeling that captures sentiment evolution across dialogue turns. To address these limitations, we propose CAGMamba, a context-aware gated cross-modal Mamba framework for dialogue-based sentiment analysis. Specifically, we organize the contextual and the current-utterance features into a temporally ordered binary sequence, which provides Mamba with explicit temporal structure for modeling sentiment evolution. To further enable controllable cross-modal integration, we propose a Gated Cross-Modal Mamba Network (GCMN) that integrates cross-modal and unimodal paths via learnable gating to balance information fusion and modality preservation, and is trained with a three-branch multi-task objective over text, audio, and fused predictions. All codes are available at https://github.com/User2024-xj/CAGMamba.

### [21] [Document-Level Numerical Reasoning across Single and Multiple Tables in Financial Reports](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03664)
*Yi-Cheng Wang,Wei-An Wang,Chu-Song Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces FinLongDocQA, a benchmark for numerical question answering over long financial reports, and FinLongDocAgent, a multi-agent RAG system that improves cross-table numerical reasoning via iterative retrieval and verification.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are good at language understanding but perform poorly on reliable QA over long, structured financial documents, especially when numerical reasoning and cross-table integration are required. Existing benchmarks mostly use single tables and short contexts, so they do not capture the real-world difficulties analysts face when working with entire annual reports that can exceed 100k tokens and require precise arithmetic over dispersed evidence.

Method: The authors construct FinLongDocQA, a dataset derived from long financial annual reports, containing both single-table and cross-table numerical reasoning questions. They systematically evaluate various closed-source and open-source LLMs on this benchmark, identifying performance bottlenecks related to long-context retrieval and multi-step arithmetic. To tackle these issues, they design FinLongDocAgent, a Multi-Agent Multi-Round RAG framework in which agents iteratively (1) retrieve potentially relevant tables and passages, (2) perform intermediate calculations, and (3) cross-check and verify tentative answers across multiple rounds, refining retrieval and reasoning at each step.

Result: Benchmarking shows that existing LLMs struggle significantly when reports are very long (often over 129k tokens), both in retrieving the right financial tables due to context rot and in executing accurate multi-step numerical reasoning even when given relevant evidence. The proposed FinLongDocAgent improves numerical QA performance on FinLongDocQA, demonstrating that iterative retrieval plus multi-round verification yields more accurate and reliable answers than one-shot prompting or standard RAG baselines.

Conclusion: Long-context financial QA requires more than just bigger context windows: it needs robust mechanisms to locate sparse, structured evidence and to carry out verifiable multi-step calculations. FinLongDocQA exposes these challenges in realistic annual-report settings, while FinLongDocAgent shows that a multi-agent, multi-round RAG strategy with iterative retrieval, intermediate computation, and answer verification can substantially enhance the reliability of numerical reasoning in long financial documents.

Abstract: Despite the strong language understanding abilities of large language models (LLMs), they still struggle with reliable question answering (QA) over long, structured documents, particularly for numerical reasoning. Financial annual reports exemplify this difficulty: financial statement analysis often hinges on accurate arithmetic, and analysts derive key indicators by integrating evidence scattered across multiple tables and narrative text. However, existing benchmarks focus largely on single-table settings, leaving cross-table document-level numerical reasoning underexplored. To address this gap, we introduce FinLongDocQA, a dataset for both single-table and cross-table financial numerical reasoning in long-context reports. Evaluating both closed-source and open-source LLMs on FinLongDocQA reveals two bottlenecks: (1) annual reports often exceed 129k tokens, exacerbating the context rot problem for locating relevant tables; and (2) even when relevant evidence is located, LLMs remain prone to errors in multi-step numerical reasoning. We propose FinLongDocAgent, a Multi-Agent Multi-Round Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) approach that iteratively retrieves evidence, performs intermediate calculations, and verifies results across rounds. Experiments highlight the importance of iterative retrieval and verification for reliable numerical QA in long financial documents.

</details>

The work also refines methodological assumptions in probing research and extends interpretability studies to Italian, contributing to multilingual perspectives on model interpretability.

Contextual vector representations are extracted from BERT and used as input to layer-wise probing classifiers, systematically evaluating information encoded across the model's internal layers. The results shed light on the extent to which constructional form and meaning are reflected in contextual embeddings, contributing empirical evidence to the dialogue between constructionist theory and neural language modelling

### [24] [Unlocking Prompt Infilling Capability for Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03677)
*Yoshinari Fujinuma,Keisuke Sakaguchi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that masked diffusion language models can be turned into strong prompt infillers by changing how they’re fine-tuned: mask both prompts and responses instead of only responses.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Masked diffusion LMs have a powerful bidirectional denoising ability, but standard supervised fine-tuning only lets them generate responses, not infill prompts. This underuses their architecture and blocks them from being used to automatically construct or optimize prompts.

Method: During supervised fine-tuning, instead of masking only the response segment, the authors apply full-sequence masking over both prompts and responses so the model learns to infill any masked span in a prompt template, conditioned on few-shot examples. They then use the model to infill masked parts of prompt templates and evaluate these infilled prompts on downstream tasks and across models, and compare them to manual and other optimized prompts.

Result: With full-sequence masking, the same masked diffusion LM can now infill prompts. The automatically infilled prompt templates perform on par with or better than manually engineered prompts, generalize across different models, and also work in combination with other prompt optimization techniques.

Conclusion: The main barrier to using masked diffusion language models as effective prompt infillers is the standard response-only masking practice in SFT, not the model architecture. Adjusting training to use full-sequence masking unlocks their bidirectional denoising ability for prompt infilling, yielding strong, transferable, and complementary prompt templates.

Abstract: Masked diffusion language models (dLMs) generate text through bidirectional denoising, yet this capability remains locked for infilling prompts. This limitation is an artifact of the current supervised finetuning (SFT) convention of applying response-only masking. To unlock this capability, we extend full-sequence masking during SFT, where both prompts and responses are masked jointly. Once unlocked, the model infills masked portions of a prompt template conditioned on few-shot examples. We show that such model-infilled prompts match or surpass manually designed templates, transfer effectively across models, and are complementary to existing prompt optimization methods. Our results suggest that training practices, not architectural limitations, are the primary bottleneck preventing masked diffusion language models from infilling effective prompts

### [25] [LightThinker++: From Reasoning Compression to Memory Management](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03679)
*Yuqi Zhu,Jintian Zhang,Zhenjie Wan,Yujie Luo,Shuofei Qiao,Zhengke Gui,Da Zheng,Lei Liang,Huajun Chen,Ningyu Zhang*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs perform well on complex reasoning but become inefficient when they must generate and keep track of long chains of thought, causing high token usage, latency, and context limits that hurt scalability in long-horizon or multi-round settings.

Method: LightThinker introduces dynamic compression of intermediate thoughts into compact semantic representations, reducing the length of stored reasoning traces. LightThinker++ extends this with Explicit Adaptive Memory Management: explicit memory primitives plus a trajectory synthesis pipeline that trains the model to schedule what to store, compress, or discard at the behavioral level, rather than using fixed, static compression.

Result: Experiments along three axes show: (1) LightThinker cuts peak token usage by about 70% and inference time by 26% with little accuracy loss; (2) LightThinker++ cuts peak tokens by 69.9% and even improves accuracy by 2.42% under the same context budget; (3) in long-horizon agentic tasks, the method maintains a stable memory footprint beyond 80 dialogue/interaction rounds with 60–70% fewer tokens and an average performance gain of 14.8% across complex scenarios.

Conclusion: Dynamic thought compression plus explicit adaptive memory management enables LLMs to sustain deep, long-horizon reasoning with much lower token and time overhead, offering a scalable path to more efficient and capable reasoning agents.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) excel at complex reasoning, yet their efficiency is limited by the surging cognitive overhead of long thought traces. In this paper, we propose LightThinker, a method that enables LLMs to dynamically compress intermediate thoughts into compact semantic representations. However, static compression often struggles with complex reasoning where the irreversible loss of intermediate details can lead to logical bottlenecks. To address this, we evolve the framework into LightThinker++, introducing Explicit Adaptive Memory Management. This paradigm shifts to behavioral-level management by incorporating explicit memory primitives, supported by a specialized trajectory synthesis pipeline to train purposeful memory scheduling. Extensive experiments demonstrate the framework's versatility across three dimensions. (1) LightThinker reduces peak token usage by 70% and inference time by 26% with minimal accuracy loss. (2) In standard reasoning, LightThinker++ slashes peak token usage by 69.9% while yielding a +2.42% accuracy gain under the same context budget for maximum performance. (3) Most notably, in long-horizon agentic tasks, it maintains a stable footprint beyond 80 rounds (a 60%-70% reduction), achieving an average performance gain of 14.8% across different complex scenarios. Overall, our work provides a scalable direction for sustaining deep LLM reasoning over extended horizons with minimal overhead.
