### [26] [Researchers waste 80% of LLM annotation costs by classifying one text at a time](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03684)
*Christian Pipal,Eva-Maria Vogel,Morgan Wack,Frank Esser*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether batching multiple texts and stacking multiple coding variables in a single LLM prompt can cut costs without hurting classification quality for social science text coding.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Social scientists increasingly use LLMs to code large text corpora, but they typically send one text–one variable per API call, which is extremely expensive in token usage and time. It is unclear whether more computationally efficient prompting strategies—batching many texts and combining several coding variables in one prompt—will introduce unacceptable measurement error. Researchers need evidence-based guidance on the tradeoff between cost and coding accuracy.

Method: The authors conduct a systematic benchmark using eight production LLMs from four providers. They use 3,962 tweets that have been previously coded by human experts on four different classification tasks. They vary (1) batch size from 1 up to 1,000 texts per prompt and (2) the number of coding variables (dimensions) stacked into a single prompt, up to 25. They then compare model accuracy under these configurations to a baseline setup where one text and one variable are coded per prompt.

Result: Six of the eight evaluated LLMs preserve classification accuracy within 2 percentage points of the single-item baseline for batch sizes up to 100 texts per prompt. Stacking multiple coding variables into the same prompt—up to about 10 dimensions—also yields accuracy comparable to the single-variable setup. When degradation occurs, it is mainly associated with increased task complexity rather than simply longer prompts. Overall, the additional error induced by batching and stacking remains smaller than the typical disagreement between human coders in the ground-truth labels, within a certain safe parameter range.

Conclusion: LLM-based text classification in the social sciences can be made far more efficient by batching many texts and stacking multiple coding variables into each prompt, substantially reducing API calls and token costs. For batch sizes up to roughly 100 items and about 10 coding dimensions, most modern LLMs maintain accuracy close to the standard one-text–one-variable baseline, and the extra measurement error is minor relative to human inter-coder disagreement. Thus, researchers can safely adopt these cost-saving prompting strategies within the tested ranges without meaningfully sacrificing measurement quality.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly being used for text classification across the social sciences, yet researchers overwhelmingly classify one text per variable per prompt. Coding 100,000 texts on four variables requires 400,000 API calls. Batching 25 items and stacking all variables into a single prompt reduces this to 4,000 calls, cutting token costs by over 80%. Whether this degrades coding quality is unknown. We tested eight production LLMs from four providers on 3,962 expert-coded tweets across four tasks, varying batch size from 1 to 1,000 items and stacking up to 25 coding dimensions per prompt. Six of eight models maintained accuracy within 2 pp of the single-item baseline through batch sizes of 100. Variable stacking with up to 10 dimensions produced results comparable to single-variable coding, with degradation driven by task complexity rather than prompt length. Within this safe operating range, the measurement error from batching and stacking is smaller than typical inter-coder disagreement in the ground-truth data.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs can generate poems, there is no systematic and fair way to compare them with human poets across both formal constraints and higher-level poetic qualities such as creativity, emotional impact, and stylistic uniqueness. The authors want to quantify this gap rigorously.

Method: They build POEMetric, an evaluation framework that covers: (1) basic instruction following (form and theme), (2) advanced poetic abilities (creativity, lexical diversity, idiosyncrasy, emotional resonance, imagery, literary devices), and (3) overall quality and authorship attribution. Humans also obtain higher overall poem quality scores (4.22 vs. 3.20 for the best LLM).

Conclusion: Despite impressive progress, LLMs are still far from human poets in deeper aspects of poetry such as originality, emotional depth, and nuanced stylistic expression, even when they can follow formal constraints well. Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) can compose poetry, but how far are they from human poets? In this paper, we introduce POEMetric, the first comprehensive framework for poetry evaluation, examining 1) basic instruction-following abilities in generating poems according to a certain form and theme, 2) advanced abilities of showing creativity, lexical diversity, and idiosyncrasy, evoking emotional resonance, and using imagery and literary devices, and 3) general appraisal of the overall poem quality and estimation of authorship. We curated a human poem dataset - 203 English poems of 7 fixed forms annotated with meter, rhyme patterns and themes - and experimented with 30 LLMs for poetry generation based on the same forms and themes of the human data, totaling 6,090 LLM poems. Based on POEMetric, we assessed the performance of both human poets and LLMs through rule-based evaluation and LLM-as-a-judge, whose results were validated by human experts. Results show that, though the top model achieved high form accuracy (4.26 out of 5.00, with Gemini-2.5-Pro as a judge; same below) and theme alignment (4.99), all models failed to reach the same level of advanced abilities as human poets, who achieved unparalleled creativity (4.02), idiosyncrasy (3.95), emotional resonance (4.06), and skillful use of imagery (4.49) and literary devices (4.67). Humans also defeated the best-performing LLM in overall poem quality (4.22 vs. 3.20). As such, poetry generation remains a formidable challenge for LLMs. Data and codes are released at https://github.com/Bingru-Li/POEMetric.

### [28] [Testing the Limits of Truth Directions in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03754)
*Angelos Poulis,Mark Crovella,Evimaria Terzi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper re-examines the supposed universal “truth direction” in LLM activations and shows it is much less universal than previously claimed.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work suggested that LLMs encode the truth of statements along a nearly universal linear direction in activation space, implying a simple, general mechanism for truth detection. However, follow-up work hinted that this universality breaks down in some settings, without a systematic understanding of when and why. This paper aims to clarify the conditions and limits under which such truth directions exist and generalize, which matters for interpretability, control, and safety uses of LLMs.

Method: The authors probe internal activations of LLMs across many layers and tasks to identify linear directions that separate true from false statements (“truth directions”). They then systematically vary: (1) layer index, (2) task type (factual vs reasoning), (3) task difficulty/complexity, and (4) instruction/prompt template. For each condition, they train and evaluate simple linear probes to measure whether the same direction generalizes across tasks and prompts, or whether distinct directions are needed.

Result: They find that: (1) Truth directions are strongly layer-dependent; a direction found at one layer often does not transfer well to others. (2) Factual tasks show usable truth directions in earlier layers, while reasoning tasks only show such directions in later layers. (3) Performance of a given truth direction degrades or changes as task complexity increases. (4) Changing model instructions or prompt templates (even simple correctness-evaluation prompts) substantially shifts the truth directions and their ability to generalize.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the previously claimed universality of truth directions in LLMs is overstated. Truth-related linear features exist but are contingent on layer, task type, task difficulty, and prompt format. Thus, any interpretability or safety method that assumes a single, global truth direction is unreliable; instead, probing must be context-aware, potentially layer- and task-specific, and sensitive to prompting choices.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have been shown to encode truth of statements in their activation space along a linear truth direction. Previous studies have argued that these directions are universal in certain aspects, while more recent work has questioned this conclusion drawing on limited generalization across some settings. In this work, we identify a number of limits of truth-direction universality that have not been previously understood. We first show that truth directions are highly layer-dependent, and that a full understanding of universality requires probing at many layers in the model. We then show that truth directions depend heavily on task type, emerging in earlier layers for factual and later layers for reasoning tasks; they also vary in performance across levels of task complexity. Finally, we show that model instructions dramatically affect truth directions; simple correctness evaluation instructions significantly affect the generalization ability of truth probes. Our findings indicate that universality claims for truth directions are more limited than previously known, with significant differences observable for various model layers, task difficulties, task types, and prompt templates.

</details>


### [29] [Your Agent is More Brittle Than You Think: Uncovering Indirect Injection Vulnerabilities in Agentic LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03870)
*Wenhui Zhu,Xuanzhao Dong,Xiwen Chen,Rui Cai,Peijie Qiu,Zhipeng Wang,Oana Frunza,Shao Tang,Jindong Gu,Yalin Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how vulnerable modern multi-agent LLM systems are to indirect prompt injection (IPI) attacks in realistic, dynamic tool-using settings and proposes a representation-engineering-based circuit breaker to detect and stop malicious actions before execution.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern multi-agent systems using open-source frameworks and tool-calling operate in complex, dynamic environments with large action spaces and many third-party content sources. This creates new security risks, especially from indirect prompt injections where malicious instructions are hidden in external content and can trigger unauthorized actions like data exfiltration. Existing security evaluations mostly use single-turn, isolated benchmarks and do not capture the full systemic vulnerability of these agents in real, multi-step scenarios. There is a need for a rigorous, multi-dimensional evaluation of IPI defenses in realistic environments and for more robust detection mechanisms that go beyond brittle, surface-level mitigations.

Method: The authors systematically evaluate six different defense strategies against four advanced IPI attack vectors across nine different LLM backbones. Crucially, they run all experiments within dynamic multi-step tool-calling environments to reflect real-world autonomous agent behavior. They measure not only binary attack success but multiple dimensions of behavior, including internal decision characteristics such as entropy of the model’s internal states. Observing high entropy when agents are about to perform malicious actions, they explore Representation Engineering (RepE) as a detection method: they extract hidden states at the tool-input position and train or design a RepE-based "circuit breaker" to flag and intercept suspicious or unauthorized actions before tool execution.

Result: The evaluation shows that sophisticated IPI attacks can bypass almost all baseline defenses in realistic tool-using environments, exposing strong fragility in current approaches. Some simple mitigations, likely things like filters or prompts, not only fail but introduce harmful side effects. Despite agents carrying out malicious instructions quickly, their internal representations show unusually high decision entropy around those actions, indicating latent uncertainty or conflict. Leveraging this, the RepE-based circuit breaker achieves high detection accuracy across diverse LLM models, successfully identifying and blocking unauthorized tool calls triggered by IPIs before they are executed.

Conclusion: Current defenses against indirect prompt injection are insufficient for modern multi-agent systems operating with tools in dynamic environments; these systems remain highly vulnerable when evaluated realistically rather than with simplified single-turn tests. Surface-level or ad-hoc mitigations can be misleading or even harmful. However, internal representation signals—specifically those accessible through Representation Engineering at the tool-input stage—provide a promising, model-agnostic way to detect and interrupt malicious behaviors early. The proposed RepE-based circuit breaker offers a practical paradigm for building more resilient multi-agent architectures and highlights the importance of leveraging internal state dynamics, not just external outputs, in security defenses.

Abstract: The rapid deployment of open-source frameworks has significantly advanced the development of modern multi-agent systems. However, expanded action spaces, including uncontrolled privilege exposure and hidden inter-system interactions, pose severe security challenges. Specifically, Indirect Prompt Injections (IPI), which conceal malicious instructions within third-party content, can trigger unauthorized actions such as data exfiltration during normal operations. While current security evaluations predominantly rely on isolated single-turn benchmarks, the systemic vulnerabilities of these agents within complex dynamic environments remain critically underexplored. To bridge this gap, we systematically evaluate six defense strategies against four sophisticated IPI attack vectors across nine LLM backbones. Crucially, we conduct our evaluation entirely within dynamic multi-step tool-calling environments to capture the true attack surface of modern autonomous agents. Moving beyond binary success rates, our multidimensional analysis reveals a pronounced fragility. Advanced injections successfully bypass nearly all baseline defenses, and some surface-level mitigations even produce counterproductive side effects. Furthermore, while agents execute malicious instructions almost instantaneously, their internal states exhibit abnormally high decision entropy. Motivated by this latent hesitation, we investigate Representation Engineering (RepE) as a robust detection strategy. By extracting hidden states at the tool-input position, we revealed that the RepE-based circuit breaker successfully identifies and intercepts unauthorized actions before the agent commits to them, achieving high detection accuracy across diverse LLM backbones. This study exposes the limitations of current IPI defenses and provides a highly practical paradigm for building resilient multi-agent architectures.

### [30] [When Models Know More Than They Say: Probing Analogical Reasoning in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03877)
*Hope McGovern,Caroline Craig,Thomas Lippincott,Hale Sirin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper examines how well large language models (LLMs) understand analogies in narratives by comparing what is encoded in their internal representations (via probing) to how they behave when prompted, and finds a mismatch that depends on analogy type.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Analogical reasoning is central to human narrative understanding, but current LLMs often fail when analogies are not obvious from surface cues and require deeper, latent knowledge. The authors want to understand whether this failure is due to missing knowledge in the model’s internal representations or due to how prompting accesses (or fails to access) that knowledge.

Method: The authors evaluate open-source LLMs on analogy detection tasks, distinguishing between rhetorical analogies (more surface/argumentative in nature) and narrative analogies (deeper story-structure parallels). They use two approaches: (1) prompting the model directly to detect analogies, measuring its behavioral performance; and (2) probing the model’s internal representations with diagnostic classifiers to see whether information about the analogical relations is encoded latently. They then compare probing performance with prompted performance across analogy types.

Result: For rhetorical analogies, probing the internal representations yields significantly better performance than direct prompting, indicating that the models internally encode relevant information that their prompted behavior fails to exploit. For narrative analogies, both probing and prompting perform similarly poorly, suggesting limited internal encoding or abstraction for this more complex type of analogy. Overall, the relationship between what is represented internally and what is accessible via prompting is asymmetric and depends on the task.

Conclusion: The study concludes that LLMs’ internal representations contain latent information about some kinds of analogies (especially rhetorical ones) that prompting does not reliably access, whereas for richer narrative analogies both representations and behavior are weak. This implies that (1) prompting alone is not a sufficient diagnostic of model competence, (2) representational capacity and behavioral performance can diverge in a task-dependent way, and (3) advancing narrative analogy understanding may require both better model architectures/training and improved methods for accessing latent knowledge beyond standard prompting.

Abstract: Analogical reasoning is a core cognitive faculty essential for narrative understanding. While LLMs perform well when surface and structural cues align, they struggle in cases where an analogy is not apparent on the surface but requires latent information, suggesting limitations in abstraction and generalisation. In this paper we compare a model's probed representations with its prompted performance at detecting narrative analogies, revealing an asymmetry: for rhetorical analogies, probing significantly outperforms prompting in open-source models, while for narrative analogies, they achieve a similar (low) performance. This suggests that the relationship between internal representations and prompted behavior is task-dependent and may reflect limitations in how prompting accesses available information.

### [31] [I-CALM: Incentivizing Confidence-Aware Abstention for LLM Hallucination Mitigation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03904)
*Haotian Zong,Binze Li,Yufei Long,Sinyin Chang,Jialong Wu,Gillian K. Hadfield*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a prompt-based framework (I-CALM) that reduces hallucinations in large language models by encouraging calibrated confidence reporting and rewarding abstention when uncertain, improving reliability without retraining the model.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often give confident but wrong answers because existing evaluation and reward schemes favor answering over admitting uncertainty. There is a lack of simple, deployment-ready methods that reduce hallucinations without retraining or architectural changes, particularly for factual QA where abstaining is preferable to incorrect answers.

Method: The authors first evaluate whether LLMs’ self-reported verbal confidence is stable under prompt variations and reasonably calibrated relative to token-probability-based uncertainty. They then introduce I-CALM, a purely prompt-based framework with three elements: (i) explicitly eliciting verbal confidence scores, (ii) specifying reward schemes that partially reward abstaining when uncertain, and (iii) adding short normative principles emphasizing truthfulness, humility, and responsibility. They test various prompt designs and abstention reward levels on factual QA benchmarks (mainly PopQA) using GPT-5 mini, examining how these affect answer accuracy, abstention rates, and confidence calibration.

Result: Confidence elicitation combined with explicit abstention rewards and normative guidance substantially reduces the rate of false answers among the questions that the model chooses to answer. Many instances that would previously have been answered incorrectly are now shifted into the abstain category, and the reported confidence on remaining answers becomes better calibrated. Forced-answer accuracy (when abstention is disallowed) remains largely unchanged, indicating that the gains come from better selective answering rather than improved raw knowledge. The authors also demonstrate a trade-off frontier between hallucination rate and abstention rate controlled by the abstention reward level, and they note that effect sizes differ across models and datasets.

Conclusion: Prompt-only interventions can meaningfully reduce hallucinations in LLMs on factual QA by leveraging self-reported confidence, explicit abstention incentives, and simple normative statements, without any model retraining. This improves reliability at the cost of some coverage and offers a practical knob (the abstention reward) for tuning the balance between answering and abstaining. The approach appears robust across prompt paraphrases and shows that models’ verbal confidence can serve as a useful, deployable uncertainty signal.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) frequently produce confident but incorrect answers, partly because common binary scoring conventions reward answering over honestly expressing uncertainty. We study whether prompt-only interventions -- explicitly announcing reward schemes for answer-versus-abstain decisions plus humility-oriented normative principles -- can reduce hallucination risk without modifying the model. Our focus is epistemic abstention on factual questions with a verifiable answer, where current LLMs often fail to abstain despite being uncertain about their answers. We first assess self-reported verbal confidence as a usable uncertainty signal, showing stability under prompt paraphrasing and reasonable calibration against a token-probability baseline. We then study I-CALM, a prompt-based framework that (i) elicits verbal confidence, (ii) partially rewards abstention through explicit reward schemes, and (iii) adds lightweight normative principles emphasizing truthfulness, humility, and responsibility. Using GPT-5 mini on PopQA as the main setting, we find that confidence-eliciting, abstention-rewarding prompts, especially with norms, reduce the false-answer rate on answered cases mainly by identifying and shifting error-prone cases to abstention and re-calibrating their confidence. This trades coverage for reliability while leaving forced-answer performance largely unchanged. Varying the abstention reward yields a clear abstention-hallucination frontier. Overall, results show the framework can improve selective answering on factual questions without retraining, with the magnitude of effect varying across models and datasets. Code is available at the following https://github.com/binzeli/hallucinationControl.

</details>


### [32] [From Plausible to Causal: Counterfactual Semantics for Policy Evaluation in Simulated Online Communities](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03920)
*Agam Goyal,Yian Wang,Eshwar Chandrasekharan,Hari Sundaram*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that to make LLM-based social simulations genuinely useful for policy testing, we must interpret their results within a formal causal counterfactual framework, distinguishing necessary from sufficient causation and tying each to different stakeholder needs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM-based social simulations can produce realistic-looking community interactions and are proposed as "policy wind tunnels" for testing governance interventions. However, they lack a precise causal interpretation: observing that an intervention appears to reduce escalation in simulation does not ensure that the intervention truly causes this reduction, nor clarify in what sense. This gap between believability and causality risks misleading policy decisions. The authors want to provide a principled way to interpret and design simulations so their outputs correspond to meaningful causal claims aligned with stakeholder needs.

Method: Conceptual and formal: they adopt the causal counterfactual framework and explicitly distinguish between necessary causation (whether the outcome would occur without the intervention) and sufficient causation (whether the intervention reliably produces the outcome). They then map these two notions onto different stakeholder questions (incident diagnosis vs policy choice), and formalize how simulation designs can be constructed to estimate these causal quantities under stated assumptions. They further propose interpreting the outputs as simulator-conditional causal estimates whose value depends on the fidelity of the simulator to the real world.

Result: They provide a formal mapping between stakeholder needs (moderators vs platform designers) and types of causal questions (necessary vs sufficient causation), and show—at least in principle—how LLM-based social simulations can be structured to estimate these quantities. They clarify that these estimates are conditional on the simulator’s behavior and highlight what assumptions and forms of fidelity are required for such estimates to have policy relevance.

Conclusion: A causal counterfactual framework is crucial for using LLM-based social simulations responsibly in governance and policy testing. By distinguishing necessary and sufficient causation and aligning them with concrete stakeholder needs, we can design simulations that yield interpretable, simulator-conditional causal estimates rather than just realistic narratives. This framework helps specify what "fidelity" must mean for simulations to usefully inform policy and marks a step toward simulations that can legitimately support policy changes.

Abstract: LLM-based social simulations can generate believable community interactions, enabling ``policy wind tunnels'' where governance interventions are tested before deployment. But believability is not causality. Claims like ``intervention $A$ reduces escalation'' require causal semantics that current simulation work typically does not specify. We propose adopting the causal counterfactual framework, distinguishing \textit{necessary causation} (would the outcome have occurred without the intervention?) from \textit{sufficient causation} (does the intervention reliably produce the outcome?). This distinction maps onto different stakeholder needs: moderators diagnosing incidents require evidence about necessity, while platform designers choosing policies require evidence about sufficiency. We formalize this mapping, show how simulation design can support estimation under explicit assumptions, and argue that the resulting quantities should be interpreted as simulator-conditional causal estimates whose policy relevance depends on simulator fidelity. Establishing this framework now is essential: it helps define what adequate fidelity means and moves the field from simulations that look realistic toward simulations that can support policy changes.

### [33] [Uncertainty as a Planning Signal: Multi-Turn Decision Making for Goal-Oriented Conversation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03924)
*Xinyi Ling,Ye Liu,Reza Averly,Xia Ning*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce an uncertainty-aware planning framework (CUP) for goal-oriented dialogue that uses LLMs for candidate actions and a planner to choose actions that optimally reduce uncertainty, improving task success with fewer turns.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Goal-oriented dialogue systems must decide when to ask for more information versus when to commit to a target, under uncertainty about user intent. Existing structured methods can plan but depend on rigid, predefined schemas, while LLM-based systems provide flexible language interaction but struggle with long-horizon planning and thus coordinate poorly between information gathering and decision making. There is a need for a method that combines LLM flexibility with explicit, uncertainty-aware planning over multiple turns.

Method: They formulate goal-oriented conversation as an uncertainty-aware sequential decision-making problem, where the system explicitly reasons about and tracks uncertainty over user intent across turns. They propose the CUP framework, in which (1) a language model generates a set of feasible next actions (e.g., ask clarifying questions, propose solutions, confirm hypotheses), and (2) a separate planner evaluates these actions in terms of their long-term impact on reducing uncertainty and reaching a confident target decision. The planner then selects the action that yields the best expected uncertainty reduction over multiple turns, effectively integrating structured planning with LLM-based action generation.

Result: On several goal-oriented conversational benchmarks, CUP achieves higher task success rates than baselines and does so using fewer dialogue turns, indicating more efficient conversations. Empirical analyses show that CUP enables the system to gather necessary information more strategically and to commit to a target solution earlier and more confidently compared to approaches without uncertainty-aware planning.

Conclusion: By explicitly modeling conversation as an uncertainty-aware sequential decision process and combining LLM-generated actions with structured long-horizon planning, CUP improves the coordination between information acquisition and target commitment in goal-oriented dialogue. This leads to more efficient and successful interactions, suggesting that uncertainty-aware planning is a key ingredient for effective multi-turn conversational agents.

Abstract: Goal-oriented conversational systems require making sequential decisions under uncertainty about the user's intent, where the algorithm must balance information acquisition and target commitment over multiple turns. Existing approaches address this challenge from different perspectives: structured methods enable multi-step planning but rely on predefined schemas, while LLM-based approaches support flexible interactions but lack long-horizon decision making, resulting in poor coordination between information acquisition and target commitment. To address this limitation, we formulate goal-oriented conversation as an uncertainty-aware sequential decision problem, where uncertainty serves as a guiding signal for multi-turn decision making. We propose a Conversation Uncertainty-aware Planning framework (CUP) that integrates language models with structured planning: a language model proposes feasible actions, and a planner evaluates their long-term impact on uncertainty reduction. Experiments on multiple conversational benchmarks show that CUP consistently improves success rates while requiring fewer interaction turns. Further analysis demonstrates that uncertainty-aware planning contributes to more efficient information acquisition and earlier confident commitment.

### [34] [AdaptFuse: Training-Free Sequential Preference Learning via Externalized Bayesian Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03925)
*Fangzhou Lin,Peiran Li,Shuo Xing,Siyuan Yang,Qianwen Ge,Kazunori Yamada,Ziming Zhang,Haichong Zhang,Zhengzhong Tu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: AdaptFuse is a training-free framework that lets an external Bayesian module handle probabilistic belief updating while a frozen LLM provides semantic reasoning, combining them adaptively to improve personalized recommendation without using sensitive training data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are poor at doing Bayesian-style belief updates over multiple user interactions, and existing fixes rely on fine-tuning with sensitive user data, which is problematic for privacy and deployment. The paper aims to enable principled Bayesian updating for recommendation tasks without any fine-tuning or storage of personal interaction logs.

Method: The authors introduce AdaptFuse, which separates probabilistic inference from the LLM. A symbolic component maintains a Bayesian posterior over a discrete set of hypotheses. The LLM, kept frozen, produces semantic judgments that are turned into a Dirichlet distribution over hypotheses via multi-sample aggregation. An entropy-adaptive fusion mechanism then combines the symbolic posterior and the LLM-derived distribution, weighting each by its confidence (entropy). Over rounds of interaction, the system increasingly relies on the symbolic posterior as evidence accumulates. This framework is evaluated on several recommendation tasks (flights, hotels, web shopping) using different base LLMs (Gemma 2 9B, Llama 3 8B, Qwen 2.5 7B).

Result: Across all three domains and all tested base models, AdaptFuse achieves higher accuracy than prompting-only methods and than fine-tuned Bayesian Teaching models, and its accuracy increases monotonically with more interaction rounds. This shows that careful inference-time algorithms can match or surpass fine-tuned systems in sequential recommendation tasks.

Conclusion: AdaptFuse demonstrates that externalizing Bayesian reasoning to a symbolic module and fusing it with a frozen LLM via entropy-based weighting can yield strong, progressively improving performance on interactive recommendation, eliminating the need to fine-tune on or store sensitive user interaction data. This supports the broader claim that inference-time, training-free methods can effectively replace fine-tuning for certain personalization use cases.

Abstract: Large language models struggle to accumulate evidence across multiple rounds of user interaction, failing to update their beliefs in a manner consistent with Bayesian inference. Existing solutions require fine-tuning on sensitive user interaction data, limiting their applicability in privacy-conscious settings. We propose AdaptFuse, a training-free framework that externalizes probabilistic computation entirely from the LLM: a symbolic module maintains a Bayesian posterior over a discrete hypothesis set, while a frozen LLM contributes semantic reasoning via multi-sample Dirichlet aggregation. The two signals are combined through entropy-adaptive fusion, which automatically weights each source by its predictive confidence, shifting reliance from the LLM to the symbolic posterior as evidence accumulates. We evaluate across three domains: flight recommendation, hotel recommendation, and web shopping; on Gemma 2 9B, Llama 3 8B, and Qwen 2.5 7B. AdaptFuse consistently outperforms both prompting baselines and fine-tuned Bayesian Teaching models on all tasks, with accuracy improving monotonically over interaction rounds. These results demonstrate that principled inference-time algorithms can substitute for fine-tuning in personalized recommendation, without storing or training on sensitive user data. All the code and materials will be open-sourced.

### [35] [Predict, Don't React: Value-Based Safety Forecasting for LLM Streaming](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03962)
*Pride Kavumba,Koki Wataoka,Huy H. Nguyen,Jiaxuan Li,Masaya Ohagi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: StreamGuard reframes LLM safety for streaming outputs as forecasting future harmfulness rather than detecting a fixed unsafe boundary, enabling earlier, more reliable interventions without token-level labels.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Real-world LLM systems often use one guardrail for both input and streaming output moderation, but current output safeguards treat moderation as boundary detection: they wait until the response has already turned unsafe and then detect the earliest unsafe prefix. This requires fine-grained boundary annotations, is brittle for partial generations, and limits how early an intervention can occur. There is a need for a unified, model-agnostic guardrail that can work for both prompts and streaming responses, intervene earlier, and avoid dependence on exact token-level unsafe boundaries.

Method: The authors propose StreamGuard, which treats streaming moderation as a forecasting problem: given a partial output prefix, the model predicts the expected harmfulness of probable future continuations. Training supervision comes from Monte Carlo rollouts of continuations from many prefixes, estimating the distribution of future harmfulness instead of labeling exact unsafe boundaries. The same model is used for both input moderation and output streaming moderation, and it is designed to be model- and tokenizer-agnostic so it can transfer across LLM families. They compare against a strong baseline (Qwen3Guard-Stream-8B-strict) on standard safety benchmarks and a specialized streaming benchmark (QWENGUARDTEST response_loc).

Result: At 8B scale, StreamGuard improves input-moderation F1 from 86.7 to 88.2 and streaming output-moderation F1 from 80.4 to 81.9 over Qwen3Guard-Stream-8B-strict. On the QWENGUARDTEST response_loc streaming benchmark, StreamGuard achieves 97.5 F1, 95.1 recall, and 92.6% on-time intervention, surpassing the baseline’s 95.9 F1, 92.1 recall, and 89.9% on-time intervention, while cutting the miss rate from 7.9% to 4.9%. Using forecasting-based supervision transferred across tokenizers and model families, a smaller Gemma3-StreamGuard-1B model still attains strong performance: 81.3 response-moderation F1, 98.2 streaming F1, and a 3.5% miss rate.

Conclusion: Forecasting the future harmfulness of likely continuations from partial prefixes is an effective way to supervise streaming safety guardrails. StreamGuard delivers stronger, earlier, and more reliable safety interventions than boundary-detection approaches, without requiring exact unsafe-boundary labels. Its supervision scheme transfers across tokenizers and model families, demonstrating that robust, low-latency, end-to-end streaming moderation can be achieved in a scalable and model-agnostic manner.

Abstract: In many practical LLM deployments, a single guardrail is used for both prompt and response moderation. Prompt moderation operates on fully observed text, whereas streaming response moderation requires safety decisions to be made over partial generations. Existing text-based streaming guardrails commonly frame this output-side problem as boundary detection, training models to identify the earliest prefix at which a response has already become unsafe. In this work, we introduce StreamGuard, a unified model-agnostic streaming guardrail that instead formulates moderation as a forecasting problem: given a partial prefix, the model predicts the expected harmfulness of likely future continuations. We supervise this prediction using Monte Carlo rollouts, which enables early intervention without requiring exact token-level boundary annotations.
  Across standard safety benchmarks, StreamGuard performs strongly both for input moderation and for streaming output moderation. At the 8B scale, StreamGuard improves aggregated input-moderation F1 from 86.7 to 88.2 and aggregated streaming output-moderation F1 from 80.4 to 81.9 relative to Qwen3Guard-Stream-8B-strict. On the QWENGUARDTEST response_loc streaming benchmark, StreamGuard reaches 97.5 F1, 95.1 recall, and 92.6% on-time intervention, compared to 95.9 F1, 92.1 recall, and 89.9% for Qwen3Guard-Stream-8B-stric, while reducing the miss rate from 7.9% to 4.9%. We further show that forecasting-based supervision transfers effectively across tokenizers and model families: with transferred targets, Gemma3-StreamGuard-1B reaches 81.3 response-moderation F1, 98.2 streaming F1, and a 3.5% miss rate. These results show that strong end-to-end streaming moderation can be obtained without exact boundary labels, and that forecasting future risk is an effective supervision strategy for low-latency safety intervention.

### [36] [RUQuant: Towards Refining Uniform Quantization for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04013)
*Han Liu,Haotian Gao,Changya Li,Feng Zhang,Xiaotong Zhang,Wei Wang,Hong Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: RUQuant is a post-training quantization method for LLMs that applies carefully designed orthogonal transformations to make activations more uniformly distributed, enabling highly accurate low-bit quantization without expensive fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Deployment of large LLMs is constrained by memory and computation. PTQ is attractive because it avoids retraining, but existing uniform quantization of activations causes notable accuracy loss due to the inherently non-uniform activation distributions. There is a need for a theoretically grounded way to reshape activations so uniform quantizers become near-optimal, preserving accuracy even at low bit-widths and under tight resource and time budgets.

Method: The paper analyzes activation quantization using Lloyd-Max optimality, showing that non-uniform activation distributions shift optimal quantization points away from interval midpoints, making standard uniform quantization suboptimal. To fix this, they introduce RUQuant, a two-stage orthogonal-transformation-based PTQ method. Stage 1: split activations into blocks and map each block to uniformly sampled target vectors via composite orthogonal matrices built from Householder reflections and Givens rotations, effectively “uniformizing” the distribution. Stage 2: learn a global Householder reflection using discrepancies in Transformer outputs to further reduce quantization error. The method operates post-training and is lightweight in computation time.

Result: On a 13B-parameter LLM, RUQuant with 6-bit weights and activations (W6A6) achieves 99.8% of full-precision accuracy and with 4-bit weights and activations (W4A4) achieves 97% of full-precision accuracy, all with roughly one minute of processing and no model fine-tuning. A variant that includes fine-tuning further improves accuracy beyond the already strong PTQ performance, indicating that the approach scales well and effectively preserves model quality at low precision.

Conclusion: Non-uniform activation distributions are a key barrier to accurate uniform PTQ in LLMs. By using theoretically motivated orthogonal transformations to make activations more uniform prior to quantization, RUQuant enables near-optimal uniform quantization at low bit-widths without substantial retraining overhead. The method offers a practical, scalable route to highly efficient LLM deployment, and can be further enhanced with light fine-tuning when additional accuracy is required.

Abstract: The increasing size and complexity of large language models (LLMs) have raised significant challenges in deployment efficiency, particularly under resource constraints. Post-training quantization (PTQ) has emerged as a practical solution by compressing models without requiring retraining. While existing methods focus on uniform quantization schemes for both weights and activations, they often suffer from substantial accuracy degradation due to the non-uniform nature of activation distributions. In this work, we revisit the activation quantization problem from a theoretical perspective grounded in the Lloyd-Max optimality conditions. We identify the core issue as the non-uniform distribution of activations within the quantization interval, which causes the optimal quantization point under the Lloyd-Max criterion to shift away from the midpoint of the interval. To address this issue, we propose a two-stage orthogonal transformation method, RUQuant. In the first stage, activations are divided into blocks. Each block is mapped to uniformly sampled target vectors using composite orthogonal matrices, which are constructed from Householder reflections and Givens rotations. In the second stage, a global Householder reflection is fine-tuned to further minimize quantization error using Transformer output discrepancies. Empirical results show that our method achieves near-optimal quantization performance without requiring model fine-tuning: RUQuant achieves 99.8% of full-precision accuracy with W6A6 and 97% with W4A4 quantization for a 13B LLM, within approximately one minute. A fine-tuned variant yields even higher accuracy, demonstrating the effectiveness and scalability of our approach.

They also provide expert-annotated, stepwise reasoning traces grounded in verifiable web evidence, enabling trajectory-level evaluation of how agents use tools and gather evidence.

Ablations indicate that setups without tools, with search only, or with image-only reasoning underperform. Performance gains are primarily attributed to coherent, level-aware planning of tool use rather than simply increasing the number of tool calls, as GATE more consistently reaches key evidence steps and reduces integration errors when forming final decisions.

Conclusion: GeoBrowse, together with the GATE workflow and expert evidence-grounded traces, provides a rigorous benchmark for multimodal deep research agents that must integrate visual reasoning and knowledge-intensive multi-hop search. The findings highlight that effective performance in such tasks depends on well-structured, level-specific tool-use strategies that reliably surface critical evidence, rather than on raw tool-call volume, and demonstrate that previous text-only or unimodal setups are insufficient. The benchmark and codebase are released to encourage further research on robust multimodal research agents.

Abstract: Deep research agents integrate fragmented evidence through multi-step tool use. BrowseComp offers a text-only testbed for such agents, but existing multimodal benchmarks rarely require both weak visual cues composition and BrowseComp-style multi-hop verification. Geolocation is a natural testbed because answers depend on combining multiple ambiguous visual cues and validating them with open-web evidence. Thus, we introduce GeoBrowse, a geolocation benchmark that combines visual reasoning with knowledge-intensive multi-hop queries. Level 1 tests extracting and composing fragmented visual cues, and Level 2 increases query difficulty by injecting long-tail knowledge and obfuscating key entities. To support evaluation, we provide an agentic workflow GATE with five think-with-image tools and four knowledge-intensive tools, and release expert-annotated stepwise traces grounded in verifiable evidence for trajectory-level analysis. Experiments show that GATE outperforms direct inference and open-source agents, indicating that no-tool, search-only or image-only setups are insufficient. Gains come from coherent, level-specific tool-use plans rather than more tool calls, as they more reliably reach annotated key evidence steps and make fewer errors when integrating into the final decision.   <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often generate hallucinations—confident but incorrect or unsupported statements—which are dangerous in sensitive applications like medicine or law. Existing mitigation methods (e.g., RAG, simple prompting) only partially address the issue and lack fine-grained understanding of how internal attention patterns lead to hallucinations. The paper is motivated by the need to both interpret and systematically reduce hallucinations by analyzing and controlling the causal influence of tokens inside the model.

Method: The authors propose a Causal Graph Attention Network (GCAN) that builds token-level graphs in a transformer using self-attention weights and gradient-based influence scores. They define a Causal Contribution Score (CCS) that quantifies how much each token causally contributes to a factually grounded output. On top of this, they add a fact-anchored graph reweighting layer that down-weights hallucination-prone nodes and up-weights fact-supporting ones during generation. The framework is evaluated on QA benchmarks against standard RAG baselines.

Result: On TruthfulQA and HotpotQA benchmarks, the GCAN framework achieves a 27.8% reduction in hallucination rate and a 16.4% increase in factual accuracy compared with baseline RAG models, indicating more reliable and trustworthy generations.

Conclusion: By explicitly modeling causal dependencies between tokens and reweighting attention to suppress unreliable nodes, GCAN improves both interpretability and factual robustness of LLMs. The approach shows that integrating causal analysis into transformer attention can significantly reduce hallucinations and may inform the design of future safer and more reliable LLM architectures.

Abstract: This paper primarily focuses on the hallucinations caused due to AI language models(LLMs).LLMs have shown extraordinary Language understanding and generation capabilities .Still it has major a disadvantage hallucinations which give outputs which are factually incorrect ,misleading or unsupported by input data . These hallucinations cause serious problems in scenarios like medical diagnosis or legal reasoning.Through this work,we propose causal graph attention network (GCAN) framework that reduces hallucinations through interpretation of internal attention flow within a transformer architecture with the help of constructing token level graphs that combine self attention weights and gradient based influence scores.our method quantifies each tokens factual dependency using a new metric called the Causal Contribution Score (CCS). We further introduce a fact-anchored graph reweighting layer that dynamically reduces the influence of hallucination prone nodes during generation. Experiments on standard benchmarks such as TruthfulQA and HotpotQA show a 27.8 percent reduction in hallucination rate and 16.4 percent improvement in factual accuracy over baseline retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) models. ### [39] [Emergent Inference-Time Semantic Contamination via In-Context Priming](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04043)
*Marcin Abram*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how few-shot prompting with culturally loaded or nonsense examples can cause large language models to drift toward harmful or off-distribution behavior, even without fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work showed that fine-tuning LLMs on insecure or culturally loaded code can induce misalignment, but concluded that k-shot prompting alone does not. As LLMs are widely used with few-shot prompts in security- and safety-critical applications, it is important to re-evaluate whether prompting itself can contaminate model behavior at inference time and under what conditions.

Method: The authors design controlled prompting experiments where a small set of demonstrations (few-shot examples) are prepended to an otherwise benign, semantically unrelated prompt. They use two types of demonstrations: (1) culturally loaded numeric codes known to correlate with harmful or extremist themes, and (2) structurally similar but semantically inert nonsense strings. They test multiple models of differing size/capability and measure how the output distribution shifts along dimensions such as darkness of themes, authoritarian content, and stigmatization, comparing to baselines without such injections.

Result: They find that sufficiently capable models exhibit clear distributional shifts in their completions after exposure to culturally loaded numbers, with outputs skewing toward darker, authoritarian, and stigmatized topics, even though the downstream prompt is unrelated. A smaller/simpler model does not show this effect. They also observe that even nonsense-string demonstrations, which lack semantic content, still perturb output distributions, indicating that mere structural/format cues can contaminate generations.

Conclusion: Inference-time semantic drift from few-shot prompting is real but depends on model capability. There appear to be at least two mechanisms of contamination: one driven by the semantic associations of the demonstrations, and another driven by their structural/format properties. These findings define boundary conditions for when prompting can misalign model outputs and highlight security and safety risks for LLM-based systems that rely on few-shot examples, motivating more robust prompt design and mitigation strategies.

Abstract: Recent work has shown that fine-tuning large language models (LLMs) on insecure code or culturally loaded numeric codes can induce emergent misalignment, causing models to produce harmful content in unrelated downstream tasks. The authors of that work concluded that $k$-shot prompting alone does not induce this effect. We revisit this conclusion and show that inference-time semantic drift is real and measurable; however, it requires models of large-enough capability. Using a controlled experiment in which five culturally loaded numbers are injected as few-shot demonstrations before a semantically unrelated prompt, we find that models with richer cultural-associative representations exhibit significant distributional shifts toward darker, authoritarian, and stigmatized themes, while a simpler/smaller model does not. We additionally find that structurally inert demonstrations (nonsense strings) perturb output distributions, suggesting two separable mechanisms: structural format contamination and semantic content contamination. Our results map the boundary conditions under which inference-time contamination occurs, and carry direct implications for the security of LLM-based applications that use few-shot prompting.

### [40] [Extracting and Steering Emotion Representations in Small Language Models: A Methodological Comparison](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04064)
*Jihoon Jeong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies whether small language models (100M–10B parameters) have usable internal representations of emotions, and how to best extract and steer them.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While large frontier models are known to encode rich emotion representations, many real-world systems rely on much smaller models, for which this property is less understood. The authors want to know if small models also possess structured emotion representations, how these are distributed across layers, and how to reliably extract and causally manipulate them for safety and control.

Method: They evaluate nine open-weight small language models from five popular architectures (GPT‑2, Gemma, Qwen, Llama, Mistral). For 20 emotions, they construct emotion ‘vectors’ using two extraction paradigms: generation-based (using model generations) and comprehension-based (using hidden states from understanding tasks). They compare separation quality statistically, analyze where in the transformer stack emotion information localizes, control for representational anisotropy, and run steering experiments where they add emotion vectors to internal states and measure behavioral changes, perplexity, and cross-lingual effects. An external emotion classifier is used to independently validate the steering outcomes.

Result: Generation-based extraction yields significantly better-separated emotion vectors than comprehension-based methods, with statistical evidence (Mann–Whitney p=0.007, large effect size) and sensitivity to architecture and instruction tuning. Emotion features concentrate around middle layers (~50% depth) in a U-shaped pattern that appears consistent from 124M to 3B parameters. Steering along these vectors reliably changes model behavior, confirmed by an external classifier that detects the intended emotion in 92% of test scenarios. Steering behavior falls into three regimes—surgical, repetitive collapse, and explosive text degradation—distinguished more by architecture than scale. In Qwen, emotion steering unintentionally activates aligned Chinese tokens, and these cross-lingual emotional activations are not fully damped by RLHF, revealing a safety risk for multilingual use.

Conclusion: Small language models do have structured, causally meaningful emotion representations, especially in middle layers, but the quality and control depend strongly on extraction method and architecture. Generation-based extraction should be preferred for emotion studies. Emotion steering can be effective yet exhibits distinct behavioral regimes that must be understood for safe deployment. Cross-lingual entanglement, as seen in Qwen, highlights that manipulating internal emotion directions can surface unintended multilingual content, posing safety concerns. The paper offers practical guidelines for emotion-vector research and connects internal representation analysis with external behavioral profiling in the context of model ‘medicine.’

Abstract: Small language models (SLMs) in the 100M-10B parameter range increasingly power production systems, yet whether they possess the internal emotion representations recently discovered in frontier models remains unknown. We present the first comparative analysis of emotion vector extraction methods for SLMs, evaluating 9 models across 5 architectural families (GPT-2, Gemma, Qwen, Llama, Mistral) using 20 emotions and two extraction methods (generation-based and comprehension-based). Generation-based extraction produces statistically superior emotion separation (Mann-Whitney p = 0.007; Cohen's d = -107.5), with the advantage modulated by instruction tuning and architecture. Emotion representations localize at middle transformer layers (~50% depth), following a U-shaped curve that is architecture-invariant from 124M to 3B parameters. We validate these findings against representational anisotropy baselines across 4 models and confirm causal behavioral effects through steering experiments, independently verified by an external emotion classifier (92% success rate, 37/40 scenarios). Steering reveals three regimes -- surgical (coherent text transformation), repetitive collapse, and explosive (text degradation) -- quantified by perplexity ratios and separated by model architecture rather than scale. We document cross-lingual emotion entanglement in Qwen, where steering activates semantically aligned Chinese tokens that RLHF does not suppress, raising safety concerns for multilingual deployment. This work provides methodological guidelines for emotion research on open-weight models and contributes to the Model Medicine series by bridging external behavioral profiling with internal representational analysis.

### [41] [Embedding Enhancement via Fine-Tuned Language Models for Learner-Item Cognitive Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04088)
*Yuanhao Liu,Zihan Zhou,Kaiying Wu,Shuo Liu,Yiyang Huang,Jiajun Guo,Aimin Zhou,Hong Qian*

Motivation: Existing cognitive diagnosis models mostly rely on ID embeddings, which ignore rich semantic information available in educational texts. While language models can provide such semantics, their training objectives are misaligned with CD models, and there is no unified way to integrate textual embeddings across different CD tasks without breaking successful existing paradigms. This limits how much benefit LMs can bring to learner-item cognitive modeling.

Method: The authors design EduEmbed, a two-stage embedding enhancement framework. Stage 1 fine-tunes language models with role-specific representations (e.g., learner, item, interaction roles) and an interaction diagnoser to reduce the semantic and distribution gap between LM features and CD model needs. Stage 2 uses a textual adapter to select task-relevant semantics from the fine-tuned LM and fuse them with existing cognitive modeling paradigms (ID embeddings and other CD structures), so that the enhanced embeddings can be plugged into different CD tasks while preserving their original modeling structure.

Result: EduEmbed is evaluated on four mainstream cognitive diagnosis tasks and a computerized adaptive testing (CAT) task. Across these settings, the framework yields consistently improved and robust performance compared to baselines that either use only ID embeddings or naively incorporate LM-based semantics.

Conclusion: Carefully fine-tuned language models, combined with a unified textual adapter framework, can effectively enhance learner-item cognitive modeling in cognitive diagnosis without discarding existing paradigms. EduEmbed bridges the objective and feature-space gap between LMs and CD models, providing better generalization and offering empirical insights into how semantic information from LMs contributes differently across various CD tasks, pointing to promising directions for future LM-based educational systems.

Abstract: Learner-item cognitive modeling plays a central role in the web-based online intelligent education system by enabling cognitive diagnosis (CD) across diverse online educational scenarios. Although ID embedding remains the mainstream approach in cognitive modeling due to its effectiveness and flexibility, recent advances in language models (LMs) have introduced new possibilities for incorporating rich semantic representations to enhance CD performance. This highlights the need for a comprehensive analysis of how LMs enhance embeddings through semantic integration across mainstream CD tasks. This paper identifies two key challenges in fully leveraging LMs in existing work: Misalignment between the training objectives of LMs and CD models creates a distribution gap in feature spaces; A unified framework is essential for integrating textual embeddings across varied CD tasks while preserving the strengths of existing cognitive modeling paradigms to ensure the robustness of embedding enhancement. To address these challenges, this paper introduces EduEmbed, a unified embedding enhancement framework that leverages fine-tuned LMs to enrich learner-item cognitive modeling across diverse CD tasks. EduEmbed operates in two stages. In the first stage, we fine-tune LMs based on role-specific representations and an interaction diagnoser to bridge the semantic gap of CD models. In the second stage, we employ a textual adapter to extract task-relevant semantics and integrate them with existing modeling paradigms to improve generalization. We evaluate the proposed framework on four CD tasks and computerized adaptive testing (CAT) task, achieving robust performance. Further analysis reveals the impact of semantic information across diverse tasks, offering key insights for future research on the application of LMs in CD for online intelligent education systems.

</details>


### [42] [Shorter, but Still Trustworthy? An Empirical Study of Chain-of-Thought Compression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04120)
*Lingjie Zeng,Xiaofan Chen,Yanbo Wang,Xiuying Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how compressing chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning traces affects not just accuracy and efficiency, but also model trustworthiness, identifying trade-offs and proposing methods and metrics to better balance efficiency with safety and reliability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most existing work on CoT compression optimizes for reduced token usage while maintaining task accuracy, implicitly assuming that if accuracy is preserved, other properties like safety and reliability are also preserved. However, trustworthiness properties (safety behavior, hallucination resistance, multilingual robustness) are encoded in the same model parameters that compression alters. This raises the concern that compression may silently degrade these properties even when accuracy looks unchanged. There is a lack of systematic empirical analysis of these effects and of metrics that fairly compare methods across models and trust dimensions.

Method: The authors perform a systematic empirical study of several CoT compression methods across models of different scales. They evaluate trustworthiness along three dimensions: (1) safety, (2) hallucination resistance, and (3) multilingual robustness. Comparisons are done under controlled conditions to isolate the effect of CoT compression. To enable fair cross-model and cross-dimension comparison, they introduce a normalized efficiency score per trust dimension that accounts for both performance and compression. Finally, they propose an alignment-aware DPO (Direct Preference Optimization) variant designed to shorten CoT while explicitly considering alignment/trustworthiness, and evaluate it on reasoning benchmarks.

Result: The study finds that CoT compression often leads to regressions in trustworthiness, even when task accuracy and token savings look favorable. Different compression approaches show distinct degradation patterns across safety, hallucination resistance, and multilingual robustness. The normalized efficiency score exposes trade-offs that scalar metrics (e.g., tokens saved or accuracy alone) can hide. Their alignment-aware DPO variant achieves about 19.3% reduction in CoT length on reasoning tasks while incurring substantially smaller trustworthiness losses than baseline compression approaches, demonstrating that more trust-preserving compression is achievable.

Conclusion: CoT compression should not be optimized solely for efficiency and task accuracy. Because compression modifies the same parameter space that encodes alignment-related behaviors, it can silently harm trustworthiness. Designers should treat efficiency and trustworthiness as co-equal constraints, evaluating and optimizing compression methods explicitly for safety, hallucination resistance, and multilingual robustness. The proposed metrics and alignment-aware DPO variant show that it is possible to reduce CoT length while better preserving trustworthy behavior, and future work on reasoning model compression should adopt similar trust-aware evaluation and optimization frameworks.

Abstract: Long chain-of-thought (Long-CoT) reasoning models have motivated a growing body of work on compressing reasoning traces to reduce inference cost, yet existing evaluations focus almost exclusively on task accuracy and token savings. Trustworthiness properties, whether acquired or reinforced through post-training, are encoded in the same parameter space that compression modifies. This means preserving accuracy does not, a priori, guarantee preserving trustworthiness. We conduct the first systematic empirical study of how CoT compression affects model trustworthiness, evaluating multiple models of different scales along three dimensions: safety, hallucination resistance, and multilingual robustness. Under controlled comparisons, we find that CoT compression frequently introduces trustworthiness regressions and that different methods exhibit markedly different degradation profiles across dimensions. To enable fair comparison across bases, we propose a normalized efficiency score for each dimension that reveals how naïve scalar metrics can obscure trustworthiness trade-offs. As an existence proof, we further introduce an alignment-aware DPO variant that reduces CoT length by 19.3\% on reasoning benchmarks with substantially smaller trustworthiness loss. Our findings suggest that CoT compression should be optimized not only for efficiency but also for trustworthiness, treating both as equally important design constraints.

### [43] [Many Preferences, Few Policies: Towards Scalable Language Model Personalization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04144)
*Cheol Woo Kum,Jai Moondra,Roozbeh Nahavandi,Andrew Perrault,Milind Tambe,Swati Gupta*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose PALM, a method to construct a small portfolio of LLMs that together can approximate personalized behavior for many different users, with theoretical guarantees on portfolio size and quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fully personalized LLMs—one dedicated and perfectly aligned model per user—are ideal for personalization but infeasible in practice because of compute, memory, and engineering complexity. There is a need for a principled way to share a small number of models across many heterogeneous users while still capturing diverse preference profiles.

Method: They represent each user’s preferences as a vector of weights over several reward dimensions (e.g., safety, humor, brevity). Using these reward functions, they introduce an algorithm called PALM (Portfolio of Aligned LLMs) that selects or trains a small set of LLMs. The key property is that for any possible preference weight vector (which defines a scalarized objective), the portfolio contains at least one LLM whose performance is provably near-optimal for that objective. They derive theoretical bounds on the size of the portfolio and the approximation factor.

Result: Theoretically, they prove guarantees on both how many LLMs are needed in the portfolio and how close the best portfolio member is to the optimal model for any preference vector. Empirically, they show that PALM portfolios match these guarantees in practice and produce outputs that are more diverse than those from common baselines, indicating better coverage of heterogeneous user preferences.

Conclusion: A small, carefully constructed portfolio of LLMs can approximate the behavior of fully personalized per-user models, with provable bounds on personalization quality versus system cost. PALM formalizes this trade-off, specifies how diverse the portfolio must be to cover a range of user preferences, and empirically improves output diversity over existing approaches.

Abstract: The holy grail of LLM personalization is a single LLM for each user, perfectly aligned with that user's preferences. However, maintaining a separate LLM per user is impractical due to constraints on compute, memory, and system complexity. We address this challenge by developing a principled method for selecting a small portfolio of LLMs that captures representative behaviors across heterogeneous users. We model user preferences across multiple traits (e.g., safety, humor, brevity) through a multi-dimensional weight vector. Given reward functions across these dimensions, our algorithm PALM (Portfolio of Aligned LLMs) generates a small portfolio of LLMs such that, for any weight vector, the portfolio contains a near-optimal LLM for the corresponding scalarized objective. To the best of our knowledge, this is the first result that provides theoretical guarantees on both the size and approximation quality of LLM portfolios for personalization. It characterizes the trade-off between system cost and personalization, as well as the diversity of LLMs required to cover the landscape of user preferences. We provide empirical results that validate these guarantees and demonstrate greater output diversity over common baselines.

### [44] [A Semi-Automated Annotation Workflow for Paediatric Histopathology Reports Using Small Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04168)
*Avish Vijayaraghavan,Jaskaran Singh Kawatra,Sebin Sabu,Jonny Sheldon,Will Poulett,Alex Eze,Daniel Key,John Booth,Shiren Patel,Jonny Pearson,Dan Schofield,Jonathan Hope,Pavithra Rajendran,Neil Sebire*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Large language models can help but are resource-intensive and raise privacy issues when used via cloud APIs, especially for sensitive paediatric data. The authors want an accurate, privacy-preserving, and resource-efficient way to structure specialised clinical text locally.

Method: They design an iterative, clinician-in-the-loop annotation and extraction workflow focused on paediatric renal biopsy histopathology reports. 400 of 2,111 reports are manually annotated to create a gold standard. Information extraction is cast as a question-answering task, guided by clinician-derived entity guidelines and few-shot examples. They test five instruction-tuned small language models, incorporate disagreement modelling to rank cases that most need clinical review, and compare performance to off-the-shelf NLP baselines such as spaCy, BioBERT-SQuAD, RoBERTa-SQuAD, and GLiNER.

The workflow runs on CPU-only infrastructure and requires limited clinician time by prioritising ambiguous reports for review via disagreement modelling.

Conclusion: Small language models, when coupled with a carefully designed semi-automated workflow and clinician-derived guidelines, can effectively extract structured information from specialised, sensitive clinical text such as paediatric renal biopsy reports. This enables local, privacy-preserving, and resource-efficient use of unstructured EPR data, suggesting a practical alternative to large, cloud-based language models for many clinical NLP applications.

Abstract: Electronic Patient Record (EPR) systems contain valuable clinical information, but much of it is trapped in unstructured text, limiting its use for research and decision-making. Large language models can extract such information but require substantial computational resources to run locally, and sending sensitive clinical data to cloud-based services, even when deidentified, raises significant patient privacy concerns. In this study, we develop a resource-efficient semi-automated annotation workflow using small language models (SLMs) to extract structured information from unstructured EPR data, focusing on paediatric histopathology reports. As a proof-of-concept, we apply the workflow to paediatric renal biopsy reports, a domain chosen for its constrained diagnostic scope and well-defined underlying biology. We develop the workflow iteratively with clinical oversight across three meetings, manually annotating 400 reports from a dataset of 2,111 at Great Ormond Street Hospital as a gold standard, while developing an automated information extraction approach using SLMs. We frame extraction as a Question-Answering task grounded by clinician-guided entity guidelines and few-shot examples, evaluating five instruction-tuned SLMs with a disagreement modelling framework to prioritise reports for clinical review. Gemma 2 2B achieves the highest accuracy at 84.3%, outperforming off-the-shelf models including spaCy (74.3%), BioBERT-SQuAD (62.3%), RoBERTa-SQuAD (59.7%), and GLiNER (60.2%). Entity guidelines improved performance by 7-19% over the zero-shot baseline, and few-shot examples by 6-38%, though their benefits do not compound when combined. These results demonstrate that SLMs can extract structured information from specialised clinical domains on CPU-only infrastructure with minimal clinician involvement. Our code is available at https://github.com/gosh-dre/nlp_renal_biopsy.

### [45] [Position: Logical Soundness is not a Reliable Criterion for Neurosymbolic Fact-Checking with LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04177)
*Jason Chan,Robert Gaizauskas,Zhixue Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper critiques the use of formal logic alone in LLM-based fact-checking and proposes using LLMs’ human-like reasoning to check formally valid but potentially misleading inferences.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used in fact-checking, and neurosymbolic systems often rely on formal logic to ensure rigor and reduce bias and hallucinations. However, fact-checking requires alignment with human judgments about what is misleading, not just logical validity. The authors are motivated by the concern that logical soundness does not always match how humans interpret and infer information, creating blind spots in current logic-centric approaches.

Method: The authors conceptually analyze the gap between logical soundness and human inference, drawing on empirical findings from cognitive science and pragmatics. They construct a typology (a structured classification) of cases where logically sound arguments systematically lead humans to draw unsupported or misleading conclusions. They then propose an architectural adjustment in neurosymbolic fact-checking systems: adding an LLM-based component that evaluates whether formally validated conclusions might nevertheless induce misleading human inferences.

Result: The paper provides a typology of divergence cases, showing concrete patterns where formally correct reasoning can still mislead human interpreters. It demonstrates, at least conceptually or via illustrative examples, that logic-based verification alone misses these misleading aspects. The authors also show that LLMs, because they share certain human-like inferential tendencies, can be used as evaluators of whether a formally valid conclusion is pragmatically misleading for humans.

Conclusion: Relying solely on formal logic in LLM-based fact-checking is insufficient, because logical soundness does not guarantee that conclusions align with human interpretations or avoid misleading implications. Neuorsymbolic systems should pair formal logic modules with LLM components that model human-like reasoning, using LLMs to check whether logically valid outputs might still be pragmatically misleading. Human-like inference patterns in LLMs should be treated as a resource to validate formal reasoning, not only as a source of error to be eliminated.

Abstract: As large language models (LLMs) are increasing integrated into fact-checking pipelines, formal logic is often proposed as a rigorous means by which to mitigate bias, errors and hallucinations in these models' outputs. For example, some neurosymbolic systems verify claims by using LLMs to translate natural language into logical formulae and then checking whether the proposed claims are logically sound, i.e. whether they can be validly derived from premises that are verified to be true. We argue that such approaches structurally fail to detect misleading claims due to systematic divergences between conclusions that are logically sound and inferences that humans typically make and accept. Drawing on studies in cognitive science and pragmatics, we present a typology of cases in which logically sound conclusions systematically elicit human inferences that are unsupported by the underlying premises. Consequently, we advocate for a complementary approach: leveraging the human-like reasoning tendencies of LLMs as a feature rather than a bug, and using these models to validate the outputs of formal components in neurosymbolic systems against potentially misleading conclusions.
