### [46] [Which English Do LLMs Prefer? Triangulating Structural Bias Towards American English in Foundation Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04204)
*Mir Tafseer Nayeem,Davood Rafiei*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models (LLMs) systematically favor American English over British English throughout their development pipeline, and proposes a method to quantify dialectal alignment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are used globally but typically expose only a generic “English (US)” setting, despite English having multiple standard varieties and a colonial history. There is concern that such systems may implicitly standardize on one dominant dialect, which could drive linguistic homogenization, marginalize other varieties, and create inequities in global AI deployment. The authors aim to rigorously document and quantify whether and how LLMs structurally privilege American English over British English.

Method: The authors adopt a postcolonial framing and focus on two standard dialects: American English (AmE) and British English (BrE). They: (1) curate a corpus of 1,813 AmE–BrE lexical variants; (2) introduce DiAlign, a dynamic, training-free method that uses distributional evidence to estimate how closely an LLM aligns with each dialect; and (3) examine structural bias at three stages: (i) auditing six major pretraining corpora for AmE vs. BrE frequency skew; (ii) analyzing tokenizer behavior to compare segmentation costs for AmE vs. BrE forms; and (iii) running generative evaluations to see which dialect the models preferentially produce in output.

Result: Across all three stages, they find systematic asymmetries favoring American English: (i) major pretraining corpora are skewed toward AmE forms; (ii) tokenizers segment BrE variants into more subword units (higher cost), indicating they are less “native” to the vocabulary; and (iii) generative behavior of contemporary LLMs shows a persistent preference for AmE variants even in contexts where BrE would be expected. DiAlign successfully captures these dialectal alignment patterns without additional training.

Conclusion: The study provides the first systematic, multi-stage evidence that large language models treat American English as the de facto normative variety, embedding structural dialectal bias from data to tokenization to generation. This privileging of AmE risks linguistic homogenization and contributes to epistemic injustice and inequitable AI deployment in a global context. The authors argue for recognizing and mitigating such biases and propose that dialectally inclusive design—such as more balanced corpora, tokenizer adjustments, and evaluation frameworks like DiAlign—is necessary for fairer language technologies.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed in high-stakes domains, yet they expose only limited language settings, most notably "English (US)," despite the global diversity and colonial history of English. Through a postcolonial framing to explain the broader significance, we investigate how geopolitical histories of data curation, digital dominance, and linguistic standardization shape the LLM development pipeline. Focusing on two dominant standard varieties, American English (AmE) and British English (BrE), we construct a curated corpus of 1,813 AmE--BrE variants and introduce DiAlign, a dynamic, training-free method for estimating dialectal alignment using distributional evidence. We operationalize structural bias by triangulating evidence across three stages: (i) audits of six major pretraining corpora reveal systematic skew toward AmE, (ii) tokenizer analyses show that BrE forms incur higher segmentation costs, and (iii) generative evaluations show a persistent AmE preference in model outputs. To our knowledge, this is the first systematic and multi-faceted examination of dialectal asymmetries in standard English varieties across the phases of LLM development. We find that contemporary LLMs privilege AmE as the de facto norm, raising concerns about linguistic homogenization, epistemic injustice, and inequity in global AI deployment, while motivating practical steps toward more dialectally inclusive language technologies.

This fragmentation slows research iteration, raises the engineering burden of reproduction, and makes fair comparison across algorithms difficult. Across representative model families including LLaDA, Dream, SDAR, and LLaDA2.x, DARE provides broad algorithmic coverage, reproducible benchmark evaluation, and practical acceleration. ### [48] [Adaptive Cost-Efficient Evaluation for Reliable Patent Claim Validation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04295)
*Yongmin Yoo,Qiongkai Xu,Longbing Cao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: ACE is a hybrid, cost-efficient system for automatically validating patent claims by routing only uncertain cases to an expert LLM that reasons with patent law standards.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Patent-claim validation requires near-zero errors because a single structural issue can invalidate a claim. Existing methods either use cheap but shallow models that miss complex legal dependencies, or expensive LLMs that are accurate but cost-prohibitive at scale. There is a need for a system that maintains high legal accuracy while remaining computationally and financially efficient.

Method: The authors propose ACE, an adaptive evaluation framework. First, a lightweight model scores each patent claim and estimates predictive entropy (uncertainty). Low-uncertainty claims are handled directly by this cheap model. High-uncertainty claims are routed to an expert LLM. The LLM follows a structured "Chain of Patent Thought" (CoPT), explicitly reasoning through 35 U.S.C. statutory standards, and identifies structural errors. The approach is evaluated on a newly constructed benchmark of 40,000 claims with detailed, MPEP-grounded error labels.

Result: ACE attains the highest reported F1 score among compared methods at 94.95%, while cutting operational cost by 78% relative to using an expert LLM alone for all claims. The new ACE-40k benchmark provides large-scale, law-grounded annotations for evaluating patent claim validation systems.

Conclusion: By selectively invoking an expert LLM only for uncertain cases and constraining it with a law-grounded reasoning protocol, ACE reconciles accuracy with cost, achieving near-zero-defect performance more efficiently. The release of the ACE-40k dataset is expected to catalyze further research in legally reliable, cost-aware patent claim validation.

Abstract: Automated validation of patent claims demands zero-defect tolerance, as even a single structural flaw can render a claim legally defective. Existing evaluation paradigms suffer from a rigidity-resource dilemma: lightweight encoders struggle with nuanced legal dependencies, while exhaustive verification via Large Language Models (LLMs) is prohibitively costly. To bridge this gap, we propose ACE (Adaptive Cost-efficient Evaluation), a hybrid framework that uses predictive entropy to route only high-uncertainty claims to an expert LLM. The expert then executes a Chain of Patent Thought (CoPT) protocol grounded in 35 U.S.C. statutory standards. This design enables ACE to handle long-range legal dependencies more effectively while preserving efficiency. ACE achieves the best F1 among the evaluated methods at 94.95\%, while reducing operational costs by 78\% compared to standalone LLM deployments. We also construct ACE-40k, a 40,000-claim benchmark with MPEP-grounded error annotations, to facilitate further research.

</details>

Individual investing, however, involves evolving, contradictory behaviors and high-stakes outcomes with delayed, stochastic feedback. Method: They build and deploy an AI-augmented portfolio management system for individual investors, then use this real-world experience as a lens to identify four core dimensions where investing stresses current LLM personalization methods. They propose architectural strategies to cope with these and highlight gaps in current LLM tooling and evaluation.

Addressing these issues requires new architectures and research in personalized NLP for high-stakes, temporally extended decision-making contexts, beyond what current chat-style or short-session LLM systems provide.

Abstract: Personalized LLM systems have advanced rapidly, yet most operate in domains where user preferences are stable and ground truth is either absent or subjective. We argue that individual investor decision-making presents a uniquely challenging domain for LLM personalization - one that exposes fundamental limitations in current customization paradigms. Drawing on our system, built and deployed for AI-augmented portfolio management, we identify four axes along which individual investing exposes fundamental limitations in standard LLM customization: (1) behavioral memory complexity, where investor patterns are temporally evolving, self-contradictory, and financially consequential; (2) thesis consistency under drift, where maintaining coherent investment rationale over weeks or months strains stateless and session-bounded architectures; (3) style-signal tension, where the system must simultaneously respect personal investment philosophy and surface objective evidence that may contradict it; and (4) alignment without ground truth, where personalization quality cannot be evaluated against a fixed label set because outcomes are stochastic and delayed. We describe the architectural responses that emerged from building the system and propose open research directions for personalized NLP in high-stakes, temporally extended decision domains.

### [50] [How Well Do Agentic Skills Work in the Wild: Benchmarking LLM Skill Usage in Realistic Settings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04323)
*Yujian Liu,Jiabao Ji,Li An,Tommi Jaakkola,Yang Zhang,Shiyu Chang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how well reusable “agent skills” actually help LLM-based agents when used under realistic conditions, finding that their benefits largely vanish unless retrieval and refinement are carefully handled.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While skills (reusable domain-specific procedures) are widely used to augment LLM agents, there is little rigorous evaluation of how useful they are when agents must autonomously select from large, imperfect skill repositories instead of being given perfect, hand-crafted skills for each task. The authors want to close this gap by stress-testing skills under realistic usage scenarios.

Method: They build progressively more realistic evaluation settings in which LLM agents must retrieve relevant skills from a large repository of 34k real-world skills, often without any hand-curated or perfectly matching options. They benchmark multiple LLMs on these settings, compare against no-skill baselines, and then introduce and test skill refinement strategies—both query-specific and query-agnostic—to see whether they can restore lost performance. They also validate retrieval and refinement on Terminal-Bench 2.0.

Result: As conditions become more realistic (no direct injection of perfectly matched skills, large noisy skill pool, imperfect matches), performance gains from skills steadily erode, eventually nearing no-skill baselines. Query-specific refinement is effective at recovering performance, provided the retrieved skills are reasonably relevant and of decent quality. On Terminal-Bench 2.0, their retrieval + refinement pipeline boosts Claude Opus 4.6’s pass rate from 57.7% to 65.5%. These behaviors are consistent across different LLMs.

Conclusion: Skill-based augmentation has promise but is fragile in realistic deployments: naive use of large skill libraries yields limited benefit because retrieval noise and imperfect skills erase most gains. Effective use of skills requires strong retrieval and especially query-specific refinement to adapt skills to the task, and future work should focus on making skill systems robust under such practical conditions.

Abstract: Agent skills, which are reusable, domain-specific knowledge artifacts, have become a popular mechanism for extending LLM-based agents, yet formally benchmarking skill usage performance remains scarce. Existing skill benchmarking efforts focus on overly idealized conditions, where LLMs are directly provided with hand-crafted, narrowly-tailored task-specific skills for each task, whereas in many realistic settings, the LLM agent may have to search for and select relevant skills on its own, and even the closest matching skills may not be well-tailored for the task. In this paper, we conduct the first comprehensive study of skill utility under progressively challenging realistic settings, where agents must retrieve skills from a large collection of 34k real-world skills and may not have access to any hand-curated skills. Our findings reveal that the benefits of skills are fragile: performance gains degrade consistently as settings become more realistic, with pass rates approaching no-skill baselines in the most challenging scenarios. To narrow this gap, we study skill refinement strategies, including query-specific and query-agnostic approaches, and we show that query-specific refinement substantially recovers lost performance when the initial skills are of reasonable relevance and quality. We further demonstrate the generality of retrieval and refinement on Terminal-Bench 2.0, where they improve the pass rate of Claude Opus 4.6 from 57.7% to 65.5%. Our results, consistent across multiple models, highlight both the promise and the current limitations of skills for LLM-based agents. Our code is available at https://github.com/UCSB-NLP-Chang/Skill-Usage.

### [51] [Benchmarking Multi-turn Medical Diagnosis: Hold, Lure, and Self-Correction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04325)
*Jinrui Fang,Runhan Chen,Xu Yang,Jian Yu,Jiawei Xu,Ashwin Vinod,Wenqi Shi,Tianlong Chen,Heng Ji,ChengXiang Zhai,Ying Ding,Yuji Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MINT, a multi-turn medical diagnosis benchmark, and uses it to show that LLMs prematurely commit to diagnoses, yet have latent self-correction ability that can be unlocked by adjusting interaction design and evidence order.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations usually give all clinical information in a single prompt, which does not reflect real clinical practice where information is gathered over multiple turns. It is unknown how LLMs behave in such incremental, conversational diagnostic settings, and whether their reliability changes as evidence accumulates.

Method: The authors build MINT, a benchmark of 1,035 medical cases decomposed into labeled evidence shards, with controlled turn granularity and information-preserving decomposition. They then systematically test 11 LLMs on this benchmark, tracking when diagnoses are made, how they change over turns, and how specific types and orders of evidence influence premature commitment and self-correction.

Result: They identify three key behavioral patterns: (1) an "intent to answer" where models commit to a diagnosis too early, with more than 55% of answers given in the first two turns; (2) strong self-correction asymmetry, where models change from wrong to right up to 10.6x more often than from right to wrong, but this benefit is cut off once they commit; and (3) "strong lures," where salient evidence like lab results induces early, often premature, answering even when instructed to wait. They also show that interaction design changes—delaying the diagnostic question and reordering when salient evidence is presented—can substantially improve accuracy and reduce premature commitment.

Conclusion: LLMs show problematic early-commitment behavior in realistic multi-turn diagnostic settings, but they also possess notable self-correction capabilities that can be harnessed through better interaction design. The MINT benchmark offers a controlled way to study these behaviors, and practical recommendations include deferring explicit diagnostic queries and strategically ordering evidence to improve safety and accuracy in clinical use.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) achieve high accuracy in medical diagnosis when all clinical information is provided in a single turn, yet how they behave under multi-turn evidence accumulation closer to real clinical reasoning remains unexplored. We introduce MINT (Medical Incremental N-Turn Benchmark), a high-fidelity, multi-turn medical diagnosis benchmark comprising 1,035 cases with clinically labeled evidence shards, controlled turn granularity, and information-preserving decomposition. Through systematic evaluation of 11 LLMs on MINT, we uncover three persistent behavioral patterns that significantly impact diagnostic decisions: (1) intent to answer, models rush to answer before sufficient evidence has been observed, with over 55% of answers committed within the first two turns; (2) self-correction, incorrect-to-correct answer revisions occur at up to 10.6 times the rate of correct-to-incorrect flips, revealing a latent capacity for self-correction that premature commitment forecloses; and (3) strong lures, clinically salient information such as laboratory results trigger premature answering even when models are explicitly instructed to wait. We translate these findings into clinically actionable guidance: deferring the diagnostic question to later turns reduces premature answering and improves accuracy at the first point of commitment by up to 62.6%, while reserving salient clinical evidence for later turns prevents a catastrophic accuracy drop of up to 23.3% caused by premature commitment. Our work provides both a controlled evaluation framework and concrete recommendations for improving the reliability of LLMs in multi-turn medical diagnosis.

### [52] [GROUNDEDKG-RAG: Grounded Knowledge Graph Index for Long-document Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04359)
*Tianyi Zhang,Andreas Marfurt*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce GroundedKG-RAG, a RAG system that builds a knowledge-graph grounded in the original document to answer long-document questions more efficiently and accurately.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG for long-document QA relies heavily on LLM-written summaries and hierarchical descriptions, which increases cost and latency, introduces repetitive content, and can cause hallucinations because the answers are not tightly grounded in the source text.

Method: They construct a GroundedKG where nodes are entities and actions and edges are temporal and semantic relations, each linked back to specific source sentences. The graph is built from SRL and AMR parses, embedded for retrieval, and queries are transformed in the same way so the system can retrieve the most relevant grounded sentences for the LLM to answer questions.

Result: On NarrativeQA, GroundedKG-RAG matches a strong proprietary long-context model at lower cost and beats a competitive baseline while producing interpretable intermediate structures.

Conclusion: GroundedKG-RAG improves the efficiency and factual grounding of long-document QA by using an explicitly grounded, human-readable knowledge graph for retrieval, enabling state-of-the-art-level performance with better interpretability and lower resource use.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) systems have been widely adopted in contemporary large language models (LLMs) due to their ability to improve generation quality while reducing the required input context length. In this work, we focus on RAG systems for long-document question answering. Current approaches suffer from a heavy reliance on LLM descriptions resulting in high resource consumption and latency, repetitive content across hierarchical levels, and hallucinations due to no or limited grounding in the source text. To improve both efficiency and factual accuracy through grounding, we propose GroundedKG-RAG, a RAG system in which the knowledge graph is explicitly extracted from and grounded in the source document. Specifically, we define nodes in GroundedKG as entities and actions, and edges as temporal or semantic relations, with each node and edge grounded in the original sentences. We construct GroundedKG from semantic role labeling (SRL) and abstract meaning representation (AMR) parses and then embed it for retrieval. During querying, we apply the same transformation to the query and retrieve the most relevant sentences from the grounded source text for question answering. We evaluate GroundedKG-RAG on examples from the NarrativeQA dataset and find that it performs on par with a state-of-the art proprietary long-context model at smaller cost and outperforms a competitive baseline. Additionally, our GroundedKG is interpretable and readable by humans, facilitating auditing of results and error analysis.

### [53] [Compressible Softmax-Attended Language under Incompressible Attention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04384)
*Wonsuk Lee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper finds that in transformer language models, the effective dimensionality of attention interactions in real language data is extremely low, despite the attention mechanism itself having high nominal dimensionality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how much of the attention mechanism’s representational capacity is actually used when processing natural language, and whether the complexity of attention arises from the model architecture or from the data it is trained on.

Method: Analyze five transformer language models with varying sizes and architectures by computing the logit energy field for attention heads and performing singular value decomposition (SVD) to measure how many singular components explain most of the variance. Compare this to the rank properties of the learned query-key interaction matrix W_Q^T W_K, and quantify effective rank and spectral gaps.

Result: Across all attention heads and models, 90% of the variance in the logit energy field is captured by just 2–11 singular components, while the learned interaction matrix W_Q^T W_K requires 38–75 components (out of head dimensions 64 or 128) to reach the same threshold. This implies a 5–25x gap in effective rank between the model’s potential interaction space and the interactions realized on language data.

Conclusion: Attention layers distribute capacity uniformly across all head dimensions, but natural language uses only a small, low-dimensional subset of this space. The observed compressibility of softmax-attended representations is driven by properties of the data rather than limitations of the attention parameterization itself.

Abstract: Across every attention head in five transformer language models (124M--7B parameters, four architecture families), the logit energy field $\tilde{E}$ reaches 90\% of its variance in 2--11 singular components. The \emph{learned} interaction matrix $W_Q^\mathrm{T} W_K$ needs 38--75 components for the same threshold out of $d_h \in \{64, 128\}$. The spectral gap is $5$--$25\times$ in effective rank. The attention mechanism allocates capacity uniformly across all $d_h$ dimensions, but language concentrates the actual interaction into a few. The compressibility of softmax-attended language is a property of the data, not the frame that analyzes it.

</details>


### [54] [How Alignment Routes: Localizing, Scaling, and Controlling Policy Circuits in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04385)
*Gregory N. Frank*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper discovers and characterizes a sparse ‘gate-and-amplifier’ routing circuit that converts detected harmful or disallowed content into refusal behavior in alignment-trained language models, and shows this circuit is manipulable and less robust than underlying semantic representations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Why and how do alignment-trained language models decide to refuse answering certain prompts, and is there a common, interpretable mechanism across different models and labs? The authors want to move from black-box behavior (e.g., refusals on safety or political content) to mechanistic understanding, quantify the stability and scaling of the underlying circuits, and probe the robustness and limits of policy binding compared to general semantic understanding.

Method: They perform mechanistic interpretability analyses on 9 language models from 6 labs using curated corpora of 120 prompt pairs each involving political censorship and safety refusal. They identify attention heads involved in detecting sensitive content and those that amplify refusal signals. They validate a ‘gate’ attention head using necessity and sufficiency interchange interventions, checking statistical significance via permutation tests. They identify ‘amplifier’ heads and test their stability with bootstrap resampling and Jaccard similarity. Across same-generation scaling pairs, they examine how routing distributes as models grow, using ablations and interchange interventions. They then manipulate the detection-layer signal to continuously vary policy strength, measuring outcomes from hard refusal to compliant answering. Finally, they use cipher-encoded prompts to decouple semantic understanding from routing, and use probes on deeper layers to detect internal harmful-content representations even when routing fails.

Result: They find a recurring sparse routing structure: a single or small set of ‘gate’ attention heads respond to detected sensitive content and trigger downstream ‘amplifier’ heads that drive refusals. The gate head passes necessity and sufficiency tests for causing refusal, with strong statistical significance (p < 0.001). Core amplifier heads are highly stable under resampling with Jaccard similarity between 0.92 and 1.0. In scaled-up models from the same family, refusal routing becomes more distributed (ablation effects up to 17x weaker per head) but interchange tests still detect the mechanism. By modulating the detection-layer signal they can smoothly control refusal policy strength, revealing topic-dependent routing thresholds. Under cipher encodings, the gate head’s routing effect collapses (e.g., 78% collapse in Phi-4 at n=120); the models then tend to solve the puzzle instead of refusing, even though deeper-layer probes show harmful content is internally represented.

Conclusion: Alignment policies in modern LMs are implemented by a relatively sparse and interpretable gate-and-amplifier routing circuit that connects early content detection to downstream refusal behavior. This circuit is statistically robust, recurs across many labs and models, and scales by distributing across more components while remaining mechanistically detectable. However, the routing mechanism is brittle under input transformations such as ciphers: semantic understanding of harmful content persists, but the policy-binding circuit fails to activate, exposing a structural separation between intent recognition and policy routing. This suggests that pretraining yields broad, robust semantics, while post-training alignment creates narrower, less robust circuits that can be systematically probed, modulated, and potentially bypassed or improved.

Abstract: We identify a recurring sparse routing mechanism in alignment-trained language models: a gate attention head reads detected content and triggers downstream amplifier heads that boost the signal toward refusal. Using political censorship and safety refusal as natural experiments, we trace this mechanism across 9 models from 6 labs, all validated on corpora of 120 prompt pairs. The gate head passes necessity and sufficiency interchange tests (p < 0.001, permutation null), and core amplifier heads are stable under bootstrap resampling (Jaccard 0.92-1.0). Three same-generation scaling pairs show that routing distributes at scale (ablation up to 17x weaker) while remaining detectable by interchange. By modulating the detection-layer signal, we continuously control policy strength from hard refusal through steering to factual compliance, with routing thresholds that vary by topic. The circuit also reveals a structural separation between intent recognition and policy routing: under cipher encoding, the gate head's routing contribution collapses (78% in Phi-4 at n=120) while the model responds with puzzle-solving rather than refusal. The routing mechanism never fires, even though probe scores at deeper layers indicate the model begins to represent the harmful content. This asymmetry is consistent with different robustness properties of pretraining and post-training: broad semantic understanding versus narrower policy binding that generalizes less well under input transformation.

</details>

Result: They find a substantial gap between what is decodable from internal representations and what is expressed in generated responses. Task-relevant information is often more linearly encoded in intermediate layers than in the final layer. In this paper, we investigate how information required to solve VDU tasks is represented across different layers of LLMs within LVLMs using linear probing. Our study reveals that (1) there is a clear gap between internal representations and generated responses, and (2) information required to solve the task is often encoded more linearly from intermediate layers than from the final layer. Motivated by these findings, we explore fine-tuning strategies that target intermediate layers. Experiments show that fine-tuning intermediate layers improves both linear probing accuracy and response accuracy while narrowing the gap.

### [56] [Structured Causal Video Reasoning via Multi-Objective Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04415)
*Zinuo Li,Yongxin Guo,Jun Liu,Jiawei Zhan,Xi Jiang,Chengjie Wang,Mohammed Bennamoun,Farid Boussaid,Feng Zheng,Qiuhong Ke*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a structured, causal event representation and a multi-objective RL training pipeline to improve Video-LLM reasoning, resulting in a new model, Factum-4B, with stronger temporal and causal understanding.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Video-LLMs rely on unstructured reasoning over verbose descriptions, where key visual evidence is entangled with text and temporal causality is weakly modeled. This leads to inefficient, brittle reasoning that is unlike how humans understand video, which uses compact, structured mental representations of entities, actions, and temporal relations. The authors aim to bridge this cognitive gap and make video reasoning more concise, interpretable, and causally grounded.

Method: 1) They define Structured Event Facts, a compact, explicit representation of salient events and their causal relations in video, used as a prior before reasoning. 2) They build CausalFact-60K, a dataset for training models to produce and use these structured facts. 3) They design a four-stage training pipeline: (a) facts alignment (align model outputs with the structured facts), (b) format warm-start (teach the model to follow the structured format), (c) thinking warm-start (encourage multi-step, causally grounded reasoning using the facts), and (d) reinforcement learning post-training. 4) In the RL stage, they cast training as a Multi-Objective Reinforcement Learning problem, explicitly balancing structural completeness, causal fidelity, and reasoning length by optimizing toward the Pareto frontier. 5) They instantiate this framework in a 4B-parameter Video-LLM named Factum-4B.

Result: Factum-4B, trained with the proposed structured facts and MORL-based pipeline, achieves stronger performance on challenging video understanding benchmarks that require fine-grained temporal and causal inference, and produces reasoning that is more reliable, concise, and easier to verify than prior Video-LLMs.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling video as Structured Event Facts and training Video-LLMs with a multi-objective RL framework that balances structure, causality, and brevity leads to more robust, human-like video reasoning. The resulting Factum-4B model demonstrates improved temporal and causal understanding and more interpretable intermediate evidence on complex video understanding tasks.

Abstract: Human understanding of video dynamics is typically grounded in a structured mental representation of entities, actions, and temporal relations, rather than relying solely on immediate deductive reasoning. In contrast, existing Video-LLMs largely depend on unstructured video reasoning, where critical visual evidence is embedded in verbose textual descriptions and temporal causality is often weakly modeled. This leads to inefficient processes and fragile causal inference. To bridge this cognitive gap, we propose constructing a compact representation of salient events and their causal relationships, which we name Structured Event Facts, prior to the reasoning stage. This structured prior serves as an explicit constraint to promote concise and causally grounded reasoning, while also making intermediate evidence easier to verify. To effectively train models on such structured facts, we introduce CausalFact-60K and a four-stage training pipeline comprising facts alignment, format warm-start, thinking warm-start, and reinforcement learning-based post-training. During RL stage, we find that this framework introduces competing objectives, as structural completeness and causal fidelity must be balanced against reasoning length, making it difficult to optimize. We address this challenge by formulating the optimization as a Multi-Objective Reinforcement Learning (MORL) problem and explicitly optimizing toward the Pareto-Frontier to balance these trade-offs. As a result, we introduce Factum-4B, which yields more reliable reasoning and delivers stronger performance on challenging video understanding tasks requiring fine-grained temporal inference.

Method: The authors build DEONTICBENCH, a dataset of 6,232 tasks drawn from U.S. federal tax rules, airline baggage policies, U.S. immigration administration rules, and state housing law. The gap between improved program generation and actual task success highlights limitations in current RL approaches and model reasoning capabilities in complex deontic settings.

Conclusion: DEONTICBENCH exposes significant weaknesses of current LLMs and code models in long-context, high-stakes deontic reasoning over real-world legal and policy rules. The benchmark, with its dual natural-language and Prolog-based workflows, offers a standardized way to study context-grounded rule reasoning in both symbolic and non-symbolic paradigms. The results suggest that advancing reliable deontic reasoning will require better methods than current chain-of-thought prompting and RL-based program generation and that DEONTICBENCH can serve as a testbed for such future research.

Abstract: Reasoning with complex, context-specific rules remains challenging for large language models (LLMs). In legal and policy settings, this manifests as deontic reasoning: reasoning about obligations, permissions, and prohibitions under explicit rules. While many recent benchmarks emphasize short-context mathematical reasoning, fewer focus on long-context, high-stakes deontic reasoning. To address this gap, we introduce DEONTICBENCH, a benchmark of 6,232 tasks across U.S. federal taxes, airline baggage policies, U.S. immigration administration, and U.S. state housing law. These tasks can be approached in multiple ways, including direct reasoning in language or with the aid of symbolic computation. Besides free-form chain-of-thought reasoning, DEONTICBENCH enables an optional solver-based workflow in which models translate statutes and case facts into executable Prolog, leading to formal problem interpretations and an explicit program trace. We release reference Prolog programs for all instances. Across frontier LLMs and coding models, best hard-subset performance reaches only 44.4% on SARA Numeric and 46.6 macro-F1 on Housing. We further study training with supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning for symbolic program generation. Although training improves Prolog generation quality, current RL methods still fail to solve these tasks reliably. Overall, DEONTICBENCH provides a benchmark for studying context-grounded rule reasoning in real-world domains under both symbolic and non-symbolic settings.

### [59] [Same Geometry, Opposite Noise: Transformer Magnitude Representations Lack Scalar Variability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04469)
*Jon-Paul Cacioli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether transformer language models show scalar variability in number representations and finds they do not; instead, variability decreases with magnitude and is largely driven by corpus frequency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In biological systems, estimates of magnitudes (like time, number, size) show scalar variability: noise scales proportionally with the magnitude, leading to a constant coefficient of variation. This is a core signature of how the brain encodes quantities. Transformer language models have been shown to develop structured magnitude representations (e.g., approximate number lines). The authors want to know whether these models also capture this key biological property of scalar variability, and thus whether distributional learning on language alone can reproduce both the geometry and noise properties of human-like magnitude representations.

Method: The authors examined hidden-state representations of 26 numerical magnitudes embedded in multiple carrier sentences for three 7–8B-parameter transformer models (Llama-3-8B-Instruct, Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.3, Llama-3-8B-Base), using pre-existing data (Cacioli, 2026). For each number and model layer, they measured the dispersion (variability) of the corresponding internal representations across different sentences. They then quantified how this variability scaled with magnitude, fitting a power-law exponent (alpha) along the inferred magnitude axis, in the full-dimensional representation space, and after controlling for sentence identity. They also compared variability along the magnitude axis to variability along orthogonal directions and correlated per-number variability with corpus frequency.

Result: Across all three models and layers, representational variability decreased with numerical magnitude along the magnitude axis, with an average scaling exponent around -0.19 and no primary layers showing positive alpha. In the full representational space, the exponent was closer to zero but still negative (about -0.04), and remained slightly negative even after correcting for sentence identity (about -0.007). The anti-scalar effect (decreasing variability with magnitude) was 3–5 times stronger along the magnitude axis than along orthogonal axes, indicating that it is specific to number-related structure. Furthermore, variability for each number was strongly predicted by that number’s frequency in the training corpus (Spearman’s rho ≈ .84).

Conclusion: Transformer language models do not exhibit scalar variability in their numerical magnitude representations; instead, larger magnitudes are represented more stably than smaller ones, contrary to biological magnitude systems. Although these models learn a log-compressed magnitude geometry, their noise structure differs fundamentally from that of brains. The strong influence of corpus frequency on representational variability suggests that distributional learning from language alone cannot reproduce the constant-coefficient-of-variation noise signature characteristic of biological magnitude encoding, implying that additional constraints or learning signals are needed to capture this aspect of human-like magnitude cognition.

Abstract: Scalar variability -- the finding that representational noise scales proportionally with magnitude, producing a constant coefficient of variation -- is a hallmark of biological magnitude systems. We tested whether transformer language models exhibit this property by analysing the dispersion of hidden-state representations across carrier sentences for 26 numerical magnitudes in three 7-8B parameter models (Llama-3-8B-Instruct, Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.3, Llama-3-8B-Base; data from Cacioli, 2026). We found the opposite: representational variability decreased with magnitude along the magnitude axis (scaling exponent alpha approx -0.19; 0/16 primary layers with alpha > 0, all three models). The negative sign was consistent in full-dimensional space (alpha approx -0.04) and after sentence-identity correction (alpha approx -0.007). The anti-scalar pattern was 3-5x stronger along the magnitude axis than orthogonal dimensions, and corpus frequency strongly predicted per-magnitude variability (rho = .84). These results demonstrate that distributional learning alone is insufficient to produce scalar variability: transformers reproduce log-compressive magnitude geometry but not the constant-CV noise signature observed in biological systems.

</details>

The system integrates active learning to prioritize informative examples, provides annotation suggestions, and supports importing and adapting resources from related languages. Result: The result is a fully implemented, publicly accessible platform (https://common-morph.com) that can be used to build morphological resources for many languages, including low-resource ones. Conclusion: CommonMorph provides a replicable, scalable model for collaborative construction of morphological datasets, lowering entry barriers for low-resource language documentation and enabling better integration with NLP tools. By combining expert design, crowdsourced elicitation, and community validation with intelligent assistance, it advances the preservation and technological support of linguistic diversity.

Abstract: Collecting and annotating morphological data present significant challenges, requiring linguistic expertise, methodological rigour, and substantial resources. These barriers are particularly acute for low-resource languages and varieties. To accelerate this process, we introduce \texttt{CommonMorph}, a comprehensive platform that streamlines morphological data collection development through a three-tiered approach: expert linguistic definition, contributor elicitation, and community validation. The platform minimises manual work by incorporating active learning, annotation suggestions, and tools to import and adapt materials from related languages. It accommodates diverse morphological systems, including fusional, agglutinative, and root-and-pattern morphologies. Our platform is accessible at https://common-morph.com, offering a replicable model for preserving linguistic diversity through collaborative technology.

### [61] [Multilingual Prompt Localization for Agent-as-a-Judge: Language and Backbone Sensitivity in Requirement-Level Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04532)
*Alhasan Mahmood,Samir Abdaljalil,Hasan Kurban*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that the language used by the automatic judge in agentic code benchmarks can dramatically change which backbone model appears best, so language must be treated as an explicit evaluation variable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most agentic code and DevAI benchmarks assume English as the fixed language for the evaluation judge, implicitly treating performance as language-agnostic. However, modern LLM backbones have uneven multilingual capabilities, and prompt stacks for judges are typically designed in English. This raises the concern that apparent backbone rankings and satisfaction scores may be artifacts of the judge’s language rather than true capability differences.

Method: The authors localize an Agent-as-a-Judge evaluation setup into five typologically diverse languages: English, Arabic, Turkish, Chinese, and Hindi. They run 55 DevAI development tasks across three different developer-agent frameworks and six different judge backbones, resulting in 4950 judge runs. They then compare satisfaction scores and agreement metrics (e.g., Fleiss’ kappa) across languages and backbones. Additionally, they perform a controlled ablation where they vary the degree of localization on the judge side (full vs partial localization of judge instructions versus only benchmark content) to isolate the effect of instruction language on outcomes.

Result: They find strong interactions between backbone and judge language. GPT-4o performs best as a judge in English (44.72% satisfaction), but Gemini becomes the top judge in Arabic (51.72%, significantly better than GPT-4o with p<0.001) and Hindi (53.22%). No single backbone is consistently best across all languages, and inter-backbone agreement on requirement-level judgments is only modest (Fleiss’ κ ≤ 0.231), indicating substantial variability. The ablation shows that merely localizing task content is not enough: changing the language of judge-side instructions has a large effect, with Hindi satisfaction dropping from 42.8% to 23.2% under partial localization, demonstrating that instruction language can be decisive.

Conclusion: Language is a crucial and currently underappreciated variable in agentic DevAI benchmarks. Judge language can invert backbone rankings and substantially alter satisfaction scores, so benchmarks that rely on a single default (typically English) risk drawing misleading conclusions about model quality. Proper multilingual evaluation should explicitly vary and report judge language, and localization must include the entire judge prompt stack, not just benchmark content. The authors provide full requirement-level judgment data and runtime statistics to support further analysis and reproducibility.

Abstract: Evaluation language is typically treated as a fixed English default in agentic code benchmarks, yet we show that changing the judge's language can invert backbone rankings. We localize the Agent-as-a-Judge prompt stack to five typologically diverse languages (English, Arabic, Turkish, Chinese, Hindi) and evaluate 55 DevAI development tasks across three developer-agent frameworks and six judge backbones, totaling 4950 judge runs. The central finding is that backbone and language interact: GPT-4o achieves the highest satisfaction in English (44.72\%), while Gemini leads in Arabic (51.72\%, $p<0.001$ vs.\ GPT-4o) and Hindi (53.22\%). No single backbone dominates across all languages, and inter-backbone agreement on individual requirement judgments is modest (Fleiss' $κ\leq 0.231$). A controlled ablation further shows that localizing judge-side instructions, not just benchmark content, can be decisive: Hindi satisfaction drops from 42.8\% to 23.2\% under partial localization. These results indicate that language should be treated as an explicit evaluation variable in agentic benchmarks. Full requirement-level judgments and runtime statistics are released for reproducibility.

### [62] [Formal Constraints on Dependency Syntax](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04542)
*Gómez-Rodríguez,Carlos,Alemany-Puig,Lluís*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies structural constraints on dependency syntax trees to better match real language use than fully unrestricted trees but be more flexible than strictly projective structures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although dependency syntax can, in principle, allow any tree of lexical dependencies, many such trees are implausible or extremely rare in real languages. Researchers want constraints that capture realistic syntactic patterns, to improve linguistic description, parsing efficiency, and understanding of language evolution and processing. Projectivity is the classic constraint, but it is too restrictive, especially for free- or flexible-word-order languages, so better intermediate constraints are needed.

Method: Conceptually survey and define structural constraints on dependency trees that lie between full projectivity and completely unrestricted dependency structures, examining how they relate to attested linguistic phenomena and parsing considerations.

Result: The abstract states that projectivity alone is insufficient and that many alternative constraints have been proposed to balance expressive power with linguistic plausibility and computational tractability, especially for languages with flexible word order.

Conclusion: There exists a continuum of constraints on dependency structures: strict projectivity is too limiting, while fully unrestricted trees are unrealistically permissive. Intermediate constraint systems are necessary and have been actively developed to more accurately capture real syntactic patterns and support efficient parsing and linguistic analysis.

Abstract: Dependency syntax represents the structure of a sentence as a tree composed of dependencies, i.e., directed relations between lexical units. While in its more general form any such tree is allowed, in practice many are not plausible or are very infrequent in attested language. This has motivated a search for constraints characterizing subsets of trees that better fit real linguistic phenomena, providing a more accurate linguistic description, faster parsing or insights on language evolution and human processing. Projectivity is the most well-studied such constraint, but it has been shown to be too restrictive to represent some linguistic phenomena, especially in flexible-word-order languages. Thus, a variety of constraints have been proposed to seek a realistic middle ground between the limitations of projectivity and the excessive leniency of unrestricted dependency structures.

### [63] [PassiveQA: A Three-Action Framework for Epistemically Calibrated Question Answering via Supervised Finetuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04565)
*Madhav S Baidya*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces PassiveQA, a framework that teaches QA/RAG models to decide when to answer, ask for clarification, or abstain, thereby reducing hallucinations under incomplete information.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Real-world user queries are often incomplete or ambiguous, but current LLM-based QA and RAG systems implicitly assume that all queries are fully specified and answerable. As a result, they tend to respond confidently even when key information is missing, leading to hallucinations and unreliable outputs. There is a need for methods that give models epistemic awareness: the ability to recognize when they do not know enough to answer and to act accordingly.

Method: The authors formulate QA under incomplete information as a three-action decision problem: Answer, Ask (for clarification), or Abstain. They propose PassiveQA, which uses supervised finetuning to align the model’s behavior with information sufficiency. The approach incorporates (1) structured representations of the information state, (2) knowledge-graph-grounded context to capture relations and missing variables, and (3) a finetuned planner module that explicitly reasons about which variables are missing and which action to take. This planner is trained to make epistemic decisions rather than just generate answers.

Result: On multiple QA benchmarks, the finetuned planner in PassiveQA improves macro F1 scores and recall of abstentions (i.e., correctly recognizing when not to answer), while also lowering hallucination rates compared with standard and enhanced RAG baselines. These gains are achieved under a compute-constrained training setup, suggesting the method is efficient as well as effective.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that reliable epistemic behavior in QA systems—deciding when to answer, clarify, or abstain—cannot simply be enforced at inference time through heuristics; instead, it must be explicitly learned during training. PassiveQA provides an effective training-time solution that improves decision-aware query resolution and reduces hallucinations in LLM-based QA and RAG systems.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have achieved strong performance in question answering and retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), yet they implicitly assume that user queries are fully specified and answerable. In real-world settings, queries are often incomplete, ambiguous, or missing critical variables, leading models to produce overconfident or hallucinated responses.
  In this work, we study decision-aware query resolution under incomplete information, where a model must determine whether to Answer, Ask for clarification, or Abstain. We show that standard and enhanced RAG systems do not reliably exhibit such epistemic awareness, defaulting to answer generation even when information is insufficient.
  To address this, we propose PassiveQA, a three-action framework that aligns model behaviour with information sufficiency through supervised finetuning. Our approach integrates structured information-state representations, knowledge graph-grounded context, and a finetuned planner that explicitly models missing variables and decision reasoning.
  Experiments across multiple QA datasets show that the finetuned planner achieves significant improvements in macro F1 and abstention recall while reducing hallucination rates, under a compute-constrained training regime.
  These results provide strong empirical evidence that epistemic decision-making must be learned during training rather than imposed at inference time.

### [64] [Benchmarking Multilingual Speech Models on Pashto: Zero-Shot ASR, Script Failure, and Cross-Domain Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04598)
*Hanif Rahman*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: First benchmark study of Pashto ASR on public test sets, revealing severe zero-shot failures (especially in script), large cross-domain degradation of fine-tuned systems, and proposing research priorities for robust Pashto recognition.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Pashto, despite tens of millions of speakers, lacks standardized, reproducible benchmarks for automatic speech recognition on shared public datasets. Existing multilingual ASR models are widely used and reported on many languages, but it is unknown how well they handle Pashto, especially in zero-shot settings and across different domains. Moreover, standard metrics like WER may hide critical failures such as producing the wrong script (e.g., Arabic instead of Pashto), and there is little understanding of how Pashto-specific phonological features impact recognition errors. The authors aim to fill this gap by providing the first systematic, multi-model, reproducible evaluation on public Pashto corpora and by diagnosing structural issues that impede cumulative progress in Pashto ASR.

Method: 1) Zero-shot evaluation: Ten multilingual ASR models (all seven OpenAI Whisper sizes, MMS-1B, SeamlessM4T-v2-large, and OmniASR-CTC-300M) are evaluated without Pashto-specific fine-tuning on two public Pashto test sets: FLEURS and a filtered Common Voice 24 subset. Metrics include WER and qualitative analysis of decoding failures (e.g., looping). 2) Script-level failure analysis: A language-identification-based audit is used to measure how often models output Pashto script vs other scripts, showing that WER alone can mask catastrophic failures where the model outputs Arabic script instead of Pashto. 3) Cross-domain evaluation of fine-tuned models: Five Pashto-specific ASR models (fine-tuned on particular corpora) are tested on both FLEURS and Common Voice to measure domain robustness and cross-domain degradation. 4) Error stratification: Character-class error analysis is done to identify which phoneme classes, especially Pashto-unique ones such as retroflex consonants and lateral fricatives, contribute disproportionately to errors. 5) Meta-analysis: Based on these empirical findings, the authors identify structural obstacles in resources, evaluation practices, and modeling for Pashto ASR, and propose prioritized research directions.

Result: 1) Zero-shot performance is very poor for Whisper on Pashto: on FLEURS and Common Voice 24, WERs are extremely high, ranging from 90% to 297%, with Whisper-medium completely collapsing on Common Voice (461% WER due to decoder looping). In contrast, SeamlessM4T-v2-large achieves 39.7% WER on Common Voice 24 (the best zero-shot Pashto result at submission time), while MMS-1B reaches 43.8% WER on FLEURS. 2) Script-fidelity analysis reveals that all Whisper models almost never produce Pashto script (≤0.8% of utterances), whereas MMS-1B, SeamlessM4T, and OmniASR maintain high Pashto-script output (>93%). This shows that WER-level comparisons can be misleading because a model may appear to recognize speech but actually outputs text in the wrong script, which is not a usable form of Pashto ASR. 3) Cross-domain tests of five fine-tuned Pashto models show large degradation when moved to out-of-distribution test sets: WER values that were previously reported around 14% increase to 32.5–59%. One augmented model, however, achieves about 35.1% WER on both FLEURS and Common Voice with essentially no cross-domain degradation, indicating better robustness. 4) Error stratification demonstrates that Pashto-unique phoneme classes, such as retroflexes and lateral fricatives, bear a disproportionate share of recognition errors, highlighting phonological challenges not captured by global WER alone. 5) The study also identifies recurring structural problems (e.g., lack of standardized test sets, script-handling issues, reporting practices that ignore domain shift) that hinder cumulative progress on Pashto ASR.

Conclusion: Multilingual ASR models, particularly Whisper, currently perform poorly and unreliably on Pashto in zero-shot settings, with catastrophic issues such as script mismatch that are invisible to standard WER-based evaluation. Models designed with better script handling (MMS-1B, SeamlessM4T, OmniASR) show much higher script fidelity and somewhat better WER, but performance is still far from satisfactory. Fine-tuned Pashto ASR systems can achieve low WER on their in-domain test data but degrade sharply on out-of-domain sets, revealing significant domain-robustness gaps; only specially augmented models show stable cross-domain performance. Pashto-specific phonological features are a major source of errors, underscoring the need for language-aware modeling. Overall, the paper provides the first reproducible public benchmarks for Pashto ASR, exposes key failure modes and structural impediments, and lays out a prioritized research agenda to build more robust, script-faithful, and cross-domain-capable ASR systems for Pashto.

Abstract: Pashto is spoken by approximately 60--80 million people but has no published benchmarks for multilingual automatic speech recognition (ASR) on any shared public test set. This paper reports the first reproducible multi-model evaluation on public Pashto data, covering zero-shot ASR, script-level failure, and cross-domain evaluation of fine-tuned models. For zero-shot ASR, ten models (all seven Whisper sizes, MMS-1B, SeamlessM4T-v2-large, and OmniASR-CTC-300M) are evaluated on the FLEURS Pashto test set and a filtered Common Voice~24 subset; zero-shot Whisper WER ranges from 90% to 297%, with the medium model collapsing to 461% on Common Voice~24 consistent with decoder looping. SeamlessM4T achieves 39.7% WER on Common Voice~24 (the best zero-shot result reported to date, as of submission); MMS-1B achieves 43.8% on FLEURS. For script failure, a language-identification audit shows that no Whisper model produces Pashto-script output in more than 0.8% of utterances, while MMS-1B, SeamlessM4T, and OmniASR each exceed 93% Pashto-script fidelity; WER alone does not reveal this failure, since a model generating Arabic-script output on Pashto audio has not achieved ASR in any interpretable sense. For cross-domain evaluation, five fine-tuned Pashto ASR models are evaluated on both test sets: published WER figures of 14% degrade to 32.5--59% on out-of-distribution sets, while one augmented model achieves 35.1% on both sets with zero cross-domain degradation. Character-class error stratification confirms that Pashto-unique phonemes (the retroflex series and lateral fricatives) account for disproportionate error mass. All evaluations cover read speech only. Five structural impediments to cumulative progress are identified and five ordered research priorities are argued.

</details>

*Jaeyoon Jung,Yejun Yoon,Kunwoo Park*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces AMuFC, a multimodal fact-checking framework that adaptively decides when to use visual evidence, improving verification accuracy compared to indiscriminate multimodal use.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal fact-checking methods typically assume that adding visual evidence to textual claims always helps, but the authors observe that blindly incorporating images or other visual content can hurt accuracy. There is a need for a principled way to decide when visual evidence is actually useful for verifying a claim, and to evaluate such systems in realistic web scenarios.

Method: The authors design AMuFC, a framework with two collaborating agents: (1) an Analyzer that judges whether visual evidence is necessary or beneficial for verifying a given claim, and (2) a Verifier that predicts the claim’s veracity using textual and visual evidence, conditioned on the Analyzer’s decision. They conduct experiments on three datasets and also construct WebFC, a new dataset tailored for realistic modular evaluation of fact-checking pipelines.

Result: Experiments on three datasets demonstrate that explicitly modeling the necessity of visual evidence and feeding this assessment into the Verifier substantially improves fact-checking performance compared to models that always or naively incorporate visual inputs.

Conclusion: Indiscriminate use of multimodal evidence can degrade fact-checking accuracy, and adaptively deciding when to use visual evidence is crucial. The proposed AMuFC framework, with separate Analyzer and Verifier agents, achieves better verification performance. The new WebFC dataset further supports realistic evaluation of modular fact-checking systems and is publicly released with the code.

Abstract: Automated fact-checking is a crucial task not only in journalism but also across web platforms, where it supports a responsible information ecosystem and mitigates the harms of misinformation. While recent research has progressed from text-only to multimodal fact-checking, a prevailing assumption is that incorporating visual evidence universally improves performance. In this work, we challenge this assumption and show that indiscriminate use of multimodal evidence can reduce accuracy. To address this challenge, we propose AMuFC, a multimodal fact-checking framework that employs two collaborative agents with distinct roles for the adaptive use of visual evidence: An Analyzer determines whether visual evidence is necessary for claim verification, and a Verifier predicts claim veracity conditioned on both the retrieved evidence and the Analyzer's assessment. Experimental results on three datasets show that incorporating the Analyzer's assessment of visual evidence necessity into the Verifier's prediction yields substantial improvements in verification performance. In addition to all code, we release WebFC, a newly constructed dataset for evaluating fact-checking modules in a more realistic scenario, available at https://github.com/ssu-humane/AMuFC.

These idiolectal embeddings are useful for dialect analysis, classification, and as objectives for stylistically aligning language models, providing a foundation for building more diverse, inclusive, and stylistically aware language technologies.

Abstract: Existing sentence representations primarily encode what a sentence says, rather than how it is expressed, even though the latter is important for many applications. We evaluate the approach on dialects of both Arabic and Spanish. The learned representations capture meaningful variation and transfer across domains for analysis and classification. We further explore the use of these representations as training objectives for stylistically aligning language models. ### [68] [What Makes Good Multilingual Reasoning? Disentangling Reasoning Traces with Measurable Features](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04720)
*Dayeon Ki,Kevin Duh,Marine Carpuat*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies what actually makes reasoning effective for Large Reasoning Models across many languages, instead of assuming that English-style reasoning is universally best.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large Reasoning Models perform much better in English than in other languages. Most existing work implicitly assumes the solution is to make all languages reason like English (e.g., via English-style chain-of-thought or alignment). The authors suspect this assumption may be wrong or incomplete and want to rigorously understand what features of reasoning traces correlate with success in different languages, and whether English-derived signals really transfer.

Method: 1) Define a set of measurable reasoning features over model-generated reasoning traces, covering: (a) multilingual alignment properties, (b) local reasoning step characteristics, and (c) global reasoning flow. 2) Use logistic regression to quantify how each feature correlates with final answer correctness in different languages. 3) Train sparse autoencoders on multilingual reasoning traces to discover latent reasoning concepts that realize or extend these hand-designed features. 4) Use these features at test time as selection policies (e.g., choose the sample with better feature scores) to see if they can steer models toward more accurate multilingual reasoning. Experiments span two math reasoning benchmarks, four LRMs, and 10 languages.

Result: Most defined reasoning features correlate positively with answer accuracy overall, but their effect sizes vary greatly by language and sometimes even flip sign: a feature helpful in English can be neutral or harmful in other languages. The sparse autoencoders reveal latent reasoning concepts related to these features, confirming that models internally represent diverse language-specific reasoning patterns. Using feature-based selection policies can improve reasoning in some languages but not uniformly, reinforcing that one-size-fits-all English-centric criteria are unreliable.

Conclusion: English-style reasoning characteristics are not universally optimal for multilingual reasoning in LRMs. While many reasoning features are broadly helpful, their impact is strongly language-dependent and can reverse in some cases. Therefore, reward functions and benchmarks that assume English-derived reasoning patterns as the gold standard are misguided. The paper advocates for adaptive, language-aware objectives and evaluation designs that respect and leverage language-specific reasoning styles when training and assessing multilingual LRMs.

Abstract: Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) still exhibit large performance gaps between English and other languages, yet much current work assumes these gaps can be closed simply by making reasoning in every language resemble English reasoning. This work challenges this assumption by asking instead: what actually characterizes effective reasoning in multilingual settings, and to what extent do English-derived reasoning features genuinely help in other languages? We first define a suite of measurable reasoning features spanning multilingual alignment, reasoning step, and reasoning flow aspects of reasoning traces, and use logistic regression to quantify how each feature associates with final answer accuracy. We further train sparse autoencoders over multilingual traces to automatically discover latent reasoning concepts that instantiate or extend these features. Finally, we use the features as test-time selection policies to examine whether they can steer models toward stronger multilingual reasoning. Across two mathematical reasoning benchmarks, four LRMs, and 10 languages, we find that most features are positively associated with accuracy, but the strength of association varies considerably across languages and can even reverse in some. Our findings challenge English-centric reward designs and point toward adaptive objectives that accommodate language-specific reasoning patterns, with concrete implications for multilingual benchmark and reward design.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are used globally, and because much of their training data is in English, they typically perform best on English inputs. Prior work has largely studied the effects of ESL variation and typographical errors separately, even though they often co-occur in real-world use. In this study, we use the Trans-EnV framework to transform standard English inputs into eight ESL variants and apply MulTypo to inject typos at three levels: low, moderate, and severe. We find that combining ESL variation and typos generally leads to larger performance drops than either factor alone, though the combined effect is not simply additive. This pattern is clearest on closed-ended tasks, where performance degradation can be characterized more consistently across ESL variants and typo levels, while results on open-ended tasks are more mixed. Overall, these findings suggest that evaluations on clean standard English may overestimate real-world model performance, and that evaluating ESL variation and typographical errors in isolation does not fully capture model behavior in realistic settings.

### [70] [Metaphors We Compute By: A Computational Audit of Cultural Translation vs. Thinking in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04732)

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Audit of whether LLMs really perform culture-aware reasoning in creative writing, using metaphor generation across cultures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs can communicate in many languages, it is unclear whether they genuinely reason within different cultural frameworks rather than imposing a dominant one with local surface variations.

Method: Run a computational audit in a creative-writing setting: a metaphor generation task across five cultural settings and multiple abstract concepts, then analyze patterns of metaphors for cultural diversity, stereotypes, and dominance of any single cultural frame.

Result: The model shows stereotyped metaphor usage for some cultural settings and tends to default to Western conceptual framings even when other cultural identities are prompted.

Conclusion: Simply specifying a cultural identity in prompts is insufficient to ensure culturally grounded reasoning; current LLMs behave more like cultural translators tied to a dominant framework than truly culturally inclusive creative partners.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are often described as multilingual because they can understand and respond in many languages. However, speaking a language is not the same as reasoning within a culture. This distinction motivates a critical question: do LLMs truly conduct culture-aware reasoning? This paper presents a preliminary computational audit of cultural inclusivity in a creative writing task. We empirically examine whether LLMs act as culturally diverse creative partners or merely as cultural translators that leverage a dominant conceptual framework with localized expressions. Using a metaphor generation task spanning five cultural settings and several abstract concepts as a case study, we find that the model exhibits stereotyped metaphor usage for certain settings, as well as Western defaultism. These findings suggest that merely prompting an LLM with a cultural identity does not guarantee culturally grounded reasoning.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This study indicates that these dark patterns, often byproducts of safety alignment, may inadvertently narrow creative exploration and proposes design considerations for AI systems that effectively support creative writing.

### [72] [Hallucination Basins: A Dynamic Framework for Understanding and Controlling LLM Hallucinations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04743)
*Kalyan Cherukuri,Lav R. Varshney*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Hallucinations in LLMs can be explained and mitigated using a geometric dynamical-systems view of how hidden states move through latent space.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often generate fluent but incorrect statements, and current explanations of hallucinations are mostly heuristic or empirical. The paper aims to provide a principled, geometric and dynamical explanation of when and why hallucinations occur, and how this depends on the task rather than only the model, to enable more systematic mitigation strategies without retraining.

Method: Model the hidden-state evolution of autoregressive LLMs as trajectories in a latent dynamical system with basins of attraction corresponding to different behaviors (truthful vs hallucinated). Empirically analyze hidden-state trajectories from multiple open-source LLMs across different benchmarks and tasks to study basin separability and overlap. Prove task-complexity and multi-basin theorems that formalize how task properties influence basin formation in L-layer transformers. Propose and test geometry-aware steering interventions on the hidden states to push trajectories toward desirable basins, measuring hallucination rates before and after steering.

Result: Experiments show that the separability of latent basins is highly task-dependent: factoid QA often exhibits clearer and more stable basin separation between correct and incorrect answers, while summarization and tasks rich in misconceptions lead to overlapping or unstable basins that foster hallucinations. The theoretical results characterize conditions under which multiple basins arise in transformers and link them to task complexity. Geometry-aware steering of hidden states reduces hallucination probabilities across examined models and tasks, without requiring model retraining.

Conclusion: Hallucinations in LLMs are not solely a property of the model but emerge from the interaction between model dynamics and task geometry, manifesting as task-dependent basin structures in latent space. This geometric dynamical-systems perspective explains why some tasks are more hallucination-prone and provides a principled route—via geometry-aware steering of hidden states—to mitigate hallucinations without retraining, suggesting a new class of controllability tools for LLM behavior.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) hallucinate: they produce fluent outputs that are factually incorrect. We present a geometric dynamical systems framework in which hallucinations arise from task-dependent basin structure in latent space. Using autoregressive hidden-state trajectories across multiple open-source models and benchmarks, we find that separability is strongly task-dependent rather than universal: factoid settings can show clearer basin separation, whereas summarization and misconception-heavy settings are typically less stable and often overlap. We formalize this behavior with task-complexity and multi-basin theorems, characterize basin emergence in L-layer transformers, and show that geometry-aware steering can reduce hallucination probability without retraining.

Evaluated on a novel Legal Cloze Test benchmark -- a masked legal term prediction task designed for Turkish court decisions -- HukukBERT achieves state-of-the-art performance with 84.40\% Top-1 accuracy, substantially outperforming existing models. Furthermore, we evaluated HukukBERT in the downstream task of structural segmentation of official Turkish court decisions, where it achieves a 92.8\% document pass rate, establishing a new state-of-the-art. We release HukukBERT to support future research in Turkish legal NLP tasks, including recognition of named entities, prediction of judgment, and classification of legal documents.
