*Yuhang Liu,Heyan Huang,Yizhe Yang,Hongyan Zhao,Zhizhuo Zeng,Yang Gao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a stage-wise framework to evaluate how well large language models handle full, end-to-end mathematical modeling tasks, revealing a major gap between understanding problems and correctly executing solutions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard reasoning benchmarks mostly test isolated skills or short problems and do not reliably indicate whether LLMs can solve complex, real-world tasks like mathematical modeling, which require multi-step workflows from problem understanding to final analysis. There is a need for a rigorous, expert-grounded way to measure LLM performance across all stages of such workflows and to understand where they fail.

Method: The authors design a problem-oriented, stage-wise evaluation framework that decomposes mathematical modeling competition problems into key stages (e.g., problem identification, model formulation, solving, coding, analysis). For each stage, they define expert-verified criteria and create automatic scoring methods. They validate the framework by applying it to China Postgraduate Mathematical Contest in Modeling problems and comparing automatically produced scores with independent human expert ratings, and they benchmark various state-of-the-art LLMs under this framework.

Result: The proposed evaluation framework shows substantially higher agreement with human expert judgments than existing evaluation methods. When applied to multiple state-of-the-art LLMs, the framework uncovers that these models perform relatively well on early, comprehension-heavy stages (understanding the problem, formulating models) but perform poorly on execution-heavy stages such as solving models, implementing correct code, and analyzing results. Increasing model size does not eliminate these deficiencies, and it is observed that errors early in execution stages propagate through later stages without being corrected.

Conclusion: LLMs currently have a pronounced comprehension-execution gap on complex mathematical modeling tasks: they can understand and set up problems but struggle with precise, reliable execution and verification of solutions. The validated stage-wise framework offers a more faithful way to assess such capabilities and indicates that simply scaling up models is insufficient; new methods that improve specification, verification, and validation processes are needed for LLMs to be effective at real-world, end-to-end problem solving.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have achieved strong performance on reasoning benchmarks, yet their ability to solve real-world problems requiring end-to-end workflows remains unclear. Mathematical modeling competitions provide a stringent testbed for evaluating such end-to-end problem-solving capability. We propose a problem-oriented, stage-wise evaluation framework that assesses LLM performance across modeling stages using expert-verified criteria. We validate the framework's reliability by comparing automatic scores with independent human expert judgments on problems from the China Postgraduate Mathematical Contest in Modeling, demonstrating substantially stronger alignment than existing evaluation schemes. Using this framework, we reveal a comprehension-execution gap in state-of-the-art LLMs: while they perform well in early stages such as problem identification and formulation, they exhibit persistent deficiencies in execution-oriented stages including model solving, code implementation, and result analysis. These gaps persist even with increased model scale. We further trace these failures to insufficient specification, missing verification, and lack of validation, with errors propagating across stages without correction. Our findings suggest that bridging this gap requires approaches beyond model scaling, offering insights for applying LLMs to complex real-world problem solving.

### [75] [SkillX: Automatically Constructing Skill Knowledge Bases for Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04804)
*Chenxi Wang,Zhuoyun Yu,Xin Xie,Wuguannan Yao,Runnan Fang,Shuofei Qiao,Kexin Cao,Guozhou Zheng,Xiang Qi,Peng Zhang,Shumin Deng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: SkillX is an automated framework that builds a reusable, hierarchical skill knowledge base for LLM agents, improving transfer, efficiency, and success on complex interactive tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM agents learn from limited, isolated experience, causing redundant exploration, repeated rediscovery of similar behaviors, and poor generalization across tasks and environments. There is a need for a systematic way to extract, store, and share learned behaviors (skills) so that different agents and settings can benefit from past experience rather than re-learning from scratch.

Method: SkillX constructs a plug-and-play skill knowledge base via a fully automated pipeline with three core components: (1) Multi-Level Skills Design, which converts raw interaction trajectories into a three-level hierarchy of strategic plans, functional skills, and atomic skills; (2) Iterative Skills Refinement, which uses execution feedback to automatically revise and improve stored skills over time; and (3) Exploratory Skills Expansion, which proactively generates and validates new candidate skills beyond the original training data to broaden coverage. The framework is instantiated with a strong base LLM agent (GLM-4.6) to build the initial skill library.

Result: Using GLM-4.6 to build the skill library, the authors evaluate SkillX on long-horizon, user-interactive benchmarks such as AppWorld, BFCL-v3, and τ^2-Bench. When this skill knowledge base (SkillKB) is plugged into weaker base agents, those agents achieve higher task success rates and greater execution efficiency compared to their baselines without SkillKB, demonstrating effective transfer of skills across agents and environments.

Conclusion: SkillX demonstrates that automatically constructed, hierarchical skill knowledge bases can be reused across different agents and tasks to improve performance and efficiency. The results suggest that representing experience as structured, multi-level skills and continuously refining and expanding these skills is a powerful approach for generalizable LLM agent learning. The work underscores the value of modular, plug-and-play skills for building more capable and data-efficient LLM agents.

Abstract: Learning from experience is critical for building capable large language model (LLM) agents, yet prevailing self-evolving paradigms remain inefficient: agents learn in isolation, repeatedly rediscover similar behaviors from limited experience, resulting in redundant exploration and poor generalization. To address this problem, we propose SkillX, a fully automated framework for constructing a \textbf{plug-and-play skill knowledge base} that can be reused across agents and environments. SkillX operates through a fully automated pipeline built on three synergistic innovations: \textit{(i) Multi-Level Skills Design}, which distills raw trajectories into three-tiered hierarchy of strategic plans, functional skills, and atomic skills; \textit{(ii) Iterative Skills Refinement}, which automatically revises skills based on execution feedback to continuously improve library quality; and \textit{(iii) Exploratory Skills Expansion}, which proactively generates and validates novel skills to expand coverage beyond seed training data. Using a strong backbone agent (GLM-4.6), we automatically build a reusable skill library and evaluate its transferability on challenging long-horizon, user-interactive benchmarks, including AppWorld, BFCL-v3, and $τ^2$-Bench. Experiments show that SkillKB consistently improves task success and execution efficiency when plugged into weaker base agents, highlighting the importance of structured, hierarchical experience representations for generalizable agent learning. Our code will be publicly available soon at https://github.com/zjunlp/SkillX.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors build LiveFact, a live, continuously updated benchmark that constructs dynamic temporal evidence sets for claims, simulating the evolving information environment around emerging news. They design a dual-mode evaluation: (1) Classification Mode, where models make final true/false/unknown judgments; and (2) Inference Mode, where models must perform evidence-based reasoning over partial, time-sliced information. They then run comparative tests across 22 LLMs, including both proprietary systems and open-source Mixture-of-Experts models like Qwen3-235B-A22B.

The evaluation reveals a notable “reasoning gap”: while capable models can demonstrate epistemic humility by labeling early, under-evidenced claims as unverifiable, many models still fail to handle such temporal uncertainty robustly, which is not visible on static benchmarks.

Conclusion: LiveFact provides a scalable, sustainable framework for evaluating LLMs on misinformation detection and fact-checking in realistic, time-evolving settings. By using dynamic evidence, dual-mode evaluation, and contamination monitoring, it shifts the focus from static knowledge recall to robust, temporally aware reasoning and highlights epistemic humility as a key competence that prior benchmarks largely miss.

Abstract: The rapid development of Large Language Models (LLMs) has transformed fake news detection and fact-checking tasks from simple classification to complex reasoning. However, evaluation frameworks have not kept pace. Current benchmarks are static, making them vulnerable to benchmark data contamination (BDC) and ineffective at assessing reasoning under temporal uncertainty. To address this, we introduce LiveFact a continuously updated benchmark that simulates the real-world "fog of war" in misinformation detection. LiveFact uses dynamic, temporal evidence sets to evaluate models on their ability to reason with evolving, incomplete information rather than on memorized knowledge. We propose a dual-mode evaluation: Classification Mode for final verification and Inference Mode for evidence-based reasoning, along with a component to monitor BDC explicitly. Tests with 22 LLMs show that open-source Mixture-of-Experts models, such as Qwen3-235B-A22B, now match or outperform proprietary state-of-the-art systems. More importantly, our analysis finds a significant "reasoning gap." Capable models exhibit epistemic humility by recognizing unverifiable claims in early data slices-an aspect traditional static benchmarks overlook. LiveFact sets a sustainable standard for evaluating robust, temporally aware AI verification.

### [77] [Plausibility as Commonsense Reasoning: Humans Succeed, Large Language Models Do not](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04825)
*Sercan Karakaş*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether large language models use world knowledge and syntax in a human-like way when resolving Turkish relative-clause attachment ambiguities, finding that models do not align with human plausibility-driven preferences.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs perform well on many language tasks, it is unclear if they resolve syntactic ambiguities using world knowledge in a structure-sensitive way like humans. Turkish prenominal relative-clause attachment offers a controlled setting to test whether models use plausibility to guide attachment decisions, as humans do.

Method: The authors use Turkish prenominal relative-clause attachment ambiguities, where a single surface string allows either high or low attachment. They construct items where syntactic structure and pragmatic possibility are held constant but event plausibility selectively favors either High or Low Attachment. These plausibility contrasts are independently normed with human ratings. Human readers are tested in a speeded forced-choice comprehension task to measure how plausibility affects their attachment decisions. Turkish and multilingual LLMs are then evaluated in a parallel preference-based paradigm by comparing mean per-token log-probabilities of matched High vs. Low Attachment continuations.

Result: Humans exhibit a strong plausibility effect: their attachment choices reliably follow the plausibility manipulation, preferring the parse supported by world knowledge. By contrast, across tested Turkish and multilingual LLMs, plausibility-driven shifts in attachment preference are weak, inconsistent, or even opposite in direction, indicating that the models often fail to use plausibility to guide syntactic ambiguity resolution in the tested setting.

Conclusion: The tested LLMs do not integrate plausibility information into relative-clause attachment decisions in a robust, human-like manner for Turkish prenominal RC ambiguities. This suggests a gap between surface-level task performance and deeper, structure-sensitive use of world knowledge in current models. The paper also proposes Turkish RC attachment as a valuable cross-linguistic diagnostic task for probing syntactic ambiguity resolution beyond coarse benchmark scores.

Abstract: Large language models achieve strong performance on many language tasks, yet it remains unclear whether they integrate world knowledge with syntactic structure in a human-like, structure-sensitive way during ambiguity resolution. We test this question in Turkish prenominal relative-clause attachment ambiguities, where the same surface string permits high attachment (HA) or low attachment (LA). We construct ambiguous items that keep the syntactic configuration fixed and ensure both parses remain pragmatically possible, while graded event plausibility selectively favors High Attachment vs.\ Low Attachment. The contrasts are validated with independent norming ratings. In a speeded forced-choice comprehension experiment, humans show a large, correctly directed plausibility effect. We then evaluate Turkish and multilingual LLMs in a parallel preference-based setup that compares matched HA/LA continuations via mean per-token log-probability. Across models, plausibility-driven shifts are weak, unstable, or reversed. The results suggest that, in the tested models, plausibility information does not guide attachment preferences as reliably as it does in human judgments, and they highlight Turkish RC attachment as a useful cross-linguistic diagnostic beyond broad benchmarks.

The work concludes that current LLMs are still not reliably safe for high-stakes therapeutic use and that specialized, psychology-informed red-teaming frameworks are necessary for robust safety alignment in mental healthcare settings.

Abstract: The increasing use of large language models (LLMs) in mental healthcare raises safety concerns in high-stakes therapeutic interactions. A key challenge is distinguishing therapeutic empathy from maladaptive validation, where supportive responses may inadvertently reinforce harmful beliefs or behaviors in multi-turn conversations. This risk is largely overlooked by existing red-teaming frameworks, which focus mainly on generic harms or optimization-based attacks. To address this gap, we introduce Personality-based Client Simulation Attack (PCSA), the first red-teaming framework that simulates clients in psychological counseling through coherent, persona-driven client dialogues to expose vulnerabilities in psychological safety alignment. Experiments on seven general and mental health-specialized LLMs show that PCSA substantially outperforms four competitive baselines. Perplexity analysis and human inspection further indicate that PCSA generates more natural and realistic dialogues. Our results reveal that current LLMs remain vulnerable to domain-specific adversarial tactics, providing unauthorized medical advice, reinforcing delusions, and implicitly encouraging risky actions.

### [80] [Synthetic Sandbox for Training Machine Learning Engineering Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04872)
*Yuhang Zhou,Lizhu Zhang,Yifan Wu,Jiayi Liu,Xiangjun Fan,Zhuokai Zhao,Hong Yan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SandMLE, a framework that creates small but realistic synthetic ML tasks so that large language model agents can be trained with fast, on-policy reinforcement learning for machine learning engineering.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Verifying large language model agents on machine learning engineering tasks is extremely slow and expensive because each reinforcement-learning rollout requires running full ML pipelines on large datasets. This makes standard on-policy RL impractical and pushes researchers toward supervised fine-tuning or offline rewards, which limit exploration and generalization. The authors want a way to keep the benefits of RL while drastically reducing verification cost.

Method: They design SandMLE, a multi-agent system that takes a few real MLE seed tasks and automatically generates many synthetic tasks that preserve realistic structure and technical difficulty but use micro-scale datasets (50–200 samples). These smaller environments dramatically speed up pipeline execution, making trajectory-wise on-policy RL feasible. They then train LLM agents with this setup and evaluate on standardized MLE benchmarks.

Result: Using SandMLE reduces execution time by more than 13x, enabling large-scale on-policy RL in MLE for the first time. On the MLE-bench-lite benchmark, policies trained with SandMLE outperform supervised fine-tuning baselines on multiple Qwen3 models (8B, 14B, 30B-A3B), achieving relative medal rate gains between 20.3% and 66.9%. The learned policy also transfers well to new scaffolds, obtaining up to a 32.4% improvement in HumanRank on MLE-Dojo.

Conclusion: Carefully designed, micro-scale synthetic MLE environments can preserve task complexity while greatly reducing verification cost, making on-policy RL practical for training LLM agents in machine learning engineering. SandMLE not only accelerates training but also produces agents that generalize better than those trained with standard supervised or offline approaches.

Abstract: As large language model agents advance beyond software engineering (SWE) tasks toward machine learning engineering (MLE), verifying agent behavior becomes orders of magnitude more expensive: while SWE tasks can be verified via fast-executing unit tests, MLE verification requires running full ML pipelines -- data preprocessing, model training, and metric evaluation -- on large datasets at each rollout step, rendering trajectory-wise on-policy reinforcement learning (RL) prohibitively slow. Existing approaches retreat to supervised fine-tuning (SFT) or offline proxy rewards, sacrificing the exploration and generalization benefits of on-policy RL. We observe that sandbox data size is the primary source of this bottleneck. Based on this insight, we introduce SandMLE, a multi-agent framework that generates diverse, verifiable synthetic MLE environments from a small number of seed tasks, preserving the structural and technical complexity of real-world problems while constraining datasets to micro-scale (each task is paired with only 50-200 training samples). Through extensive experiments, we show that SandMLE reduces execution time by over 13 times, enabling large-scale, on-policy trajectory-wise RL for the first time in the MLE domain. On MLE-bench-lite, SandMLE yields significant gains over SFT baselines across Qwen3-8B, 14B, and 30B-A3B, with relative medal rate improvements ranging from 20.3% to 66.9%. Furthermore, the trained policy generalizes across unseen agentic scaffolds, achieving up to 32.4% better HumanRank score on MLE-Dojo.

</details>


### [81] [Rethinking Exploration in RLVR: From Entropy Regularization to Refinement via Bidirectional Entropy Modulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04894)
*Hengrui Gu,Xiaotian Han,Yujing Bian,Kaixiong Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They analyze why standard entropy-based exploration in RL for LLMs fails and introduce AsymGRPO, which refines policy entropy by treating positive and negative rollouts asymmetrically to maintain useful diversity while removing noisy behaviors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RL with verifiable rewards improves LLM reasoning but quickly collapses to a small set of solutions (restricted exploration). Standard entropy regularization is fragile and gives small gains. The authors want a principled understanding of how entropy relates to exploration quality and a better way to encourage exploration that preserves genuinely useful diversity in reasoning paths.

Method: They derive a parametric formulation of group-relative advantage estimation and study how it affects policy entropy. From this, they decompose policy entropy into informative entropy (helpful diversity in reasoning paths) and spurious entropy (harmful randomness that breaks reasoning patterns). They show that group-relative methods implicitly perform "entropy refinement": boosting informative entropy on successful trajectories and damping spurious entropy on failed ones. Based on this, they design AsymGRPO, which explicitly and asymmetrically modulates updates from positive vs. negative rollouts, allowing separate control over preserving informative entropy and suppressing spurious entropy.

Result: AsymGRPO improves performance over strong RLVR and entropy-regularized baselines on reasoning tasks for LLMs. It maintains better exploration (more diverse but high-quality solutions) and can be combined with existing entropy regularizers for additional gains. Experiments indicate more stable training and less hyperparameter sensitivity than naive entropy maximization.

Conclusion: Blindly maximizing policy entropy is not an effective exploration strategy for LLMs in RLVR. What matters is refining entropy—maintaining diversity that corresponds to good reasoning while eliminating noise. Group-relative advantage estimation naturally supports this refinement, and AsymGRPO makes it explicit by treating positive and negative rollouts asymmetrically. This leads to stronger, more stable reasoning performance and can complement standard entropy regularization approaches.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) has significantly advanced the reasoning capabilities of large language models (LLMs). However, it faces a fundamental limitation termed \textit{restricted exploration}, where the policy rapidly converges to a narrow set of solutions. While entropy regularization is a popular approach used to sustain exploration, it often proves unreliable for LLMs, suffering from high hyperparameter sensitivity and yielding only marginal performance gains. Motivated by these inefficiencies, we propose to rethink the relationship between policy entropy and exploration. By deriving a parametric formulation of group-relative advantage estimation and analyzing entropy dynamics, we conceptually decompose policy entropy into \textit{informative entropy}, which preserves diverse solution paths, and \textit{spurious entropy}, which erodes reasoning patterns. Our analysis reveals that, in contrast to blind maximization, effective exploration requires \textit{entropy refinement}-a mechanism implicitly embedded in group-relative advantage estimation that sustains informative entropy on positive rollouts while suppressing spurious entropy on negative ones. Guided by this insight, we propose \textbf{AsymGRPO}, an exploratory framework that explicitly decouples the modulation of positive and negative rollouts. This allows for independent control over the preservation of informative entropy and the suppression of spurious noise. Extensive experiments demonstrate that AsymGRPO achieves superior performance compared to strong baselines and exhibits the potential to synergize with existing entropy regularization methods.

</details>


### [82] [TriAttention: Efficient Long Reasoning with Trigonometric KV Compression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04921)
*Weian Mao,Xi Lin,Wei Huang,Yuxin Xie,Tianfu Fu,Bohan Zhuang,Song Han,Yukang Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces TriAttention, a KV cache compression method that uses structural properties of pre-RoPE Q/K vectors to estimate key importance, achieving large KV memory savings and speedups while preserving long-context reasoning accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Extended reasoning with LLMs over long contexts leads to huge KV cache memory usage, which becomes a bottleneck for throughput and deployment on limited hardware like consumer GPUs. Existing KV compression methods depend on attention scores from post-RoPE queries, but RoPE causes queries to rotate with position, so there are very few representative queries. This leads to poor selection of important keys and unstable reasoning, especially for very long contexts. The authors seek a more stable and principled way to estimate key importance for KV cache compression that works well for long-context reasoning.

Method: They examine the pre-RoPE representation space and observe that both queries (Q) and keys (K) are tightly concentrated around fixed, non-zero centers that remain stable across positions (Q/K concentration). They analyze how, under RoPE, these centers imply that queries will prefer keys at particular relative distances, describable via a trigonometric series. Building on this analysis, they propose TriAttention: a key-importance estimation mechanism that uses the learned Q/K centers to infer distance preferences over positions via the trigonometric series. TriAttention scores keys based on their positions according to these distance preferences and uses Q/K norms as an extra signal of importance. These scores drive KV cache compression by preferentially retaining keys deemed important according to this model, instead of relying solely on noisy post-RoPE attention scores.

Result: On AIME25 with 32K-token generation, TriAttention achieves reasoning accuracy comparable to full, uncompressed attention while delivering 2.5x higher throughput or 10.7x reduction in KV memory usage. Competing KV compression baselines obtain only about half the reasoning accuracy at similar efficiency levels. TriAttention also makes it feasible to deploy OpenClaw with long contexts on a single consumer GPU, where full attention would otherwise cause out-of-memory failures.

Conclusion: Q/K vectors in the pre-RoPE space are concentrated around stable, non-zero centers, which induce systematic distance preferences in attention that can be captured via a trigonometric series. Exploiting these structural properties allows TriAttention to estimate key importance more accurately than post-RoPE attention-based methods, enabling aggressive yet robust KV cache compression. This yields substantial speed and memory gains without sacrificing long-context reasoning performance, making large-context LLM deployment on constrained hardware more practical.

Abstract: Extended reasoning in large language models (LLMs) creates severe KV cache memory bottlenecks. Leading KV cache compression methods estimate KV importance using attention scores from recent post-RoPE queries. However, queries rotate with position during RoPE, making representative queries very few, leading to poor top-key selection and unstable reasoning. To avoid this issue, we turn to the pre-RoPE space, where we observe that Q and K vectors are highly concentrated around fixed non-zero centers and remain stable across positions -- Q/K concentration. We show that this concentration causes queries to preferentially attend to keys at specific distances (e.g., nearest keys), with the centers determining which distances are preferred via a trigonometric series. Based on this, we propose TriAttention to estimate key importance by leveraging these centers. Via the trigonometric series, we use the distance preference characterized by these centers to score keys according to their positions, and also leverage Q/K norms as an additional signal for importance estimation. On AIME25 with 32K-token generation, TriAttention matches Full Attention reasoning accuracy while achieving 2.5x higher throughput or 10.7x KV memory reduction, whereas leading baselines achieve only about half the accuracy at the same efficiency. TriAttention enables OpenClaw deployment on a single consumer GPU, where long context would otherwise cause out-of-memory with Full Attention.

</details>


### [83] [Early Stopping for Large Reasoning Models via Confidence Dynamics](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04930)
*Parsa Hosseini,Sumit Nawathe,Mahdi Salmani,Meisam Razaviyayn,Soheil Feizi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose CoDE-Stop, an early-stopping strategy for chain-of-thought reasoning that monitors confidence dynamics of intermediate answers, cutting 25–50% of tokens while preserving accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large reasoning models use long chains of thought, which are computationally expensive and can even hurt accuracy when the model overthinks. Existing systems lack a good mechanism to decide when to stop reasoning, leading to wasted computation and sometimes worse answers. The authors want a principled, training-free way to know when the model is already confident enough and should stop generating more reasoning steps.

Method: They analyze how a model’s confidence in intermediate answers evolves over the course of reasoning traces, comparing correct vs. incorrect trajectories. They find that correct trajectories typically reach a stable, high-confidence answer early, whereas incorrect ones generate longer, unstable, low-confidence reasoning. Based on this, they design CoDE-Stop (Confidence Dynamics Early Stop), which monitors the confidence scores of intermediate answers during chain-of-thought and triggers stopping when certain confidence dynamics criteria are met. The method is plug-and-play, requires no additional training, and is applied during inference to control how long the model continues reasoning.

Result: On multiple reasoning and science benchmarks and across several models, CoDE-Stop achieves a better accuracy–compute tradeoff than previous early-stopping methods. It reduces total token usage by 25–50% compared with always running full-length reasoning, while maintaining or improving accuracy compared to baselines.

Conclusion: Confidence dynamics in intermediate reasoning steps are informative signals for deciding when to stop chain-of-thought generation. By exploiting these dynamics, CoDE-Stop offers a simple, training-free mechanism that integrates easily into existing large reasoning models, substantially cutting computation while preserving performance and providing additional insight into how confidence behaves in correct vs. incorrect reasoning trajectories.

Abstract: Large reasoning models rely on long chain-of-thought generation to solve complex problems, but extended reasoning often incurs substantial computational cost and can even degrade performance due to overthinking. A key challenge is determining when the model should stop reasoning and produce the final answer. In this work, we study the confidence of intermediate answers during reasoning and observe two characteristic behaviors: correct reasoning trajectories often reach high-confidence answers early, while incorrect rollouts tend to produce long, unproductive reasoning traces and exhibit less reliable confidence dynamics. Motivated by these observations, we propose CoDE-Stop (Confidence Dynamics Early Stop), an early stopping method that leverages the dynamics of intermediate answer confidence to decide when to terminate reasoning, requiring no additional training and easily integrating into existing models. We evaluate CoDE-Stop on diverse reasoning and science benchmarks across multiple models. Compared to prior early stopping methods, it achieves a more favorable accuracy-compute tradeoff and reduces total token usage by 25-50% compared to standard full-length reasoning. In addition, we provide analyses of confidence dynamics during reasoning, offering insights into how confidence changes in both correct and incorrect trajectories.

</details>


### [84] [Beyond the Final Actor: Modeling the Dual Roles of Creator and Editor for Fine-Grained LLM-Generated Text Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04932)
*Yang Li,Qiang Sheng,Zhengjia Wang,Yehan Yang,Danding Wang,Juan Cao*

TL;DR: They propose RACE, a method to finely detect different kinds of human/LLM mixed texts using rhetorical analysis, outperforming many baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They define a four-class detection setting (pure human, pure LLM, LLM-polished human, humanized LLM) and introduce RACE, which models the text’s creator and editor separately: it applies Rhetorical Structure Theory to build a logical discourse graph that reflects the creator’s underlying structure, and extracts Elementary Discourse Unit-level features to capture the editor’s stylistic modifications, then uses these for classification.

Result: Across experiments and 12 baseline methods, RACE achieves better performance at recognizing the four fine-grained text origins with lower false alarm rates.

Conclusion: RACE provides a more precise, policy-aligned detector for LLM-generated and human–LLM hybrid texts by leveraging rhetorical and discourse-level cues, supporting more nuanced LLM usage regulation.

Abstract: The misuse of large language models (LLMs) requires precise detection of synthetic text. Existing works mainly follow binary or ternary classification settings, which can only distinguish pure human/LLM text or collaborative text at best. This remains insufficient for the nuanced regulation, as the LLM-polished human text and humanized LLM text often trigger different policy consequences. In this paper, we explore fine-grained LLM-generated text detection under a rigorous four-class setting. To handle such complexities, we propose RACE (Rhetorical Analysis for Creator-Editor Modeling), a fine-grained detection method that characterizes the distinct signatures of creator and editor. Specifically, RACE utilizes Rhetorical Structure Theory to construct a logic graph for the creator's foundation while extracting Elementary Discourse Unit-level features for the editor's style. Experiments show that RACE outperforms 12 baselines in identifying fine-grained types with low false alarms, offering a policy-aligned solution for LLM regulation.

### [86] [IC3-Evolve: Proof-/Witness-Gated Offline LLM-Driven Heuristic Evolution for IC3 Hardware Model Checking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03232)
*Mingkai Miao,Guangyu Hu,Ziyi Yang,Hongce Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: IC3-Evolve uses an LLM-driven offline patching framework to automatically improve IC3/PDR model-checking heuristics while preserving soundness via strict proof- and witness-based validation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: IC3/PDR is a widely used algorithm for hardware safety model checking, but its practical performance heavily depends on many intertwined heuristics and implementation choices. Manually tuning these heuristics is expensive, fragile, and hard to reproduce. There is a need for an automated, reliable way to improve IC3 implementations without sacrificing soundness or adding online ML overhead.

Method: The paper introduces IC3-Evolve, an offline code-evolution framework that leverages a large language model to generate small, slot-restricted, auditable patches to an existing IC3 implementation. Each candidate patch is tested on benchmarks and is only accepted if it passes strict correctness gates: for SAFE results, the checker must output independently checkable inductive invariants (certificates), and for UNSAFE results, it must output replayable counterexample traces. This proof-/witness-gated validation ensures that only sound changes are admitted. The resulting evolved checker is then deployed as a standalone binary with no runtime LLM or ML inference dependency.

Result: Applying IC3-Evolve on the public HWMCC benchmark suite yields evolved IC3 implementations with improved practical performance due to better heuristics, discovered automatically by the LLM under the given constraints. The evolved checkers are then evaluated on both unseen public and industrial benchmarks, demonstrating that the learned heuristic improvements generalize beyond the training set.

Conclusion: IC3-Evolve shows that offline LLM-driven code evolution, combined with strict proof- and witness-based validation, can safely and effectively improve the performance of IC3/PDR hardware model checkers. It achieves practical heuristic gains without introducing runtime ML overhead or compromising soundness, and the approach generalizes to unseen model checking benchmarks.

Abstract: IC3, also known as property-directed reachability (PDR), is a commonly-used algorithm for hardware safety model checking. It checks if a state transition system complies with a given safety property. IC3 either returns UNSAFE (indicating property violation) with a counterexample trace, or SAFE with a checkable inductive invariant as the proof to safety. In practice, the performance of IC3 is dominated by a large web of interacting heuristics and implementation choices, making manual tuning costly, brittle, and hard to reproduce. This paper presents IC3-Evolve, an automated offline code-evolution framework that utilizes an LLM to propose small, slot-restricted and auditable patches to an IC3 implementation. Crucially, every candidate patch is admitted only through proof- /witness-gated validation: SAFE runs must emit a certificate that is independently checked, and UNSAFE runs must emit a replayable counterexample trace, preventing unsound edits from being deployed. Since the LLM is used only offline, the deployed artifact is a standalone evolved checker with zero ML/LLM inference overhead and no runtime model dependency. We evolve on the public hardware model checking competition (HWMCC) benchmark and evaluate the generalizability on unseen public and industrial model checking benchmarks, showing that IC3-Evolve can reliably discover practical heuristic improvements under strict correctness gates.

</details>


### [87] [Structural Segmentation of the Minimum Set Cover Problem: Exploiting Universe Decomposability for Metaheuristic Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03234)
*Isidora Hernández,Héctor Ferrada,Cristóbal A. Navarro*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They speed up and improve Minimum Set Cover solving by splitting the universe into independent parts before running a GRASP heuristic, then recombining the results.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most existing algorithms for the Minimum Set Cover Problem (MSCP) treat each instance as a single, undivided problem. However, many real-world instances may have internal structure, such as elements that never interact across certain groups. Ignoring such structure can waste computation and miss opportunities for parallel or decomposed solving. The authors want to see if exploiting this latent structure—specifically, segmenting the universe of elements into independent components—can systematically improve heuristic performance.

Method: They introduce the notion of universe segmentability: decomposing the universe of elements into independent components based on how elements co-occur in subsets. Using a disjoint-set union (union–find) data structure, they perform a preprocessing step that identifies connected components induced by co-occurrence relations between elements. Each connected component defines an independent MSCP subproblem. They then solve each subproblem separately using a GRASP (Greedy Randomized Adaptive Search Procedure) metaheuristic. Solutions to the subproblems are finally merged, which is straightforward because the components are independent. To support efficiency on large instances, they also use a compact bit-level representation for sets to speed up set operations.

Result: On both standard benchmarks and large synthetic datasets, the segmented approach outperforms the baseline GRASP that does not exploit segmentation. The improvements are most pronounced for large instances that decompose into multiple components, where solution quality is better and running time scales more favorably. The bit-level representation further reduces computational overhead, making the whole approach efficient in practice.

Conclusion: Exploiting universe segmentability in MSCP via a union–find based preprocessing step is an effective way to improve heuristic optimization. Decomposing the original instance into independent subproblems, solving each with GRASP, and recombining the partial solutions yields better solution quality and scalability, particularly for structurally decomposable and large instances. The approach is computationally practical thanks to succinct bit-level set representations, and it shows that structural analysis of instances can be a powerful complement to traditional heuristic design.

Abstract: The Minimum Set Cover Problem (MSCP) is a classical NP-hard combinatorial optimization problem with numerous applications in science and engineering. Although a wide range of exact, approximate, and metaheuristic approaches have been proposed, most methods implicitly treat MSCP instances as monolithic, overlooking potential intrinsic structural properties of the universe. In this work, we investigate the concept of \emph{universe segmentability} in the MSCP and analyze how intrinsic structural decomposition (universe segmentability) can be exploited to enhance heuristic optimization. We propose an efficient preprocessing strategy based on disjoint-set union (union--find) to detect connected components induced by element co-occurrence within subsets, enabling the decomposition of the original instance into independent subproblems. Each subproblem is solved using the GRASP metaheuristic, and partial solutions are combined without compromising feasibility. Extensive experiments on standard benchmark instances and large-scale synthetic datasets show that exploiting natural universe segmentation consistently improves solution quality and scalability, particularly for large and structurally decomposable instances. These gains are supported by a succinct bit-level set representation that enables efficient set operations, making the proposed approach computationally practical at scale.

</details>

We substantiate this position by dissecting current validity failures and revisiting evaluation paradigms across computer science and psychometrics. Most critically, there is a 31-point decline in the Faith and Spirituality dimension. ### [91] [VERT: Reliable LLM Judges for Radiology Report Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03376)
*Federica Bologna,Jean-Philippe Corbeil,Matthew Wilkens,Asma Ben Abacha*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates and improves LLM-based automatic metrics (LLM judges) for radiology report quality across diverse imaging modalities and anatomies, proposing a new metric VERT and a fine-tuned Qwen3-30B judge that better match radiologist ratings while being faster.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work mostly builds LLM-based metrics and small fine-tuned models for chest X-ray report evaluation, but it is unknown if they generalize to other modalities/anatomies or which models/prompts work best as LLM judges. A robust, scalable, and efficient automatic evaluation metric aligned with expert radiologists is needed for broader clinical NLP and medical imaging applications.

Method: The authors conduct correlation analyses between radiologist (expert) ratings and several LLM-based metrics (RadFact, GREEN, FineRadScore, and their new metric VERT) using multiple open- and closed-source LLMs (with and without reasoning capabilities, different sizes) on two multi-modality, multi-anatomy datasets (RadEval and RaTE-Eval). They also study few-shot prompting, ensembling, and parameter-efficient fine-tuning (on Qwen3 30B with ~1,300 RaTE-Eval samples) and perform a systematic error detection and categorization study to understand where the metrics agree or disagree with experts.

Result: VERT achieves up to 11.7% higher correlation with radiologist judgments than the previous best metric GREEN. Fine-tuning Qwen3 30B with only ~1,300 samples yields up to 25% improvement in alignment with experts and speeds up inference by up to 37.2x compared to baseline configurations. The error analysis reveals which error types are better or worse captured by each metric and how closely they track expert judgments.

Conclusion: LLM-based judges can provide reliable automatic evaluation of radiology reports across diverse imaging settings, especially when carefully designed (VERT) and lightly adapted via parameter-efficient fine-tuning (e.g., Qwen3 30B). Such lightweight adaptations significantly improve correlation with radiologists while reducing computational cost, supporting practical deployment of robust, generalizable evaluation tools for radiology NLP systems.

Abstract: Current literature on radiology report evaluation has focused primarily on designing LLM-based metrics and fine-tuning small models for chest X-rays. However, it remains unclear whether these approaches are robust when applied to reports from other modalities and anatomies. Which model and prompt configurations are best suited to serve as LLM judges for radiology evaluation? We conduct a thorough correlation analysis between expert and LLM-based ratings. We compare three existing LLM-as-a-judge metrics (RadFact, GREEN, and FineRadScore) alongside VERT, our proposed LLM-based metric, using open- and closed-source models (reasoning and non-reasoning) of different sizes across two expert-annotated datasets, RadEval and RaTE-Eval, spanning multiple modalities and anatomies. We further evaluate few-shot approaches, ensembling, and parameter-efficient fine-tuning using RaTE-Eval. To better understand metric behavior, we perform a systematic error detection and categorization study to assess alignment of these metrics against expert judgments and identify areas of lower and higher agreement. Our results show that VERT improves correlation with radiologist judgments by up to 11.7% relative to GREEN. Furthermore, fine-tuning Qwen3 30B yield gains of up to 25% using only 1,300 training samples. The fine-tuned model also reduces inference time up to 37.2 times. These findings highlight the effectiveness of LLM-based judges and demonstrate that reliable evaluation can be achieved with lightweight adaptation.

### [92] [Hume's Representational Conditions for Causal Judgment: What Bayesian Formalization Abstracted Away](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03387)
*Yiling Wu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that Hume’s theory of causal judgment relies on three specific representational conditions, shows that modern Bayesian and predictive-processing frameworks dropped these conditions while retaining Hume’s updating structure, and uses large language models as a counterexample to highlight what was lost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To clarify what is distinctive in Hume’s psychology of causation beyond mere probabilistic updating, and to show how later formal epistemologies and cognitive models have quietly abandoned those extra assumptions. By making these background conditions explicit, the paper aims to illuminate both Hume’s original theory and the conceptual gaps in contemporary accounts of inference, particularly in light of large language models.

Method: Conceptual and textual analysis of Hume’s writings to extract three representational conditions (experiential grounding, structured retrieval, vivacity transfer); then a comparative, theoretical examination of how these conditions fare within Bayesian epistemology and predictive processing; finally, an application of this framework to large language models as a case study of systems that implement statistical updating without Humean representational constraints.

Result: The analysis identifies three necessary conditions that Hume implicitly assumes for causal judgment and shows that modern formal frameworks retain only the updating aspect while discarding experiential grounding, structured associational structures, and phenomenological vivacity. Large language models are shown to fit these modern frameworks in terms of updating but to lack Hume’s representational features, thereby exposing a divergence between Humean causal psychology and contemporary computational models.

Conclusion: Hume’s account of causal judgment is richer than its modern descendants in Bayesian and predictive-processing theories: it depends on specific representational and phenomenological conditions that are now typically ignored. Recognizing that large language models implement updating without these conditions reveals that Hume’s assumptions are not conceptually necessary for probabilistic inference, and it invites a reevaluation of what is required for genuinely Humean causal cognition versus mere statistical prediction.

Abstract: Hume's account of causal judgment presupposes three representational conditions: experiential grounding (ideas must trace to impressions), structured retrieval (association must operate through organized networks exceeding pairwise connection), and vivacity transfer (inference must produce felt conviction, not merely updated probability). This paper extracts these conditions from Hume's texts and argues that they are integral to his causal psychology. It then traces their fate through the formalization trajectory from Hume to Bayesian epistemology and predictive processing, showing that later frameworks preserve the updating structure of Hume's insight while abstracting away these further representational conditions. Large language models serve as an illustrative contemporary case: they exhibit a form of statistical updating without satisfying the three conditions, thereby making visible requirements that were previously background assumptions in Hume's framework.

### [93] [TABQAWORLD: Optimizing Multimodal Reasoning for Multi-Turn Table Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03393)
*Tung Sum Thomas Kwok,Xinyu Wang,Xiaofeng Lin,Peng Lu,Chunhe Wang,Changlun Li,Hanwei Wu,Nan Tang,Elisa Kreiss,Guang Cheng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes TABQAWORLD, a training-free multimodal table reasoning framework that adaptively chooses visual or textual table representations and optimizes reasoning trajectories using table metadata, achieving higher accuracy and lower latency than prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-turn table reasoning models rely on fixed text serialization of tables, leading to compounding representation errors over multiple turns. While tabular grounding can alleviate this, it is computationally expensive and impractical for real-world deployment. There is a need for a more reliable yet efficient table reasoning framework that can maintain high accuracy without large inference costs.

Method: TABQAWORLD jointly optimizes tabular reasoning via two components: representation and estimation. For representation, it uses an action-conditioned multimodal selection policy that dynamically switches between visual and textual table views to maximize reliability of table state readouts at each reasoning step. For estimation, it leverages table metadata—such as dimensions, data types, and key values—to plan safe, stepwise reasoning trajectories, compress low-complexity actions, and reduce the number of conversation turns and overall latency. The framework is designed to be training-free, wrapping around existing models and tools.

Result: Empirical evaluations show that TABQAWORLD achieves state-of-the-art performance on table reasoning tasks. It improves accuracy by 4.87% over strong baselines, and specifically yields a 5.42% accuracy gain and 33.35% reduction in inference latency compared to static (non-adaptive) settings.

Conclusion: TABQAWORLD establishes a new standard for reliable and efficient table reasoning by dynamically choosing between visual and textual table representations and optimizing reasoning trajectories using metadata, all in a training-free framework that improves both accuracy and latency over existing methods.

Abstract: Multimodal reasoning has emerged as a powerful framework for enhancing reasoning capabilities of reasoning models. While multi-turn table reasoning methods have improved reasoning accuracy through tool use and reward modeling, they rely on fixed text serialization for table state readouts. This introduces representation errors in table encoding that significantly accumulate over multiple turns. Such accumulation is alleviated by tabular grounding methods in the expense of inference compute and cost, rendering real world deployment impractical. To address this, we introduce TABQAWORLD, a table reasoning framework that jointly optimizes tabular action through representation and estimation. For representation, TABQAWORLD employs an action-conditioned multimodal selection policy, which dynamically switches between visual and textual representations to maximize table state readout reliability. For estimation, TABQAWORLD optimizes stepwise reasoning trajectory through table metadata including dimension, data types and key values, safely planning trajectory and compressing low-complexity actions to reduce conversation turns and latency. Designed as a training-free framework, empirical evaluations show that TABQAWORLD achieves state-of-the-art performance with 4.87% accuracy improvements over baselines, with 5.42% accuracy gain and 33.35% inference latency reduction over static settings, establishing a new standard for reliable and efficient table reasoning.

### [94] [Contextual Control without Memory Growth in a Context-Switching Task](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03479)
*Song-Ju Kim*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a new recurrent neural architecture where different contexts act by adding interventions to a shared latent state instead of giving context as input or increasing hidden size, and show it can handle context-dependent sequential decision tasks under partial observability as well as larger-memory baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing approaches for context-dependent sequential decision making either feed context directly into the model or enlarge recurrent memory so that the network can internally represent contextual variations. Both strategies can be inefficient or undesirable: direct context access may not be realistic or available, and increasing recurrent dimensionality raises computational cost and parameter count. The authors want an alternative way to realize contextual dependence that keeps the recurrent state size fixed while still allowing flexible context-specific behavior.

Method: They design an intervention-based recurrent architecture where a recurrent core first computes a shared pre-intervention latent state independent of context. Contextual information then modifies this state via an additive, context-indexed operator (an intervention) rather than by changing the dimensionality of the recurrent state. They test this on a context-switching sequential decision task under partial observability. They compare three model families: (1) a label-assisted baseline that has direct access to context labels, (2) a memory baseline with a larger recurrent state to encode context implicitly, and (3) the proposed intervention model, which does not feed context into the recurrent core and keeps recurrent dimensionality fixed. They also analyze models using conditional mutual information I(C; O | S) between context C and outcomes O given a fixed latent state S, treating this as an operational probe of how context influences behavior at fixed state.

Result: On the main context-switching benchmark, the intervention model achieves strong performance that is competitive with baselines, despite not increasing recurrent state dimensionality or giving direct context input to the recurrent core. The conditional mutual information analysis shows that for phase-1 outcomes that matter for the task, the intervention model has positive conditional contextual information, indicating that context still meaningfully influences behavior at a fixed latent state through the intervention mechanism.

Conclusion: Intervening additively on a shared recurrent latent state offers a practical and effective alternative to enlarging recurrent memory for implementing contextual control in sequential decision problems. The proposed intervention-based architecture can express context-dependent policies under partial observability without growing recurrent dimensionality, and information-theoretic probing suggests that this mechanism successfully realizes contextual dependence at fixed latent state.

Abstract: Context-dependent sequential decision making is commonly addressed either by providing context explicitly as an input or by increasing recurrent memory so that contextual information can be represented internally. We study a third alternative: realizing contextual dependence by intervening on a shared recurrent latent state, without enlarging recurrent dimensionality. To this end, we introduce an intervention-based recurrent architecture in which a recurrent core first constructs a shared pre-intervention latent state, and context then acts through an additive, context-indexed operator. We evaluate this idea on a context-switching sequential decision task under partial observability. We compare three model families: a label-assisted baseline with direct context access, a memory baseline with enlarged recurrent state, and the proposed intervention model, which uses no direct context input to the recurrent core and no memory growth. On the main benchmark, the intervention model performs strongly without additional recurrent dimensions. We also evaluate the models using the conditional mutual information (I(C;O | S)) as a theorem-motivated operational probe of contextual dependence at fixed latent state. For task-relevant phase-1 outcomes, the intervention model exhibits positive conditional contextual information. Together, these results suggest that intervention on a shared recurrent state provides a viable alternative to recurrent memory growth for contextual control in this setting.

While LoRA improved recall in DistilGPT2, overall transformer-based and generative models underperformed. These findings suggest interpretable, resource-efficient models may outperform compact LLMs in real-world, imbalanced clinical prediction tasks.

Method: The authors build ActionNex, an agentic system that: (1) ingests multimodal operational inputs (outage tickets, telemetry, chats) and compresses them into "critical events" capturing key state transitions; (2) uses a hierarchical memory architecture with long-term Key-Condition-Action knowledge from playbooks and history, episodic memory of past outages, and working memory of the current incident; (3) runs a reasoning agent that matches current critical events to KCA preconditions, retrieves relevant memories, and generates role- and stage-specific next-best action recommendations; and (4) uses executed human actions as implicit feedback for continual self-improvement in a human-in-the-loop loop.

Conclusion: ActionNex demonstrates that an agentic system with multimodal perception and hierarchical memory can provide accurate, production-viable next-action recommendations for cloud outage management, effectively augmenting human operators and improving outage response workflows.

Abstract: Outage management in large-scale cloud operations remains heavily manual, requiring rapid triage, cross-team coordination, and experience-driven decisions under partial observability. We present \textbf{ActionNex}, a production-grade agentic system that supports end-to-end outage assistance, including real-time updates, knowledge distillation, and role- and stage-conditioned next-best action recommendations. ActionNex ingests multimodal operational signals (e.g., outage content, telemetry, and human communications) and compresses them into critical events that represent meaningful state transitions. It couples this perception layer with a hierarchical memory subsystem: long-term Key-Condition-Action (KCA) knowledge distilled from playbooks and historical executions, episodic memory of prior outages, and working memory of the live context. A reasoning agent aligns current critical events to preconditions, retrieves relevant memories, and generates actionable recommendations; executed human actions serve as an implicit feedback signal to enable continual self-evolution in a human-agent hybrid system. We evaluate ActionNex on eight real Azure outages (8M tokens, 4,000 critical events) using two complementary ground-truth action sets, achieving 71.4\% precision and 52.8-54.8\% recall. The system has been piloted in production and has received positive early feedback.

### [99] [Structural Rigidity and the 57-Token Predictive Window: A Physical Framework for Inference-Layer Governability in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03524)
*Gregory M. Ruddell*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes when and how language models show internal early-warning signals (“pre-commitment”) before generating unsafe or incorrect outputs, and finds such signals are rare, model- and setup-specific, and absent for hallucinations, motivating external verification for factual reliability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI safety often assumes we can monitor a model’s internals or behavior to catch rule-breaking or harmful outputs before they are emitted, but there is little empirical evidence on whether models actually exhibit such advance warning signals in their inference dynamics. The authors want a principled, measurable way to connect transformer computations to constraint-satisfaction behavior so that we can reason about when internal monitoring can govern model behavior and when it cannot.

Method: They build an energy-based governance framework that interprets transformer inference as a constraint-satisfaction process with an associated “energy” geometry. Within this, they define trajectory tension, rho = ||a|| / ||v||, as a measure of resistance in the model’s activation dynamics as it moves toward a token choice. They then study seven instruction-tuned models across five geometric regimes of inference behavior (Authority Band, Late Signal, Inverted, Flat, Scaffold-Selective) on arithmetic constraint probes and hallucination tasks, measuring energy asymmetry between aligned and misaligned trajectories as a marker of structural rigidity and potential pre-commitment signals.

Result: They find a clear pre-commitment window of about 57 tokens only in one specific setting: Phi-3-mini-4k-instruct under greedy decoding on arithmetic constraint probes. This pre-commitment signal is highly contingent on model, task, and configuration. In the seven-model cohort, only this single configuration shows predictive internal signals before the model commits to an answer; other configurations instead show either no signal, signals arising too late, inverted signals, or flat geometries. For factual hallucination across 72 test conditions, no predictive internal signal is detected, in line with the idea that hallucinations correspond to settling into spurious attractors without strong world-model constraints.

Conclusion: Internal-geometry-based monitoring can sometimes detect upcoming rule violations when the model’s internal dynamics exhibit resistance (energy asymmetry), but this is not a general property of instruction-tuned models and does not extend to factual hallucinations. Rule violations and hallucinations are therefore distinct failure modes with different detection strategies: rule-breaking may be partially governed through internal energy monitoring where structural rigidity exists, while detecting hallucinations requires external, world-referenced verification rather than relying on internal pre-commitment signals. The proposed taxonomy and energy-based framework offer tools for assessing how governable a model’s inference process is and for evaluating deployment risks of autonomous AI systems.

Abstract: Current AI safety relies on behavioral monitoring and post-training alignment, yet empirical measurement shows these approaches produce no detectable pre-commitment signal in a majority of instruction-tuned models tested. We present an energy-based governance framework connecting transformer inference dynamics to constraint-satisfaction models of neural computation, and apply it to a seven-model cohort across five geometric regimes.
  Using trajectory tension (rho = ||a|| / ||v||), we identify a 57-token pre-commitment window in Phi-3-mini-4k-instruct under greedy decoding on arithmetic constraint probes. This result is model-specific, task-specific, and configuration-specific, demonstrating that pre-commitment signals can exist but are not universal.
  We introduce a five-regime taxonomy of inference behavior: Authority Band, Late Signal, Inverted, Flat, and Scaffold-Selective. Energy asymmetry (Σ\r{ho}_misaligned / Σ\r{ho}_aligned) serves as a unifying metric of structural rigidity across these regimes.
  Across seven models, only one configuration exhibits a predictive signal prior to commitment; all others show silent failure, late detection, inverted dynamics, or flat geometry.
  We further demonstrate that factual hallucination produces no predictive signal across 72 test conditions, consistent with spurious attractor settling in the absence of a trained world-model constraint.
  These results establish that rule violation and hallucination are distinct failure modes with different detection requirements. Internal geometry monitoring is effective only where resistance exists; detection of factual confabulation requires external verification mechanisms.
  This work provides a measurable framework for inference-layer governability and introduces a taxonomy for evaluating deployment risk in autonomous AI systems.

Without clear rationale, developers cannot distinguish between intelligent efficiency -- using specialized models for appropriate tasks -- and latent failures caused by budget-driven model selection. We present Topaz, a framework that introduces formal auditability to agentic routing. Topaz replaces silent model assignments with an inherently interpretable router that incorporates three components: (i) skill-based profiling that synthesizes performance across diverse benchmarks into granular capability profiles (ii) fully traceable routing algorithms that utilize budget-based and multi-objective optimization to produce clear traces of how skill-match scores were weighed against costs, and (iii) developer-facing explanations that translate these traces into natural language, allowing users to audit system logic and iteratively tune the cost-quality tradeoff. The authors aim to bridge this gap and lower the barrier for historians to use AI on primary sources.

Method: The paper presents the first module of Chronos, an AI Historian. This module uses a vision-language model (VLM) at its core but wraps it in an interactive, natural-language interface that lets historians: (1) upload image scans of primary sources; (2) specify extraction tasks and workflows in natural language instead of code; (3) customize and adapt workflows to different and heterogeneous corpora; (4) evaluate model performance on specific tasks and sources; and (5) iteratively refine prompts and workflows through conversational interaction with the Chronos agent. The system is implemented as open-source software intended to run on users’ own historical corpora.

Result: They deliver an operational, open-source module of Chronos that can be directly used by historical researchers. The module supports natural-language–driven extraction of data from image scans, workflow customization, and task-specific evaluation of the underlying AI models. While detailed quantitative benchmarks are not given in the abstract, the main result is a working system that demonstrates that flexible, conversational workflows around a VLM are feasible for real historical materials.

Conclusion: An AI Historian can be practically realized as a modular, open-source tool centered on natural-language interaction rather than rigid pipelines. By enabling historians to control, adapt, and evaluate AI-powered extraction from primary-source images, Chronos lowers the barrier to AI adoption in historical research and provides an extensible foundation for further AI-supported methods in the humanities.

Abstract: AI is supporting, accelerating, and automating scientific discovery across a diverse set of fields. However, AI adoption in historical research remains limited due to the lack of solutions designed for historians. In this technical progress report, we introduce the first module of Chronos, an AI Historian under development. This module enables historians to convert image scans of primary sources into data through natural-language interactions. Rather than imposing a fixed extraction pipeline powered by a vision-language model (VLM), it allows historians to adapt workflows for heterogeneous source corpora, evaluate the performance of AI models on specific tasks, and iteratively refine workflows through natural-language interaction with the Chronos agent. The module is open-source and ready to be used by historical researchers on their own sources.

### [103] [When Do Hallucinations Arise? A Graph Perspective on the Evolution of Path Reuse and Path Compression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03557)
*Xinnan Dai,Kai Yang,Cheng Luo,Shenglai Zeng,Kai Guo,Jiliang Tang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a graph-search view of next-token prediction in decoder-only LLMs to explain why they produce reasoning hallucinations, identifying two core mechanisms—Path Reuse and Path Compression—that jointly account for many observed hallucination behaviors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reasoning hallucinations—fluent but unsupported or logically inconsistent conclusions—are common in LLMs, yet we lack mechanistic explanations of how the Transformer architecture produces them. Existing work often treats hallucinations as black-box failures or focuses on surface-level fixes (prompting, decoding tricks) rather than understanding the internal computation. The authors aim to provide a principled, mechanistic account of hallucinations by reinterpreting next-token prediction as graph search over an implicit knowledge graph learned by the model.

Method: The authors introduce a conceptual/modeling framework where an LLM’s internal knowledge is represented as a large directed graph whose nodes are entities or abstract states and whose edges correspond to learned transition probabilities between them. Next-token prediction is then cast as a search process over this graph: with context, the model searches a constrained sampled subgraph (intrinsic reasoning); without strong context, it relies on global, memorized graph structure (extrinsic reasoning). Within this framework, they analyze training dynamics and path usage to identify two generic mechanisms—Path Reuse and Path Compression—that explain how hallucinated reasoning chains emerge and stabilize. They likely support this with controlled experiments or toy tasks that track transitions, path frequencies, and shortcut formation over training.

Result: They find evidence that: (1) Early in training, the model tends to reuse high-probability, memorized transition paths even when they conflict with contextual constraints (Path Reuse), causing context-ignoring hallucinations; and (2) as training progresses, multi-step reasoning chains that are frequently used become compressed into single-step, high-probability shortcut transitions (Path Compression), which can skip necessary intermediate checks and produce overconfident but incorrect conclusions. These phenomena appear across both context-rich and context-poor settings and align with several known hallucination patterns in practical LLM applications.

Conclusion: By viewing LLM inference as graph search and studying its training-induced transition dynamics, the paper argues that two generic mechanisms—Path Reuse and Path Compression—offer a unified mechanistic explanation for reasoning hallucinations. This account links hallucinations to the way models store and traverse knowledge, explaining why they can systematically override context or shortcut multi-step reasoning. The framework suggests that mitigating hallucinations may require modifying training objectives, architectures, or search procedures to better respect contextual constraints and preserve intermediate reasoning steps rather than only tuning prompts or decoding strategies.

Abstract: Reasoning hallucinations in large language models (LLMs) often appear as fluent yet unsupported conclusions that violate either the given context or underlying factual knowledge. Although such failures are widely observed, the mechanisms by which decoder-only Transformers produce them remain poorly understood. We model next-token prediction as a graph search process over an underlying graph, where entities correspond to nodes and learned transitions form edges. From this perspective, contextual reasoning is a constrained search over a sampled subgraph (intrinsic reasoning), while context-free queries rely on memorized structures in the underlying graph (extrinsic reasoning). We show that reasoning hallucinations arise from two fundamental mechanisms: \textbf{Path Reuse}, where memorized knowledge overrides contextual constraints during early training, and \textbf{Path Compression}, where frequently traversed multi-step paths collapse into shortcut edges in later training. Together, these mechanisms provide a unified explanation for reasoning hallucinations in LLMs and connected to well-known behaviors observed in downstream applications.

### [104] [When Adaptive Rewards Hurt: Causal Probing and the Switching-Stability Dilemma in LLM-Guided LEO Satellite Scheduling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03562)
*Yuanhang Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how adaptive reward design affects DRL performance in multi-beam LEO satellite scheduling and finds that dynamically changing reward weights often hurts performance because PPO needs a stable reward signal for value convergence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In LEO satellite scheduling with multiple beams, the traffic regime changes over time, so it is natural to believe that dynamically adjusting reward weights to be regime-aware would outperform using fixed weights. However, it is unclear if this intuition holds for modern DRL algorithms like PPO in realistic communication-system settings, and how LLMs might help design such adaptive rewards.

Method: The authors systematically compare static versus dynamically adapted reward weights for PPO-based DRL in a multi-beam LEO scheduling task. They test several MDP architectures for reward-weight adaptation: fixed weights, rule-based adaptation, a learned MLP adapter, and a fine-tuned LLM adapter. They also introduce a single-variable causal probing technique, in which each reward term is independently perturbed by ±20%, and they measure PPO performance after 50k training steps to infer causal leverage of each weight.

Result: Contrary to intuition, near-constant reward weights achieve much higher throughput (around 342.1 Mbps) than carefully tuned dynamic weights (about 103.3±96.8 Mbps). Any form of weight adaptation repeatedly disrupts PPO value-function convergence, leading to a switching-stability dilemma. Causal probing uncovers non-obvious sensitivities: e.g., increasing the switching penalty by 20% boosts throughput by +157 Mbps in polar handover and +130 Mbps in hot-cold traffic regimes. Among adaptation architectures, the MLP-based method performs best on both known (357.9 Mbps) and novel (325.2 Mbps) regimes, while the fine-tuned LLM collapses to 45.3±43.0 Mbps due to oscillatory weight outputs rather than poor domain knowledge.

Conclusion: PPO in this satellite scheduling domain requires quasi-stationary reward signals; frequent changes to reward weights—even if well intentioned—can severely degrade learning stability and performance. Simple or learned but stable adapters (like MLPs) can outperform more expressive but inconsistent LLM-based adapters. LLMs are most valuable for interpreting natural-language intent and generating initial reward structures, but they should not directly drive rapidly changing reward weights. The work offers practical guidance on integrating LLMs with DRL: preserve reward stationarity for PPO, use causal probing to understand reward-term leverage, and rely on LLMs where their language capabilities are uniquely beneficial rather than for high-frequency control of learning signals.

Abstract: Adaptive reward design for deep reinforcement learning (DRL) in multi-beam LEO satellite scheduling is motivated by the intuition that regime-aware reward weights should outperform static ones. We systematically test this intuition and uncover a switching-stability dilemma: near-constant reward weights (342.1 Mbps) outperform carefully-tuned dynamic weights (103.3+/-96.8 Mbps) because PPO requires a quasistationary reward signal for value function convergence. Weight adaptation-regardless of quality-degrades performance by repeatedly restarting convergence. To understand why specific weights matter, we introduce a single-variable causal probing method that independently perturbs each reward term by +/-20% and measures PPO response after 50k steps. Probing reveals counterintuitive leverage: a +20% increase in the switching penalty yields +157 Mbps for polar handover and +130 Mbps for hot-cold regimes-findings inaccessible to human experts or trained MLPs without systematic probing. We evaluate four MDP architect variants (fixed, rule-based, learned MLP, finetuned LLM) across known and novel traffic regimes. The MLP achieves 357.9 Mbps on known regimes and 325.2 Mbps on novel regimes, while the fine-tuned LLM collapses to 45.3+/-43.0 Mbps due to weight oscillation rather than lack of domain knowledge-output consistency, not knowledge, is the binding constraint. Our findings provide an empirically-grounded roadmap for LLM-DRL integration in communication systems, identifying where LLMs add irreplaceable value (natural language intent understanding) versus where simpler methods suffice.
