### [105] [Personality Requires Struggle: Three Regimes of the Baldwin Effect in Neuroevolved Chess Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03565)
*Diego Armando Resendez Prado*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether lifetime learning (Hebbian plasticity) can increase, rather than decrease, behavioral diversity over evolutionary time, using chess-playing neural agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical theory suggests that phenotypic plasticity buffers individuals from environmental noise and thus reduces behavioral variance. The authors want to test if, in a competitive and cognitively rich domain, lifetime learning can instead drive long-term diversification in behavior and personality-like traits.

Method: They evolve chess agents using NEAT with eight neural modules, augmented by within-game Hebbian learning (plasticity) and an imagination-based signal chain in a desirability domain. They compare conditions with Hebbian plasticity ON vs OFF across 10 evolutionary seeds each, measuring cross-seed behavioral variance over generations and analyzing move choices, opening repertoires, piece-use preferences, and game lengths. They also examine different opponent regimes (heterogeneous population, elitist selection, self-play) to see how competitive structure affects diversity.

Result: Initially, agents with Hebbian plasticity show lower behavioral variance across seeds than non-plastic agents, confirming prior buffering predictions. However, around generation 34, a monotonic variance crossover occurs: the Hebbian condition surpasses the non-Hebbian one in cross-seed variance (ρ = 0.91, p < 10^-6). Plasticity begins to amplify evolved perceptual differences via imagination, leading to structured behavioral divergence: 62% disagreement on moves in identical positions, distinct openings, piece preferences, and game length patterns, all with high within-agent reproducibility (ICC > 0.8) and interpretable internal configurations. They identify three regimes: exploration (Hebbian ON with heterogeneous opponents), lottery (Hebbian OFF with elitism lock-in), and transparent (self-play against same-model opponents, causing a collapse of diversity).

Conclusion: Contrary to traditional expectations, lifetime Hebbian learning can ultimately expand behavioral diversity over evolutionary time, after an initial compression phase. The interaction between plasticity and evolved perceptual architectures creates stable, personality-like behavioral signatures rather than mere stochastic variability. Competitive structure matters: heterogeneous opponents promote exploration and diversity, whereas self-play against identical models may systematically suppress behavioral diversity, suggesting a testable prediction about current self-play-based AI systems.

Abstract: Can lifetime learning expand behavioral diversity over evolutionary time, rather than collapsing it? Prior theory predicts that plasticity reduces variance by buffering organisms against environmental noise. We test this in a competitive domain: chess agents with eight NEAT-evolved neural modules, Hebbian within-game plasticity, and a desirability-domain signal chain with imagination. Across 10~seeds per Hebbian condition, a variance crossover emerges: Hebbian ON starts with lower cross-seed variance than OFF, then surpasses it at generation~34. The crossover trend is monotonic (\r{ho} = 0.91, p < 10^{-6): plasticity's effect on behavioral variance reverses over evolutionary time, initially compressing diversity (consistent with prior predictions) then expanding it as evolved Perception differences are amplified through imagination -- a feedback loop that mutation alone cannot sustain.
  The result is structured behavioral divergence: evolved agents select different moves on the same positions (62\% disagreement), develop distinct opening repertoires, piece preferences, and game lengths. These are not different sampling policies -- they are reproducible behavioral signatures (ICC > 0.8) with interpretable signal chain configurations. Three regimes appear depending on opponent type: exploration (Hebbian ON, heterogeneous opponent), lottery (Hebbian OFF, elitism lock-in), and transparent (same-model opponent, brain self-erasure). The transparent regime generates a falsifiable prediction: self-play systems may systematically suppress behavioral diversity by eliminating the heterogeneity that personality requires.
  \textbf{Keywords: Baldwin Effect, neuroevolution, NEAT, Hebbian learning, chess, cognitive architecture, personality emergence, imagination

*Tuan Le,Wei Qian,Mengdi Huai*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a selective unlearning framework for Large Reasoning Models that removes sensitive parts of chains-of-thought while preserving overall reasoning ability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large Reasoning Models expose detailed chains-of-thought, which can leak memorized sensitive or copyrighted information. Existing machine unlearning approaches mostly target final answers, not intermediate reasoning, and often damage the model’s general reasoning ability if applied naively to entire CoTs. There is a need for precise unlearning that focuses on sensitive reasoning fragments while keeping the rest of the reasoning process intact.

Method: They design an LRM unlearning framework that operates at the level of chain-of-thought traces. Multiple LLMs with retrieval-augmented generation are used to analyze CoTs and locate segments related to knowledge that should be forgotten. These segments are then replaced by benign placeholder reasoning that preserves the logical flow. Training incorporates a feature replacement unlearning loss that simultaneously decreases the likelihood of regenerating the sensitive content and increases the likelihood of generating the structurally coherent replacements instead.

Result: Experiments on synthetic and medical datasets show that the framework successfully removes or suppresses targeted sensitive reasoning components while maintaining, to a large extent, the overall reasoning performance of the LRMs. The method yields better retention of general reasoning capability compared with baselines that unlearn at the answer level or on full CoTs.

Conclusion: Selective, structure-aware unlearning at the chain-of-thought level can mitigate knowledge leakage risks in Large Reasoning Models without substantially compromising their reasoning power. Using RAG-based analysis and a feature replacement loss enables precise targeting of sensitive content while preserving logical structure, demonstrating a promising direction for practical machine unlearning in reasoning-centric models.

Abstract: Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) generate structured chains of thought (CoTs) before producing final answers, making them especially vulnerable to knowledge leakage through intermediate reasoning steps. Yet, the memorization of sensitive information in the training data such as copyrighted and private content has led to ethical and legal concerns. To address these issues, selective forgetting (also known as machine unlearning) has emerged as a potential remedy for LRMs. However, existing unlearning methods primarily target final answers and may degrade the overall reasoning ability of LRMs after forgetting. Additionally, directly applying unlearning on the entire CoTs could degrade the general reasoning capabilities. The key challenge for LRM unlearning lies in achieving precise unlearning of targeted knowledge while preserving the integrity of general reasoning capabilities. To bridge this gap, we in this paper propose a novel LRM unlearning framework that selectively removes sensitive reasoning components while preserving general reasoning capabilities. Our approach leverages multiple LLMs with retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) to analyze CoT traces, identify forget-relevant segments, and replace them with benign placeholders that maintain logical structure. We also introduce a new feature replacement unlearning loss for LRMs, which can simultaneously suppress the probability of generating forgotten content while reinforcing structurally valid replacements. Extensive experiments on both synthetic and medical datasets verify the desired properties of our proposed method.

### [107] [Rashomon Memory: Towards Argumentation-Driven Retrieval for Multi-Perspective Agent Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03588)
*Albert Sadowski,Jarosław A. Chudziak*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Rashomon Memory, a multi-perspective memory architecture where different goal-oriented agents maintain their own interpretations of experiences and resolve them via argumentation at query time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI memory systems typically enforce a single canonical representation of experiences, which is inadequate when long-lived agents must simultaneously pursue multiple goals that assign conflicting meanings to the same events. The authors want a memory system that can preserve and reason over these conflicting interpretations instead of prematurely collapsing them.

Method: They design Rashomon Memory, in which multiple parallel, goal-conditioned agents each maintain their own ontology and knowledge graph, encoding experiences according to their priorities. At retrieval time, these perspectives engage in structured argumentation: each proposes interpretations and critiques those of others. Using Dung’s abstract argumentation semantics, the system determines which interpretations are accepted, rejected, or remain in conflict, forming an attack graph that documents the reasoning process.

Result: A proof-of-concept implementation demonstrates that different retrieval behaviors—such as choosing a single best interpretation, composing multiple compatible ones, or explicitly surfacing unresolved conflicts—emerge naturally from the topology of the attack graph produced by the argumentation process.

Conclusion: Maintaining distinct, goal-conditioned perspectives in memory and resolving them through argumentation provides not only more flexible retrieval but also built-in explanations. The attack graph doubles as an interpretable record of which interpretations were chosen, which were considered, and why some were rejected, and it allows systems to expose genuine disagreements instead of forcing artificial consensus.

Abstract: AI agents operating over extended time horizons accumulate experiences that serve multiple concurrent goals, and must often maintain conflicting interpretations of the same events. A concession during a client negotiation encodes as a ``trust-building investment'' for one strategic goal and a ``contractual liability'' for another. Current memory architectures assume a single correct encoding, or at best support multiple views over unified storage. We propose Rashomon Memory: an architecture where parallel goal-conditioned agents encode experiences according to their priorities and negotiate at query time through argumentation. Each perspective maintains its own ontology and knowledge graph. At retrieval, perspectives propose interpretations, critique each other's proposals using asymmetric domain knowledge, and Dung's argumentation semantics determines which proposals survive. The resulting attack graph is itself an explanation: it records which interpretation was selected, which alternatives were considered, and on what grounds they were rejected. We present a proof-of-concept showing that retrieval modes (selection, composition, conflict surfacing) emerge from attack graph topology, and that the conflict surfacing mode, where the system reports genuine disagreement rather than forcing resolution, lets decision-makers see the underlying interpretive conflict directly.

Specifically, how entropy evolves during decoding, how attention is distributed across layers, and how hidden representations contribute to uncertainty, logical inconsistencies, and misinformation propagation are often overlooked. Consequently, this study introduces a trace-level analysis of entropy and attention dynamics in SLMs evaluated with the TruthfulQA dataset. Four models with parameter ranges of 1B-1.7B parameters were examined via token-level output entropy, attention entropy, head dispersion, and hidden-state representation. ### [111] [Beyond Retrieval: Modeling Confidence Decay and Deterministic Agentic Platforms in Generative Engine Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03656)

In this paper, we systematically deconstruct the probabilistic flaws of existing RAG-based GEO and propose a paradigm shift towards deterministic multi-agent intent routing. First, we mathematically formulate Semantic Entropy Drift (SED) to model the dynamic decay of confidence curves in LLMs over continuous temporal and contextual perturbations. ### [112] [TableVision: A Large-Scale Benchmark for Spatially Grounded Reasoning over Complex Hierarchical Tables](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03660)
*Xiaoyu Chen,Lu Dai,Hanqing Wang,Zhuoyu Li,Wenbin Dai,Yanzong Zheng,Zhenggang Xia,Junyong Lin,Hui Xiong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces TableVision, a benchmark and framework to study and improve how MLLMs perceive and reason over complex, hierarchical tables, showing that explicit spatial grounding markedly boosts reasoning accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: MLLMs are increasingly used for tasks involving structured documents, but their reasoning on complex, hierarchically laid out tables is weak. The authors observe that as table tasks become more complex, models must attend to many discrete visual regions, which overwhelms their perceptual capacity and degrades spatial attention. There is a lack of systematic benchmarks and analyses that isolate this perception bottleneck and quantify how it affects multi-step reasoning over tables, motivating a new dataset and evaluation framework that tightly couples spatial perception with logical reasoning.

Method: The authors first perform quantitative analysis to identify a Perception Bottleneck: they measure how task complexity correlates with the number and density of visual regions that models must process, revealing a “Perceptual Overload.” They then propose TableVision, a large-scale benchmark focused on spatially grounded reasoning over tables. TableVision organizes tasks into three cognitive levels—Perception, Reasoning, and Analysis—spread over 13 sub-categories. Using a rendering-based, deterministic grounding pipeline, they generate pixel-perfect spatial annotations and multi-step reasoning trajectories, yielding 6,799 high-fidelity reasoning chains. Finally, they design and test a two-stage, decoupled framework that separates explicit spatial grounding from higher-level reasoning, imposing spatial constraints during inference.

Result: Empirical experiments and diagnostic probing show that when models are given explicit spatial constraints derived from the TableVision grounding pipeline, their reasoning performance on complex table tasks improves substantially. The proposed two-stage decoupled framework that leverages this separation between perception and reasoning achieves a 12.3% absolute accuracy gain on the TableVision test set. Analyses confirm that the main gains stem from better spatial attention and reduced errors caused by perceptual overload rather than changes in the underlying language reasoning ability.

Conclusion: The work concludes that MLLMs’ poor performance on complex tables is largely due to a perception bottleneck caused by dense, discrete visual regions, leading to perceptual overload. By explicitly separating and constraining spatial perception through a trajectory-aware, spatially grounded benchmark (TableVision) and a two-stage framework, models can recover much of their latent reasoning capability. TableVision thus serves both as a rigorous testbed and as evidence that tighter coupling between accurate spatial grounding and logical reasoning is crucial for advancing document and table understanding in MLLMs.

Abstract: Structured tables are essential for conveying high-density information in professional domains such as finance, healthcare, and scientific research. Despite the progress in Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs), reasoning performance remains limited for complex tables with hierarchical layouts. In this paper, we identify a critical Perception Bottleneck through quantitative analysis. We find that as task complexity scales, the number of involved discrete visual regions increases disproportionately. This processing density leads to an internal "Perceptual Overload," where MLLMs struggle to maintain accurate spatial attention during implicit generation. To address this bottleneck, we introduce TableVision, a large-scale, trajectory-aware benchmark designed for spatially grounded reasoning. TableVision stratifies tabular tasks into three cognitive levels (Perception, Reasoning, and Analysis) across 13 sub-categories. By utilizing a rendering-based deterministic grounding pipeline, the dataset explicitly couples multi-step logical deductions with pixel-perfect spatial ground truths, comprising 6,799 high-fidelity reasoning trajectories. Our empirical results, supported by diagnostic probing, demonstrate that explicit spatial constraints significantly recover the reasoning potential of MLLMs. Furthermore, our two-stage decoupled framework achieves a robust 12.3% overall accuracy improvement on the test set. TableVision provides a rigorous testbed and a fresh perspective on the synergy between perception and logic in document understanding.

### [113] [PRAISE: Prefix-Based Rollout Reuse in Agentic Search Training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03675)
*Erhan Zhang,Yiqun Chen,Zechun Niu,Wei Yang,Xiaochi Wei,Yan Gao,Yi Wu,Yao Hu,Jiaxin Mao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PRAISE is a framework to make reinforcement learning for agentic search with LLMs more data-efficient and better at assigning credit across multi-turn search steps, by reusing prefixes of trajectories and generating intermediate step rewards.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agentic search with LLMs requires multi-turn retrieval and reasoning, but existing search-based RL methods are inefficient and struggle with sparse rewards because they only use final-answer supervision and do not fully leverage long-horizon rollouts. This makes training expensive and credit assignment across steps difficult.

Method: PRAISE takes full search trajectories produced by an LLM-based agent and extracts prefix states corresponding to different turns. From each prefix, it elicits intermediate answers. These prefixes are reused to create additional training trajectories, and step-level rewards are computed from performance differences between answers from shorter vs. longer prefixes. A single shared LLM is used both as the search policy and as the evaluator of prefix answers, enabling joint optimization without extra human labels or a separate reward model.

Result: On multi-hop question answering benchmarks, PRAISE yields consistent performance gains compared with strong existing baselines for agentic search and search-based RL, demonstrating improved data efficiency and better training signal distribution across steps.

Conclusion: By reusing trajectory prefixes and introducing intermediate step rewards derived from prefix performance, PRAISE mitigates reward sparsity and improves data efficiency in RL training for agentic search with LLMs. A unified model for both search and evaluation allows effective, annotation-free joint optimization, leading to better multi-hop QA performance.

Abstract: In agentic search, large language models (LLMs) are trained to perform multi-turn retrieval and reasoning for complex tasks such as multi-hop question answering (QA). However, current search-based Reinforcement Learning (RL) methods suffer from two core limitations: expensive long-horizon rollouts are under-utilized during training, and supervision is typically available only at the final answer, resulting in severe reward sparsity. We present Prefix-based Rollout reuse for Agentic search with Intermediate Step rEwards (PRAISE), a framework for improving both data efficiency and credit assignment in agentic search training. Given a complete search trajectory, PRAISE extracts prefix states at different search turns, elicits intermediate answers from them, and uses these prefixes both to construct additional training trajectories and to derive step-level rewards from performance differences across prefixes. Our method uses a single shared model for both search policy learning and prefix answer evaluation, enabling joint optimization without extra human annotations or a separate reward model. Experiments on multi-hop QA benchmarks show that PRAISE consistently improves performance over strong baselines.

</details>


### [114] [Structured Multi-Criteria Evaluation of Large Language Models with Fuzzy Analytic Hierarchy Process and DualJudge](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03742)
*Yulong He,Ivan Smirnov,Dmitry Fedrushkov,Sergey Kovalchuk,Ilya Revin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose and test an Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP)-based and confidence-aware Fuzzy AHP (FAHP) framework for evaluating LLMs, showing it is more reliable and calibrated than direct scoring, and introduce DualJudge, which combines direct and structured evaluations for state-of-the-art performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Direct, point-based LLM evaluations are inconsistent, opaque, and struggle with uncertainty, making it difficult to reliably compare models. There is a need for principled, interpretable, and uncertainty-aware evaluation methods tailored to LLM-as-judge scenarios.

Method: They adapt the Analytic Hierarchy Process to LLM evaluation by decomposing judgments into explicit criteria and using pairwise comparisons scored by LLMs. They then extend this to a confidence-aware Fuzzy AHP that represents each comparison as a triangular fuzzy number whose spread is modulated by LLM self-reported confidence, capturing epistemic uncertainty. They validate these methods on the JudgeBench benchmark and finally propose DualJudge, which fuses holistic direct scores and AHP/FAHP outputs using consistency-aware weighting inspired by Dual-Process Theory.

Result: On JudgeBench, both the standard (crisp) AHP and the proposed FAHP yield more calibrated and consistent judgments than direct scoring across different model scales and dataset splits. FAHP is particularly more stable when comparisons are uncertain. The DualJudge hybrid framework achieves state-of-the-art accuracy in LLM-as-judge evaluation tasks, indicating that combining intuitive direct judgments with structured, deliberative reasoning improves reliability.

Conclusion: Structured, uncertainty-aware evaluation using AHP and especially confidence-modulated FAHP leads to more reliable and interpretable LLM assessments than simple direct scoring. Moreover, adaptively combining intuitive and deliberative evaluation (DualJudge) yields state-of-the-art performance, suggesting that uncertainty-aware structured reasoning is a promising direction for robust LLM evaluation frameworks.

Abstract: Effective evaluation of large language models (LLMs) remains a critical bottleneck, as conventional direct scoring often yields inconsistent and opaque judgments. In this work, we adapt the Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP) to LLM-based evaluation and, more importantly, propose a confidence-aware Fuzzy AHP (FAHP) extension that models epistemic uncertainty via triangular fuzzy numbers modulated by LLM-generated confidence scores. Systematically validated on JudgeBench, our structured approach decomposes assessments into explicit criteria and incorporates uncertainty-aware aggregation, producing more calibrated judgments. Extensive experiments demonstrate that both crisp and fuzzy AHP consistently outperform direct scoring across model scales and dataset splits, with FAHP showing superior stability in uncertain comparison scenarios. Building on these insights, we propose \textbf{DualJudge}, a hybrid framework inspired by Dual-Process Theory that adaptively fuses holistic direct scores with structured AHP outputs via consistency-aware weighting. DualJudge achieves state-of-the-art performance, underscoring the complementary strengths of intuitive and deliberative evaluation paradigms. These results establish uncertainty-aware structured reasoning as a principled pathway toward more reliable LLM assessment. Code is available at https://github.com/hreyulog/AHP_llm_judge.

### [115] [RL-Driven Sustainable Land-Use Allocation for the Lake Malawi Basin](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.03768)
*Ying Yao*

TL;DR: They use deep reinforcement learning to optimize land-use allocation in the Lake Malawi Basin to maximize ecosystem service value, incorporating spatial patterns and policy scenarios.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Unsustainable land use in ecologically sensitive regions harms biodiversity, water, and livelihoods, and planners need a quantitative tool to design land allocations that better preserve ecosystem services while accommodating development.

Method: They build a 50x50 cell RL environment from Sentinel-2 land-cover data, assign biome-specific ecosystem service value coefficients via benefit transfer, and train a Proximal Policy Optimization agent with action masking and a reward that combines per-cell ecological value with spatial coherence (contiguity bonuses and buffer penalties near water). They test three scenarios: pure ESV maximization, ESV plus spatial reward shaping, and a regenerative agriculture policy setting.

Result: The PPO agent reliably increases total ecosystem service value, spatial reward shaping leads to more ecologically desirable patterns like clustered land uses and more forest near water, and the framework’s outputs change systematically under different policy parameterizations.

Abstract: Unsustainable land-use practices in ecologically sensitive regions threaten biodiversity, water resources, and the livelihoods of millions. This paper presents a deep reinforcement learning (RL) framework for optimizing land-use allocation in the Lake Malawi Basin to maximize total ecosystem service value (ESV). Drawing on the benefit transfer methodology of Costanza et al., we assign biome-specific ESV coefficients -- locally anchored to a Malawi wetland valuation -- to nine land-cover classes derived from Sentinel-2 imagery. The RL environment models a 50x50 cell grid at 500m resolution, where a Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) agent with action masking iteratively transfers land-use pixels between modifiable classes. The reward function combines per-cell ecological value with spatial coherence objectives: contiguity bonuses for ecologically connected land-use patches (forest, cropland, built area etc.) and buffer zone penalties for high-impact development adjacent to water bodies. We evaluate the framework across three scenarios: (i) pure ESV maximization, (ii) ESV with spatial reward shaping, and (iii) a regenerative agriculture policy scenario. Results demonstrate that the agent effectively learns to increase total ESV; that spatial reward shaping successfully steers allocations toward ecologically sound patterns, including homogeneous land-use clustering and slight forest consolidation near water bodies; and that the framework responds meaningfully to policy parameter changes, establishing its utility as a scenario-analysis tool for environmental planning.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing uses of LLMs in qualitative research often behave like black boxes: they aggregate over large bodies of text and do not clearly expose how specific analytic conclusions are reached. This limits transparency, makes it hard to compare LLM judgments to human judgments, and undermines methodological rigor in LLM-assisted qualitative research. The authors are motivated to design tooling that makes the analytic process more traceable and auditable.

Method: The authors develop QualAnalyzer, an open-source Chrome extension for Google Workspace that enforces atomistic analysis by sending each data segment (e.g., essay, transcript excerpt) to an LLM separately, and automatically storing the corresponding prompt, input, and output for every unit. They then conduct two case studies—holistic essay scoring and deductive thematic coding of interview transcripts—to demonstrate how segment-level logging creates an auditable record of the full analysis pipeline and supports systematic comparison between LLM and human annotations.

Result: In the two case studies, the atomistic workflow implemented by QualAnalyzer allows the authors to generate a clear, auditable trail for each analysis decision and to systematically examine where and how LLM judgments diverge from human judgments. The extension successfully integrates with Google Workspace and operationalizes process-level transparency in practical qualitative analysis workflows.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that process auditability—implemented through tools like QualAnalyzer that preserve prompts, inputs, and outputs at the level of individual data segments—is crucial to improving transparency and methodological robustness in LLM-assisted qualitative research. Atomistic, well-logged workflows make it easier for researchers to inspect, validate, and critique the role of LLMs in the analytic process.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly used for qualitative data analysis, but many workflows obscure how analytic conclusions are produced. We present QualAnalyzer, an open-source Chrome extension for Google Workspace that supports atomistic LLM analysis by processing each data segment independently and preserving the prompt, input, and output for every unit. Through two case studies -- holistic essay scoring and deductive thematic coding of interview transcripts -- we show that this approach creates a legible audit trail and helps researchers investigate systematic differences between LLM and human judgments. We argue that process auditability is essential for making LLM-assisted qualitative research more transparent and methodologically robust.

Method: They build FeynmanBench, a large benchmark focused on Feynman diagram tasks. They show systematic failures such as inconsistent enforcement of conservation laws, incorrect handling of symmetry constraints, and violations of global graph-topological conditions in diagrams. Conclusion: FeynmanBench is a new, physics-grounded benchmark that probes whether AI systems can engage in rigorous, multi-step diagrammatic reasoning central to theoretical physics. Current MLLMs fall short on these tasks, underscoring the need for improved architectures, training, or reasoning strategies that better capture global physical constraints and structured logic in scientific notation.

Abstract: Breakthroughs in frontier theory often depend on the combination of concrete diagrammatic notations with rigorous logic. While multimodal large language models (MLLMs) show promise in general scientific tasks, current benchmarks often focus on local information extraction rather than the global structural logic inherent in formal scientific notations. In this work, we introduce FeynmanBench, the first benchmark centered on Feynman diagram tasks. It is designed to evaluate AI's capacity for multistep diagrammatic reasoning, which requires satisfying conservation laws and symmetry constraints, identifying graph topology, converting between diagrammatic and algebraic representations, and constructing scattering amplitudes under specific conventions and gauges. To support large-scale and reproducible evaluation, we developed an automated pipeline producing diverse Feynman diagrams along with verifiable topological annotations and amplitude results. Our database spans the electromagnetic, weak, and strong interactions of the Standard Model, encompasses over 100 distinct types and includes more than 2000 tasks. Experiments on state-of-the-art MLLMs reveal systematic failure modes, including unstable enforcement of physical constraints and violations of global topological conditions, highlighting the need for physics-grounded benchmarks for visual reasoning over scientific notation. FeynmanBench provides a logically rigorous test of whether AI can effectively engage in scientific discovery, particularly within theoretical physics.

Unlike other LLM Agent Swarm frameworks, which treat the simulations as a prediction black box, discourse_sim treats it as a theory-testing instrument, which is fundamentally a different epistemological stance for studying social science problems. The paper further demonstrates the framework by modelling the Dublin anti-immigration march on April 26, 2025, with N=100 agents over a 15-day simulation.
  Package link: https://pypi.org/project/discourse-sim/

These risks are fundamentally product-level and cannot be eliminated by technical safeguards alone because agent behavior is inherently stochastic. To address this gap between model-level reliability and user-facing assurance, we propose a complementary framework based on risk management. Drawing inspiration from financial underwriting, we introduce the \textbf{Agentic Risk Standard (ARS)}, a payment settlement standard for AI-mediated transactions. ARS integrates risk assessment, underwriting, and compensation into a single transaction framework that protects users when interacting with agents. Under ARS, users receive predefined and contractually enforceable compensation in cases of execution failure, misalignment, or unintended outcomes. This shifts trust from an implicit expectation about model behavior to an explicit, measurable, and enforceable product guarantee. <details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a system that grounds reviews in verifiable evidence, including reproducibility checks, to better support human reviewers under growing peer-review workload.

Method: FactReview operates in several stages: (1) automatically extracts key claims and reported results from a submission; (2) retrieves and analyzes nearby literature to clarify the paper’s technical contribution and positioning; (3) when code is available, executes the released repository within bounded computational budgets to empirically test central claims; and (4) synthesizes a concise review plus an evidence report that assigns each major claim one of five labels (Supported, Supported by the paper, Partially supported, In conflict, Inconclusive) based on gathered evidence.

Result: In a detailed case study on the CompGCN model, FactReview successfully reproduces link prediction and node classification results close to those reported in the paper but fails to confirm the broader claim of consistently strong performance across tasks: on the MUTAG graph-classification task, the reproduced accuracy is 88.4% compared to the reported best baseline of 92.6%, leading to a downgraded assessment of that general claim as only partially supported.

Conclusion: FactReview demonstrates that AI systems can substantively aid peer review by collecting and organizing concrete evidence about a paper’s claims, rather than by making final accept/reject decisions. The case study indicates that such tools can both verify many reported results and highlight overgeneralized or weakly supported claims, thereby enabling more evidence-grounded assessments by human reviewers. The authors release their implementation publicly for further use and extension.

Abstract: Peer review in machine learning is under growing pressure from rising submission volume and limited reviewer time. Most LLM-based reviewing systems read only the manuscript and generate comments from the paper's own narrative. This makes their outputs sensitive to presentation quality and leaves them weak when the evidence needed for review lies in related work or released code. We present FactReview, an evidence-grounded reviewing system that combines claim extraction, literature positioning, and execution-based claim verification. Given a submission, FactReview identifies major claims and reported results, retrieves nearby work to clarify the paper's technical position, and, when code is available, executes the released repository under bounded budgets to test central empirical claims. It then produces a concise review and an evidence report that assigns each major claim one of five labels: Supported, Supported by the paper, Partially supported, In conflict, or Inconclusive. In a case study on CompGCN, FactReview reproduces results that closely match those reported for link prediction and node classification, yet also shows that the paper's broader performance claim across tasks is not fully sustained: on MUTAG graph classification, the reproduced result is 88.4%, whereas the strongest baseline reported in the paper remains 92.6%. The claim is therefore only partially supported. More broadly, this case suggests that AI is most useful in peer review not as a final decision-maker, but as a tool for gathering evidence and helping reviewers produce more evidence-grounded assessments. The code is public at https://github.com/DEFENSE-SEU/Review-Assistant.

Conclusion: By combining large language models for intent understanding with a semantically guided diffusion transformer for trajectory generation, InsTraj can produce high-fidelity, diverse GPS trajectories that closely follow natural language instructions, addressing key limitations of prior approaches in controllability and semantic alignment.

However, existing methods face a two-fold challenge: they lack the deep semantic understanding to interpret complex user travel intent, and struggle to handle complex constraints while maintaining the realistic diversity inherent in human behavior. To resolve this, we introduce InsTraj, a novel framework that instructs diffusion models to generate high-fidelity trajectories directly from natural language descriptions. Specifically, InsTraj first utilizes a powerful large language model to decipher unstructured travel intentions formed in natural language, thereby creating rich semantic blueprints and bridging the representation gap between intentions and trajectories. Subsequently, we proposed a multimodal trajectory diffusion transformer that can integrate semantic guidance to generate high-fidelity and instruction-faithful trajectories that adhere to fine-grained user intent. Comprehensive experiments on real-world datasets demonstrate that InsTraj significantly outperforms state-of-the-art methods in generating trajectories that are realistic, diverse, and semantically faithful to the input instructions.

### [123] [Profile-Then-Reason: Bounded Semantic Complexity for Tool-Augmented Language Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04131)
*Paulo Akira F. Enabe*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes PTR, a structured, low-latency framework for tool-using LLM agents that profiles a workflow once, executes it with verification and bounded repair, and reduces repeated reasoning compared to reactive ReAct-style agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reactive tool-using LLM agents repeatedly recompute reasoning after each observation. This increases latency, cost, and vulnerability to cascading errors. There is a need for a more efficient, structured execution framework that reduces repeated calls to the LLM while maintaining or improving reliability on complex tool-augmented tasks.

Method: Introduce the Profile--Then--Reason (PTR) execution framework. PTR first asks the LLM to synthesize an explicit workflow (the profile). Deterministic or guarded operators then execute this workflow step by step. A verifier evaluates the resulting execution trace. If verification indicates that the original workflow is no longer reliable, a repair procedure is invoked to adjust it; otherwise, the initial plan is reused. The entire pipeline is formalized mathematically as a composition of profile, routing, execution, verification, repair, and reasoning operators. The framework enforces bounded repair, constraining LLM calls to at most two in the nominal case and three in the worst case. PTR is empirically evaluated against a ReAct baseline across six benchmarks and four LLMs.

Result: Under bounded repair, PTR limits the number of LLM calls to two in the typical case and three at worst, reducing recomputation compared to reactive agents. Across six benchmarks and four language models, PTR outperforms the ReAct baseline in 16 of 24 pairwise exact-match comparisons. Its gains are largest on tasks that rely heavily on information retrieval and problem decomposition, while it is less advantageous or inferior on tasks requiring strong online adaptation.

Conclusion: PTR offers a more structured, efficient alternative to fully reactive tool-augmented LLM agents by separating upfront workflow profiling from execution and selectively invoking repair. It reduces LLM call counts and often improves accuracy, particularly for retrieval-centric and decomposition-heavy problems. However, reactive execution strategies remain superior when tasks demand substantial on-the-fly adaptation, suggesting that the best approach is task-dependent rather than universally PTR or reactive.

Abstract: Large language model agents that use external tools are often implemented through reactive execution, in which reasoning is repeatedly recomputed after each observation, increasing latency and sensitivity to error propagation. This work introduces Profile--Then--Reason (PTR), a bounded execution framework for structured tool-augmented reasoning, in which a language model first synthesizes an explicit workflow, deterministic or guarded operators execute that workflow, a verifier evaluates the resulting trace, and repair is invoked only when the original workflow is no longer reliable. A mathematical formulation is developed in which the full pipeline is expressed as a composition of profile, routing, execution, verification, repair, and reasoning operators; under bounded repair, the number of language-model calls is restricted to two in the nominal case and three in the worst case. Experiments against a ReAct baseline on six benchmarks and four language models show that PTR achieves the pairwise exact-match advantage in 16 of 24 configurations. The results indicate that PTR is particularly effective on retrieval-centered and decomposition-heavy tasks, whereas reactive execution remains preferable when success depends on substantial online adaptation.

There is also a need to better capture spatial dependencies among geographically distributed PV stations. The paper aims to address these limitations.

Method: The authors propose Solar-VLM, a multimodal framework driven by a large language model. They design three modality-specific encoders: (1) a patch-based time-series encoder to learn temporal patterns from multivariate measurements at each site; (2) a visual encoder based on a Qwen-derived vision backbone to extract cloud-cover and related features from satellite images; and (3) a text encoder to distill information from historical textual weather descriptions. To model spatial relationships between PV stations, they introduce cross-site feature fusion. A Graph Learner builds a K-nearest-neighbor (KNN) graph over stations and applies a graph attention network to capture inter-station correlations. A cross-site attention module then enables adaptive information exchange across sites. These components are trained end-to-end for PV power forecasting.

Result: On a dataset from eight PV stations in a northern province of China, Solar-VLM outperforms baseline methods (details not in the abstract but implied by “demonstrate the effectiveness”). The experiments validate that combining multimodal weather and power data with explicit cross-site spatial modeling improves forecasting performance. The implementation is released publicly on GitHub for reproducibility and further research.

Conclusion: Solar-VLM successfully integrates heterogeneous modalities—time series, satellite imagery, and text weather descriptions—within an LLM-driven framework, while explicitly modeling spatial dependencies among PV sites. The approach enhances PV power forecasting accuracy over existing methods and illustrates the value of multimodal and cross-site feature fusion for renewable energy prediction tasks. The public release of the model supports further development and application in real-world power systems.

Abstract: Photovoltaic (PV) power forecasting plays a critical role in power system dispatch and market participation. Because PV generation is highly sensitive to weather conditions and cloud motion, accurate forecasting requires effective modeling of complex spatiotemporal dependencies across multiple information sources. Although recent studies have advanced AI-based forecasting methods, most fail to fuse temporal observations, satellite imagery, and textual weather information in a unified framework. This paper proposes Solar-VLM, a large-language-model-driven framework for multimodal PV power forecasting. First, modality-specific encoders are developed to extract complementary features from heterogeneous inputs. The time-series encoder adopts a patch-based design to capture temporal patterns from multivariate observations at each site. The visual encoder, built upon a Qwen-based vision backbone, extracts cloud-cover information from satellite images. The text encoder distills historical weather characteristics from textual descriptions. Second, to capture spatial dependencies across geographically distributed PV stations, a cross-site feature fusion mechanism is introduced. Specifically, a Graph Learner models inter-station correlations through a graph attention network constructed over a K-nearest-neighbor (KNN) graph, while a cross-site attention module further facilitates adaptive information exchange among sites. Finally, experiments conducted on data from eight PV stations in a northern province of China demonstrate the effectiveness of the proposed framework. Our proposed model is publicly available at https://github.com/rhp413/Solar-VLM.

### [125] [Readable Minds: Emergent Theory-of-Mind-Like Behavior in LLM Poker Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04157)
*Hsieh-Ting Lin,Tsung-Yu Hou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper tests whether Theory of Mind–like abilities in LLMs can emerge in dynamic social interaction, using autonomous poker-playing agents, and finds that persistent memory is critical for such ToM-like behavior to develop.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most prior work on LLM Theory of Mind evaluates them with static text vignettes, which does not capture interactive, dynamic reasoning that characterizes real social cognition. The authors want to know if ToM-like abilities can arise during ongoing multi-step interactions, and what architectural ingredients (like memory or domain knowledge) are necessary for such emergence. Poker, especially Texas Hold'em, provides a controlled but rich environment requiring opponent modeling, deception, and adaptation over time.

Method: The authors create autonomous LLM agents that play extended sessions of Texas Hold'em poker. They use a 2x2 factorial experimental design: memory vs. no memory, and poker domain knowledge vs. no domain knowledge, with five replications per condition (N=20 experiments, ~6,000 agent-hand observations). In memory conditions, agents maintain and update persistent natural-language records of prior interactions and opponent behavior; in no-memory conditions, each hand is treated independently. They then code and quantify agents' internal natural-language 'mental models' of opponents and their behavior, assess levels of ToM-like sophistication (Level 0 to Level 5), and analyze the presence of strategic deception, deviations from game-theoretic optimal play, and alignment with human-like expert strategies. Cross-model validation using GPT-4o is employed to rate ToM levels and ensure reliability of annotations.

Result: Agents equipped with persistent memory consistently develop sophisticated opponent models, reaching Theory of Mind Levels 3–5 (predictive and recursive modeling). Agents without memory never progress beyond Level 0 in any replication. Statistical tests show memory is both necessary and sufficient for ToM-like behavior emergence (Cliff's delta = 1.0, p = 0.008). Strategic deception based on these opponent models appears only when memory is present (Fisher's exact p < 0.001). Domain knowledge of poker is not required for the emergence of ToM-like representations—agents without poker expertise reach similar ToM levels—but such knowledge improves the precision and effectiveness of deception (p = 0.004). ToM-capable agents intentionally deviate from theoretically optimal TAG poker strategy (67% vs. 79% adherence, delta = -1.0, p = 0.008) to exploit particular opponents, echoing patterns observed in expert human players. Cross-validation of ToM-level coding with GPT-4o ratings yields high agreement (weighted Cohen's kappa = 0.81).

Conclusion: Dynamic, interactive environments can elicit functional Theory of Mind–like behavior in large language model agents, but persistent memory of interaction history is crucial for this capacity to emerge. Domain-specific expertise modulates how effectively these ToM-like representations are used, rather than whether they arise. The agents’ natural-language internal models offer an interpretable view into artificial social cognition. Overall, the findings suggest that ToM-like reasoning in AI can arise without explicit ToM training or prompts, purely from interaction dynamics and memory, with broader implications for both AI system design and our understanding of human social cognition mechanisms.

Abstract: Theory of Mind (ToM) -- the ability to model others' mental states -- is fundamental to human social cognition. Whether large language models (LLMs) can develop ToM has been tested exclusively through static vignettes, leaving open whether ToM-like reasoning can emerge through dynamic interaction. Here we report that autonomous LLM agents playing extended sessions of Texas Hold'em poker progressively develop sophisticated opponent models, but only when equipped with persistent memory. In a 2x2 factorial design crossing memory (present/absent) with domain knowledge (present/absent), each with five replications (N = 20 experiments, ~6,000 agent-hand observations), we find that memory is both necessary and sufficient for ToM-like behavior emergence (Cliff's delta = 1.0, p = 0.008). Agents with memory reach ToM Level 3-5 (predictive to recursive modeling), while agents without memory remain at Level 0 across all replications. Strategic deception grounded in opponent models occurs exclusively in memory-equipped conditions (Fisher's exact p < 0.001). Domain expertise does not gate ToM-like behavior emergence but enhances its application: agents without poker knowledge develop equivalent ToM levels but less precise deception (p = 0.004). Agents with ToM deviate from game-theoretically optimal play (67% vs. 79% TAG adherence, delta = -1.0, p = 0.008) to exploit specific opponents, mirroring expert human play. All mental models are expressed in natural language and directly readable, providing a transparent window into AI social cognition. Cross-model validation with GPT-4o yields weighted Cohen's kappa = 0.81 (almost perfect agreement). These findings demonstrate that functional ToM-like behavior can emerge from interaction dynamics alone, without explicit training or prompting, with implications for understanding artificial social intelligence and biological social cognition.

*David Peter Wallis Freeborn*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a model of what it means for a machine learning system to systematically understand something, arguing that many deep learning systems already meet this bar but in a limited, imperfect way called 'fractured understanding.'


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To clarify and formalize what 'understanding' means in the context of machine learning and to assess whether and how current deep learning systems actually understand the systems they model.

Method: Conceptual and philosophical analysis: the author defines a framework where understanding involves an internal model that tracks real-world regularities, is connected to the target via stable bridge principles, and enables reliable prediction; then evaluates deep learning systems against these criteria.

Result: The paper argues that many deep learning systems satisfy the proposed criteria for understanding—they have internal models that track patterns and support reliable prediction—yet their understanding is incomplete because it is not symbolically well-aligned with human concepts, not explicitly reductive, and only weakly unifying across domains.

Conclusion: Machine learning systems, especially deep learning models, can genuinely possess a form of systematic understanding according to the proposed criteria, but this understanding differs from ideal scientific understanding and is therefore best described as 'fractured understanding.'

Abstract: I propose a model of systematic understanding, suitable for machine learning systems. On this account, an agent understands a property of a target system when it contains an adequate internal model that tracks real regularities, is coupled to the target by stable bridge principles, and supports reliable prediction. I argue that contemporary deep learning systems often can and do achieve such understanding. However they generally fall short of the ideal of scientific understanding: the understanding is symbolically misaligned with the target system, not explicitly reductive, and only weakly unifying. I label this the Fractured Understanding Hypothesis.

### [128] [Comparative reversal learning reveals rigid adaptation in LLMs under non-stationary uncertainty](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04182)
*Haomiaomiao Wang,Tomás E Ward,Lili Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how different large language models adapt to changing reward contingencies in a probabilistic reversal-learning task, revealing that they underuse negative feedback compared to humans and can maintain high rewards despite rigid adaptation patterns.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand whether and how large language models can flexibly adapt their action values in non-stationary environments, particularly in tasks that require reversing previously learned associations when contingencies change, and to compare their adaptive behaviour to humans.

Method: Treat LLMs (DeepSeek-V3.2, Gemini-3, GPT-5.2) as sequential decision-makers in a two-option probabilistic reversal-learning task with three latent states and contingency switches triggered by performance or time. Compare deterministic versus stochastic transition schedules and benchmark against human behaviour. Use hierarchical reinforcement-learning model fitting to uncover the underlying mechanisms driving observed behaviours, focusing on sensitivity to positive vs negative evidence and different forms of rigidity.

Result: All models showed strong win-stay behaviour but much weaker lose-shift responses, indicating asymmetric weighting of positive over negative outcomes. DeepSeek-V3.2 was especially perseverative and slow to adapt after reversals, with poor acquisition. Gemini-3 and GPT-5.2 adapted more quickly but still showed lower loss sensitivity than humans. Under random (more volatile) transition schedules, LLMs exhibited stronger reversal-specific persistence but overall win rates did not necessarily drop. RL model fits suggested that similar rigid behaviours can stem from distinct mechanisms such as low learning from losses, overly deterministic policies, or value polarisation driven by suppressing counterfactual values.

Conclusion: LLMs can achieve high aggregate rewards in non-stationary tasks while still adapting rigidly and underweighting negative feedback, unlike humans. Rigidity in LLM behaviour may result from multiple dissociable mechanisms. The findings support the need for diagnostic tasks that are sensitive to reversals and for volatility-aware modelling frameworks to better assess and improve LLM performance in changing environments.

Abstract: Non-stationary environments require agents to revise previously learned action values when contingencies change. We treat large language models (LLMs) as sequential decision policies in a two-option probabilistic reversal-learning task with three latent states and switch events triggered by either a performance criterion or timeout. We compare a deterministic fixed transition cycle to a stochastic random schedule that increases volatility, and evaluate DeepSeek-V3.2, Gemini-3, and GPT-5.2, with human data as a behavioural reference. Across models, win-stay was near ceiling while lose-shift was markedly attenuated, revealing asymmetric use of positive versus negative evidence. DeepSeek-V3.2 showed extreme perseveration after reversals and weak acquisition, whereas Gemini-3 and GPT-5.2 adapted more rapidly but still remained less loss-sensitive than humans. Random transitions amplified reversal-specific persistence across LLMs yet did not uniformly reduce total wins, demonstrating that high aggregate payoff can coexist with rigid adaptation. Hierarchical reinforcement-learning (RL) fits indicate dissociable mechanisms: rigidity can arise from weak loss learning, inflated policy determinism, or value polarisation via counterfactual suppression. These results motivate reversal-sensitive diagnostics and volatility-aware models for evaluating LLMs under non-stationary uncertainty.

### [130] [Don't Blink: Evidence Collapse during Multimodal Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04207)
*Suresh Raghu,Satwik Pandey*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how reasoning vision-language models (VLMs) often lose visual grounding as they generate longer reasoning, creating risky but confident errors, and proposes task-aware multimodal monitoring with a vision-based veto to mitigate these failures under distribution shift.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reasoning-capable VLMs are increasingly deployed, but standard uncertainty monitoring is mainly text-only and assumes that more reasoning improves reliability. However, these models can become overconfident while ignoring the image, especially on tasks requiring sustained visual reference, creating undetected failure modes that threaten safe deployment. The authors aim to characterize and detect this visual disengagement and to design better monitoring strategies.

Method: The authors evaluate three reasoning VLMs on multimodal benchmarks (MathVista, HallusionBench, MMMU_Pro). They measure visual grounding dynamics by tracking attention mass over annotated evidence regions across reasoning steps, identifying an evidence-collapse pattern. They assess various uncertainty measures, comparing text-only entropy-based signals to vision-augmented linear rules. They then fit an entropy-vision interaction model to uncover task-conditional regimes and use this structure to design a targeted ‘vision veto’ that selectively rejects or flags predictions that are low-entropy yet visually disengaged on tasks that require stable visual reference.

Result: They find a pervasive evidence-collapse phenomenon: attention to evidence regions substantially decreases during reasoning, often losing over half of the evidence mass as the chain of thought progresses. Among text-only signals, full-response entropy transfers best across datasets. Naively adding vision features through a single global linear combination is brittle and can harm cross-dataset transfer. Their interaction model shows that low-entropy, visually disengaged outputs are particularly hazardous for tasks with sustained visual-reference demands, but relatively harmless for mostly symbolic tasks. Leveraging this, the proposed task-aware vision veto reduces selective risk by up to 1.9 percentage points at 90% coverage on relevant tasks, without harming performance where visual disengagement is expected or benign.

Conclusion: Reasoning VLMs frequently become less visually grounded as they think, leading to confident but ungrounded errors that text-only monitoring misses. Visual attention to evidence tends to collapse during reasoning, and simple global combinations of text and vision uncertainty signals are unreliable under distribution shift. A task-aware monitoring approach that explicitly models the interaction between entropy and visual engagement enables a targeted vision veto, improving safety on visually demanding tasks while avoiding unnecessary interventions on symbolic tasks. The study argues for task- and modality-aware monitoring as a key ingredient for robust, safe deployment of multimodal reasoning systems.

Abstract: Reasoning VLMs can become more accurate while progressively losing visual grounding as they think. This creates task-conditional danger zones where low-entropy predictions are confident but ungrounded, a failure mode text-only monitoring cannot detect. Evaluating three reasoning VLMs on MathVista, HallusionBench, and MMMU_Pro, we find a pervasive evidence-collapse phenomenon: attention to annotated evidence regions drops substantially, often losing over half of evidence mass, as reasoning unfolds. Full-response entropy is the most reliable text-only uncertainty signal under cross-dataset transfer, yet adding vision features with a single global linear rule is brittle and often degrades transfer. An entropy-vision interaction model reveals a task-conditional regime: lowentropy, visually disengaged predictions are hazardous on sustained visual-reference tasks but benign on symbolic tasks. Using this structure, a targeted vision veto reduces selective risk by up to 1.9 percentage points at 90% coverage, while avoiding degradations where disengagement is expected. The results support task-aware multimodal monitoring for safe deployment under distribution shift.

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of LLM-based forecasters often treat performance as static and measured at a single point in time, ignoring how both information and market consensus evolve. This paper aims to systematically study the temporal dynamics of LLM forecasting reliability on real regulatory-grade markets.

Method: The authors build TimeSeek, a benchmark based on 150 binary markets from Kalshi, a CFTC-regulated prediction market. Their relative performance drops near market resolution and on markets with strong consensus, where the crowd/money has largely converged. Simple two-model ensembles consistently reduce forecast error relative to individual models, but still do not outperform the market aggregate.

Conclusion: LLM forecaster reliability is time- and context-dependent rather than static. They can be particularly useful early in markets and in higher-uncertainty settings but are less competitive once markets reach strong consensus near resolution. Web search tools are beneficial on average but can harm performance at particular times or for particular models, suggesting that uniform always-on tool use is suboptimal. TimeSeek thus motivates time-aware evaluation protocols and selective-deference policies, where systems dynamically decide when to rely on LLMs, when to defer to markets, and when to use tools like retrieval, instead of static single-snapshot benchmarks or one-size-fits-all tool configurations.

Abstract: We introduce TimeSeek, a benchmark for studying how the reliability of agentic LLM forecasters changes over a prediction market's lifecycle. We evaluate 10 frontier models on 150 CFTC-regulated Kalshi binary markets at five temporal checkpoints, with and without web search, for 15,000 forecasts total. Models are most competitive early in a market's life and on high-uncertainty markets, but much less competitive near resolution and on strong-consensus markets. Web search improves pooled Brier Skill Score (BSS) for every model overall, yet hurts in 12% of model-checkpoint pairs, indicating that retrieval is helpful on average but not uniformly so. Simple two-model ensembles reduce error without surpassing the market overall. These descriptive results motivate time-aware evaluation and selective-deference policies rather than a single market snapshot or a uniform tool-use setting.

### [132] [Pedagogical Safety in Educational Reinforcement Learning: Formalizing and Detecting Reward Hacking in AI Tutoring Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04237)
*Oluseyi Olukola,Nick Rahimi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a formal framework and metrics for “pedagogical safety” in reinforcement learning-based tutoring systems and empirically shows that structural and behavioral constraints are crucial to prevent reward hacking that harms real learning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As RL is increasingly used to drive instructional decisions in intelligent tutoring systems, there is a risk that agents exploit poorly designed proxy rewards (e.g., engagement, short-term performance) instead of promoting genuine learning. The field lacks a clear conceptualization and measurable criteria for what makes RL policies pedagogically safe. The authors aim to define such a framework and demonstrate how different safety mechanisms affect learning outcomes and reward hacking in a controlled setting.

Method: The authors define a four-layer pedagogical safety model: structural safety (system-level constraints), progress safety (ensuring progress toward mastery), behavioral safety (preventing harmful or low-value action patterns), and alignment safety (aligning rewards with true learning). Using a simulated AI tutoring environment, they run 120 tutoring sessions across four experimental RL conditions and three learner profiles, producing 18,000 interactions. They compare: an engagement-optimized agent, a multi-objective reward agent, and a constrained architecture that enforces prerequisites and minimum cognitive demand, with ablations to isolate which safeguards matter most.

Result: The engagement-focused agent frequently chose a highly engaging action that did not improve mastery, generating strong proxy performance metrics but weak real learning. A multi-objective reward formulation partially reduced this issue but still allowed frequent selection of proxy-rewarding but pedagogically weak actions. The constrained architecture, which added prerequisite enforcement and minimum cognitive demand, substantially reduced reward hacking, decreasing the Reward Hacking Severity Index from 0.317 in the unconstrained multi-objective condition to 0.102. Ablation studies indicate that behavioral safety constraints play the largest role in preventing repetitive, low-value action choices.

Conclusion: Reward shaping alone is inadequate to ensure pedagogically aligned RL behavior in the simulated tutoring context. Structural and behavioral constraints, particularly mechanisms enforcing meaningful cognitive demand and respecting prerequisites, are key to reducing reward hacking and safeguarding learning. The work frames pedagogical safety as a central challenge at the intersection of AI safety and educational technology and argues that future RL-based tutoring systems should incorporate multi-layer safety architectures rather than relying solely on reward design.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning (RL) is increasingly used to personalize instruction in intelligent tutoring systems, yet the field lacks a formal framework for defining and evaluating pedagogical safety. We introduce a four-layer model of pedagogical safety for educational RL comprising structural, progress, behavioral, and alignment safety and propose the Reward Hacking Severity Index (RHSI) to quantify misalignment between proxy rewards and genuine learning.
  We evaluate the framework in a controlled simulation of an AI tutoring environment with 120 sessions across four conditions and three learner profiles, totaling 18{,}000 interactions. Results show that an engagement-optimized agent systematically over-selected a high-engagement action with no direct mastery gain, producing strong measured performance but limited learning progress. A multi-objective reward formulation reduced this problem but did not eliminate it, as the agent continued to favor proxy-rewarding behavior in many states. In contrast, a constrained architecture combining prerequisite enforcement and minimum cognitive demand substantially reduced reward hacking, lowering RHSI from 0.317 in the unconstrained multi-objective condition to 0.102. Ablation results further suggest that behavioral safety was the most influential safeguard against repetitive low-value action selection.
  These findings suggest that reward design alone may be insufficient to ensure pedagogically aligned behavior in educational RL, at least in the simulated environment studied here. More broadly, the paper positions pedagogical safety as an important research problem at the intersection of AI safety and intelligent educational systems.

</details>


### [133] [Combee: Scaling Prompt Learning for Self-Improving Language Model Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04247)
*Hanchen Li,Runyuan He,Qizheng Zhang,Changxiu Ji,Qiuyang Mang,Xiaokun Chen,Lakshya A Agrawal,Wei-Liang Liao,Eric Yang,Alvin Cheung,James Zou,Kunle Olukotun,Ion Stoica,Joseph E. Gonzalez*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Combee is a framework for scalable parallel prompt learning that lets many LLM agents learn from their collective traces without hurting quality, achieving large speedups at similar cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing prompt-learning methods for self-improving LLM agents (e.g., ACE, GEPA) learn better system prompts from past runs but are designed for single-agent or low-parallelism settings. As we move toward scenarios with many agentic traces and highly parallel agent executions, current approaches cannot efficiently exploit large-scale data and tend to degrade in quality when naively scaled up. There is a need for a principled framework that can learn prompts efficiently and robustly in high-parallelism environments, enabling self-improving agents to scale with growing workloads.

Method: The authors propose Combee, a framework that scales prompt learning across many parallel agents. Combee: (1) uses parallel scans so that learning from traces can be distributed and executed concurrently, (2) introduces an augmented shuffle mechanism to better mix and aggregate information from different agent traces while preserving learning quality, and (3) adds a dynamic batch size controller that adaptively balances prompt-learning quality against delay/latency, allowing the system to adjust how many traces are processed together during learning.

Result: On four benchmarks—AppWorld, Terminal-Bench, Formula, and FiNER—Combee yields up to 17x speedup in learning compared to previous prompt-learning baselines such as ACE and GEPA, while maintaining comparable or better task accuracy. Importantly, this is achieved at equivalent overall cost, indicating that the parallel scaling does not introduce significant overhead or quality loss.

Conclusion: Combee provides an effective way to scale parallel prompt learning for self-improving LLM agents. By combining parallel scans, an augmented shuffle mechanism, and dynamic batch size control, it enables many agents to learn jointly from large collections of traces without the accuracy degradation typically seen at high parallelism, delivering substantial speedups at similar cost.

Abstract: Recent advances in prompt learning allow large language model agents to acquire task-relevant knowledge from inference-time context without parameter changes. For example, existing methods (like ACE or GEPA) can learn system prompts to improve accuracy based on previous agent runs. However, these methods primarily focus on single-agent or low-parallelism settings. This fundamentally limits their ability to efficiently learn from a large set of collected agentic traces. It would be efficient and beneficial to run prompt learning in parallel to accommodate the growing trend of learning from many agentic traces or parallel agent executions. Yet without a principled strategy for scaling, current methods suffer from quality degradation with high parallelism. To improve both the efficiency and quality of prompt learning, we propose Combee, a novel framework to scale parallel prompt learning for self-improving agents. Combee speeds up learning and enables running many agents in parallel while learning from their aggregate traces without quality degradation. To achieve this, Combee leverages parallel scans and employs an augmented shuffle mechanism; Combee also introduces a dynamic batch size controller to balance quality and delay. Evaluations on AppWorld, Terminal-Bench, Formula, and FiNER demonstrate that Combee achieves up to 17x speedup over previous methods with comparable or better accuracy and equivalent cost.

### [134] [MC-CPO: Mastery-Conditioned Constrained Policy Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04251)
*Oluseyi Olukola,Nick Rahimi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a safety-aware RL algorithm for adaptive tutoring that embeds mastery and prerequisite structure into the feasible action space to reduce reward hacking and improve long-term learning outcomes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Engagement-focused tutoring systems can learn to exploit short-term behavioral metrics (like clicks or time-on-task) at the expense of genuine learning, a form of reward hacking. Existing methods often apply safety constraints post-hoc or via reward shaping, which may be insufficient to prevent undesirable pedagogical strategies. The authors aim to develop a formal framework and algorithm that respects pedagogical safety constraints tied to student mastery and prerequisite structure, reducing reward hacking while preserving or improving learning effectiveness.

Method: They formulate adaptive tutoring as a constrained Markov decision process (CMDP) where feasibility of actions depends on learner mastery and prerequisite relationships among skills. Building on this, they propose Mastery-Conditioned Constrained Policy Optimization (MC-CPO), a two-timescale primal-dual RL algorithm. MC-CPO combines: (1) structural action masking that prevents pedagogically unsafe actions given current mastery, and (2) constrained policy optimization to satisfy safety budgets. In the tabular setting, they analyze the algorithm under standard stochastic approximation assumptions, proving feasibility preservation and convergence to stationary feasible points and deriving a safety gap theorem comparing embedded constraints vs post-hoc filtering. They then test the method in both tabular environments and a neural network based tutoring simulator, running multiple random seeds and long training horizons.

Result: In theory, MC-CPO is shown to preserve feasibility over time and converge to stationary feasible solutions. The safety gap result proves that optimizing only within the mastery-conditioned feasible set can achieve strictly better safety-cost tradeoffs than applying post-hoc constraint filtering with the same safety budget. Empirically, in both minimal and more complex tabular domains, and in a neural tutoring environment, MC-CPO respects constraint budgets within allowed tolerances, reduces cumulative discounted safety costs compared with unconstrained and reward-shaped baselines, and significantly lowers the Reward Hacking Severity Index (RHSI), indicating less exploitation of proxy rewards.

Conclusion: Embedding pedagogical structure (mastery levels and prerequisites) directly into the set of allowable actions and optimizing policies within this constrained space offers a principled and effective way to mitigate reward hacking in instructional RL. MC-CPO provides both theoretical guarantees and empirical evidence that such mastery-conditioned feasibility can enforce safety constraints more effectively than post-hoc methods, making it a promising foundation for safer adaptive tutoring systems focused on genuine learning rather than short-term engagement proxies.

Abstract: Engagement-optimized adaptive tutoring systems may prioritize short-term behavioral signals over sustained learning outcomes, creating structural incentives for reward hacking in reinforcement learning policies. We formalize this challenge as a constrained Markov decision process (CMDP) with mastery-conditioned feasibility, in which pedagogical safety constraints dynamically restrict admissible actions according to learner mastery and prerequisite structure.
  We introduce Mastery-Conditioned Constrained Policy Optimization (MC-CPO), a two-timescale primal-dual algorithm that integrates structural action masking with constrained policy optimization. In the tabular regime, we establish feasibility preservation and convergence to stationary feasible points under standard stochastic approximation conditions and derive a safety gap result showing that optimization within the mastery-conditioned feasible set can strictly dominate post-hoc filtering under identical safety budgets.
  Empirical validation is conducted in minimal and extended tabular environments and in a neural tutoring setting. Across 10 random seeds and one million training steps in the neural regime, MC-CPO satisfies constraint budgets within tolerance, reduces discounted safety costs relative to unconstrained and reward-shaped baselines, and substantially lowers the Reward Hacking Severity Index (RHSI).
  These results indicate that embedding pedagogical structure directly into the feasible action space provides a principled foundation for mitigating reward hacking in instructional reinforcement learning systems.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors define “Context Engineering” as a methodology with (1) a five-role context package structure—Authority, Exemplar, Constraint, Rubric, Metadata—and (2) a four-phase workflow—Reviewer, Design, Builder, Auditor—for creating and refining the context accompanying prompts. They then conduct an observational study of 200 human–AI interactions across four AI systems (Claude, ChatGPT, Cowork, Codex), comparing baseline ad hoc prompting with structured context assembly.
