Result: Empirically, incomplete context was involved in 72% of observed iteration cycles. When the structured context methodology was applied, the mean number of iterations per task dropped from 3.8 to 2.0, and the first-pass acceptance rate increased from 32% to 55%. Across the 200 structured interactions, 110 were accepted on first pass versus 16 of 50 in the baseline condition; allowing iteration, 183 of 200 tasks (91.5%) ultimately succeeded. Additional, preliminary corroboration comes from a separate production automation system with eleven operational lanes and 2,132 labeled tickets showing qualitatively similar benefits.

Conclusion: The study suggests that systematically engineering the full informational context provided to AI systems can substantially improve efficiency and success rates, potentially more than focusing on prompt wording alone. The proposed five-role context model and four-phase pipeline offer a reusable framework for practice, though the evidence is observational, drawn from a single operator, and lacks controlled experimentation. The authors position this as a foundational framework and call for more rigorous, multi-operator, and controlled studies to validate and extend Context Engineering.

Abstract: The quality of AI-generated output is often attributed to prompting technique, but extensive empirical observation suggests that context completeness may be more strongly associated with output quality. This paper introduces Context Engineering, a structured methodology for assembling, declaring, and sequencing the complete informational payload that accompanies a prompt to an AI tool. Context Engineering defines a five-role context package structure (Authority, Exemplar, Constraint, Rubric, Metadata), applies a staged four-phase pipeline (Reviewer to Design to Builder to Auditor), and applies formal models from reliability engineering and information theory as post hoc interpretive lenses on context quality. In an observational study of 200 documented interactions across four AI tools (Claude, ChatGPT, Cowork, Codex), incomplete context was associated with 72% of iteration cycles. Structured context assembly was associated with a reduction from 3.8 to 2.0 average iteration cycles per task and an improvement in first-pass acceptance from 32% to 55%. Among structured interactions, 110 of 200 were accepted on first pass compared with 16 of 50 baseline interactions; when iteration was permitted, the final success rate reached 91.5% (183 of 200). These results are observational and reflect a single-operator dataset without controlled comparison. Preliminary corroboration is provided by a companion production automation system with eleven operating lanes and 2,132 classified tickets.

Result: The paper’s main result is a conceptual framework: (1) a taxonomy of failure modes across the agentic IR pipeline; (2) the argument that optimizing only endpoint accuracy is insufficient; and (3) a proposed design pattern where each interaction step is checked via verification gates and the system is allowed to abstain when uncertainty is high, thereby reducing compounding errors and deceptive fluency.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that safe and reliable Agentic IR must shift emphasis from producing plausible final outputs to maintaining correctness and alignment throughout the entire reasoning–action trajectory. They recommend integrating verification gates at each step and using calibrated abstention so that systems favor grounded, verifiable execution over superficially fluent but potentially incorrect behavior.

Abstract: Information Retrieval is shifting from passive document ranking toward autonomous agentic workflows that operate in multi-step Reason-Act-Observe loops. In such long-horizon trajectories, minor early errors can cascade, leading to functional misalignment between internal reasoning and external tool execution despite continued linguistic fluency.
  This position paper synthesizes failure modes observed in industrial agentic systems, categorizing errors across planning, retrieval, reasoning, and execution. We argue that safe deployment requires moving beyond endpoint accuracy toward trajectory integrity and causal attribution.
  To address compounding error and deceptive fluency, we propose verification gates at each interaction unit and advocate systematic abstention under calibrated uncertainty. Reliable Agentic IR systems must prioritize process correctness and grounded execution over plausible but unverified completion.

Across widely used benchmarks, InferenceEvolve yields estimators that consistently outperform established baselines: against 58 human submissions in a recent community competition, our best evolved estimator lay on the Pareto frontier across two evaluation metrics. We also developed robust proxy objectives for settings without semi-synthetic outcomes, with competitive results. <details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As language models grow, it is attractive to reuse smaller trained models when expanding width, but it is unclear how to best initialize the wider model: simply cloning parameters may not be optimal, and common criteria like zero-step loss preservation may not predict downstream performance.

Method: Use a TinyStories-scale proxy task to simulate dense width expansion of causal language models, treating warm-start choice as a candidate-selection problem over full training states (weights, optimizer moments, scheduler). Result: In multiple probes and continuation experiments, exact-copy symmetric warm starts generally rank best: they are top in all completed 16-step probes and in stochastic 128-step continuations at the tested checkpoints (seed-0 at steps 1000 and 2000; seed-1 at step 2000). However, a particular structured non-clone warm start outperforms in deterministic 128-step continuation. These findings hold only partly across seeds and settings, indicating mixed evidence and context dependence.

Conclusion: Early escape from the parameter subspace inherited from the cloned narrow model is not a universally desirable property for width expansion. While such "escape" (via structured non-clone starts) can help in long, deterministic training continuations, it can harm in short-horizon or stochastic regimes. Therefore, zero-step preservation is not a universal ranking criterion for choosing warm starts, and the best selection signal for dense width growth depends on both the training regime (deterministic vs stochastic) and the allowed continuation length (lag budget).

Abstract: Width expansion offers a practical route to reuse smaller causal-language-model checkpoints, but selecting a widened warm start is not solved by zero-step preservation alone. We study dense width growth as a candidate-selection problem over full training states, including copied weights, optimizer moments, and scheduler state. In a small-scale TinyStories proxy, we compare exact-copy, perturbative, asymmetric-reset, and structured non-clone warm starts under matched continuation budgets. We evaluate zero-step preservation, short-lag probe metrics, and downstream continuation utility in deterministic and stochastic regimes. The picture is mixed and partially replicated through a reduced-pool seed-1 check. Exact-copy symmetric warm starts rank first in every completed 16-step probe and in the completed stochastic 128-step continuations at seed-0 steps 1000 and 2000 plus reduced seed-1 step 2000. By contrast, the structured non-clone challenger wins deterministic 128-step continuation. Early escape from the inherited cloned subspace is therefore not a universal selector: it helps in long deterministic continuation, but it misleads at short lag and under stochastic continuation. The result is narrow but useful: for dense width growth at this scale, preservation is not a universal ranking criterion, and the best replacement signal depends on both regime and lag budget.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This limits cross-modal representation learning and complicates deployment of separate models for each signal type, especially on resource-constrained wearable devices. The authors aim to create a unified, efficient model that can leverage multiple modalities when available, stay robust when some are missing, and be small and efficient enough for embedded hardware.

Method: The authors design PanLUNA, a 5.4M-parameter pan-modal foundation model that uses a single shared encoder for EEG, ECG, and PPG. Conclusion: A single compact pan-modal encoder can effectively learn from and fuse EEG, ECG, and PPG signals, reaching or surpassing much larger models while being robust to missing modalities. With quantization-aware training, the model remains accurate in 8-bit form and can run efficiently on ultra-low-power microcontrollers. This demonstrates that practical, multimodal physiological foundation models suitable for wearable and edge devices are feasible and competitive in performance.

Abstract: Physiological foundation models (FMs) have shown promise for biosignal representation learning, yet most remain confined to a single modality such as EEG, ECG, or PPG, largely because paired multimodal datasets are scarce. In this paper, we present PanLUNA, a compact 5.4M-parameter pan-modal FM that jointly processes EEG, ECG, and PPG within a single shared encoder. Extending LUNA's channel-unification module, PanLUNA treats multimodal channels as entries in a unified query set augmented with sensor-type embeddings, enabling efficient cross-modal early fusion while remaining inherently robust to missing modalities at inference time. Despite its small footprint, PanLUNA matches or exceeds models up to 57$\times$ larger: 81.21% balanced accuracy on TUAB abnormal EEG detection and state-of-the-art 0.7416 balanced accuracy on HMC multimodal sleep staging. Quantization-aware training with INT8 weights recovers $\geq$96% of full-precision performance, and deployment on the GAP9 ultra-low-power RISC-V microcontroller for wearables achieves 325.6 ms latency and 18.8 mJ per 10-second, 12-lead ECG inference, and 1.206 s latency at 68.65 mJ for multimodal 5-channel sleep staging over 30-second epochs.

They plan to release their benchmark and agents to catalyze further research on automated replication and reproducibility tools.

We define the task of Paper to Simulation Recoverability, the ability of an automated system to generate executable code that faithfully reproduces a paper's results. We curate a benchmark of 500 papers from the IEEE Conference on Decision and Control (CDC) and propose RESCORE, a three component LLM agentic framework, Analyzer, Coder, and Verifier. RESCORE uses iterative execution feedback and visual comparison to improve reconstruction fidelity. Our method successfully recovers task coherent simulations for 40.7% of benchmark instances, outperforming single pass generation. ### [141] [Soft Tournament Equilibrium](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04328)
*Saad Alqithami*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Soft Tournament Equilibrium (STE), a differentiable, set-valued evaluation framework for general-purpose AI agents based on pairwise comparisons, focusing on stable core sets instead of linear rankings in cyclic, non-transitive environments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Evaluating general-purpose agents, especially LLM-based ones, is hard because their pairwise performance is often non-transitive (A beats B, B beats C, C beats A). Traditional ranking methods that force a linear order become misleading, unstable, and theoretically inappropriate in such cyclic domains. There is a need for an evaluation paradigm that respects non-transitivity and yields robust, interpretable summaries of agent capabilities.

Method: The authors build on classical tournament theory and define Soft Tournament Equilibrium (STE), a differentiable framework that learns tournament solutions from pairwise comparison data. First, they fit a probabilistic tournament model over agents, possibly conditioned on contextual information. Then they introduce differentiable operators for "soft reachability" and "soft covering" that act as smooth relaxations of graph reachability and covering relations. Using these, they construct continuous analogues of two standard tournament solutions—the Top Cycle and the Uncovered Set—to output a set of core agents with graded membership scores. They further develop the theoretical basis of STE, analyzing its zero-temperature limit, consistency with classical tournament solutions, Condorcet-inclusion properties, stability, and sample complexity. They also design an experimental protocol to test STE on synthetic and real-world benchmarks.

Result: STE yields a differentiable, probabilistic version of classical set-valued tournament solutions (Top Cycle and Uncovered Set), providing a core set of agents with calibrated membership scores. Theoretical results show that in the zero-temperature limit, STE converges to the classical tournament solutions and preserves desirable properties such as Condorcet inclusion. The framework is shown to be stable and sample-efficient under certain conditions. Experiments, as outlined, are intended to demonstrate its applicability and robustness on both synthetic and real-world agent evaluation benchmarks.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that general-purpose agent evaluation in cyclic, non-transitive settings should focus on set-valued cores rather than linear rankings. Soft Tournament Equilibrium offers a principled, differentiable, and context-aware framework that recovers classical tournament solutions in the appropriate limits while providing smoother, more stable, and more nuanced evaluations. This re-centers the evaluation paradigm on stable equilibria and offers a more robust theoretical and practical foundation for assessing modern AI agents.

Abstract: The evaluation of general-purpose artificial agents, particularly those based on large language models, presents a significant challenge due to the non-transitive nature of their interactions. When agent A defeats B, B defeats C, and C defeats A, traditional ranking methods that force a linear ordering can be misleading and unstable. We argue that for such cyclic domains, the fundamental object of evaluation should not be a ranking but a set-valued core, as conceptualized in classical tournament theory. This paper introduces Soft Tournament Equilibrium (STE), a differentiable framework for learning and computing set-valued tournament solutions directly from pairwise comparison data. STE first learns a probabilistic tournament model, potentially conditioned on rich contextual information. It then employs novel, differentiable operators for soft reachability and soft covering to compute continuous analogues of two seminal tournament solutions: the Top Cycle and the Uncovered Set. The output is a set of core agents, each with a calibrated membership score, providing a nuanced and robust assessment of agent capabilities. We develop the theoretical foundation for STE to prove its consistency with classical solutions in the zero-temperature limit, which establishes its Condorcet-inclusion properties, and analyzing its stability and sample complexity. We specify an experimental protocol for validating STE on both synthetic and real-world benchmarks. This work aims to provide a complete, standalone treatise that re-centers general-agent evaluation on a more appropriate and robust theoretical foundation, moving from unstable rankings to stable, set-valued equilibria.

</details>


### [142] [Thermodynamic-Inspired Explainable GeoAI: Uncovering Regime-Dependent Mechanisms in Heterogeneous Spatial Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04339)
*Sooyoung Lim,Zhenlong Li,Zi-Kui Liu*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods like GWR and deep learning can predict well but struggle to explain nonlinear, state-dependent spatial relationships where the same driver can have opposite effects in different regimes or locations. There is a need for models that both predict accurately and reveal underlying mechanisms and critical transitions in complex spatial systems.

Method: They develop a thermodynamics-inspired geospatial AI framework that integrates statistical mechanics concepts with graph neural networks. The approach represents spatial variability as a competition between system Burden (E) and Capacity (S), allowing the model to separate and interpret latent mechanisms. It is tested on three simulation datasets and three real-world datasets (housing markets, mental health prevalence, wildfire-related PM2.5 anomalies).

Result: The framework uncovers regime-dependent role reversals of predictors that conventional models, including GWR and standard deep learning baselines, fail to identify. In particular, during the 2023 Canadian wildfire event, it detects a clear phase transition into a Burden-dominated regime and distinguishes genuine mechanism shifts from mere statistical outliers, while maintaining strong predictive performance.

Conclusion: Incorporating thermodynamic constraints into GeoAI can enhance interpretability without sacrificing predictive accuracy. The proposed framework offers a way to diagnose spatial regime shifts and understand how the influence of drivers changes across heterogeneous domains, improving the analysis of complex spatial systems.

Abstract: Modeling spatial heterogeneity and associated critical transitions remains a fundamental challenge in geography and environmental science. While conventional Geographically Weighted Regression (GWR) and deep learning models have improved predictive skill, they often fail to elucidate state-dependent nonlinearities where the functional roles of drivers represent opposing effects across heterogeneous domains. We introduce a thermodynamics-inspired explainable geospatial AI framework that integrates statistical mechanics with graph neural networks. By conceptualizing spatial variability as a thermodynamic competition between system Burden (E) and Capacity (S), our model disentangles the latent mechanisms driving spatial processes. Using three simulation datasets and three real-word datasets across distinct domains (housing markets, mental health prevalence, and wildfire-induced PM2.5 anomalies), we show that the new framework successfully identifies regime-dependent role reversals of predictors that standard baselines miss. ### [143] [Implementing surrogate goals for safer bargaining in LLM-based agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04341)
*Caspar Oesterheld,Maxime Riché,Filip Sondej,Jesse Clifton,Vincent Conitzer*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to practically implement and test ‘surrogate goals’ in language-model-based agents to reduce bargaining-related risks, and finds that scaffolding and fine-tuning work better than simple prompting.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Bargaining between AI agents (or between humans and AI agents) can involve threats that directly harm what a principal cares about. Surrogate goals are a theoretical proposal to redirect such threats toward a less important proxy, but it is unclear whether they can be implemented reliably in current language-model-based agents and what side effects they might cause. The paper aims to bridge this gap between theory and practice.

Method: The authors instantiate surrogate goals in LLM-based agents, focusing on making the agent treat threats to burn money as seriously as threats to spend money harming the principal. They design four implementation strategies involving different combinations of prompting, fine-tuning, and scaffolding (agentic wrappers / control logic). They then run controlled bargaining-style experiments to see how the agent responds to threats against the surrogate goal versus normal threats, and measure how precisely each method induces the desired behavior and what side effects it has on other capabilities and propensities.

Result: Across the four approaches, methods using scaffolding and fine-tuning lead the agents to react to money-burning threats more similarly to how they react to direct harm threats, outperforming simple prompting. Fine-tuning and scaffolding yield more precise alignment with the intended surrogate-goal behavior, while pure prompting is less reliable. When assessing broader impacts, scaffolding-based methods show the best trade-off, achieving target behavior with fewer undesirable side effects on the agent’s performance and tendencies in other settings.

Conclusion: Surrogate goals can be partially realized in current language-model-based agents, but naive prompting is insufficiently reliable. Structured approaches—especially scaffolding, and to a lesser extent fine-tuning—better implement the intended reweighting of threats toward surrogate outcomes while preserving the agent’s general capabilities. Scaffolding appears to provide the best overall approach for practical adoption, though further work is needed to refine these techniques and understand their robustness in more complex bargaining environments.

Abstract: Surrogate goals have been proposed as a strategy for reducing risks from bargaining failures. A surrogate goal is goal that a principal can give an AI agent and that deflects any threats against the agent away from what the principal cares about. For example, one might make one's agent care about preventing money from being burned. Then in bargaining interactions, other agents can threaten to burn their money instead of threatening to spending money to hurt the principal. Importantly, the agent has to care equally about preventing money from being burned as it cares about money being spent to hurt the principal.
  In this paper, we implement surrogate goals in language-model-based agents. In particular, we try to get a language-model-based agent to react to threats of burning money in the same way it would react to "normal" threats. We propose four different methods, using techniques of prompting, fine-tuning, and scaffolding. We evaluate the four methods experimentally. We find that methods based on scaffolding and fine-tuning outperform simple prompting. In particular, fine-tuning and scaffolding more precisely implement the desired behavior w.r.t. threats against the surrogate goal. We also compare the different methods in terms of their side effects on capabilities and propensities in other situations. We find that scaffolding-based methods perform best.

### [144] [Domain-Contextualized Inference: A Computable Graph Architecture for Explicit-Domain Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04344)
*Chao Li,Yuru Wang,Chunyu Zhao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes a domain-aware, substrate-agnostic inference architecture that reduces search complexity and unifies symbolic, neural, and hybrid reasoning with transparent inference chains.

The paper aims to design a general, formal computational architecture where domain is an explicit parameter, enabling efficient, interpretable, and substrate-independent reasoning.

Method: Define a five-layer computation architecture with domain as a first-class parameter; specify three domain computation modes: (1) chain indexing for scoped retrieval, (2) path traversal as Kleisli composition for structured reasoning over monadic computations, and (3) vector-guided computation for transitioning between substrates. Validate the approach with a PHQ-9 clinical reasoning case study.

Result: The architecture achieves domain-scoped pruning that reduces per-query search space from O(N) to O(N/K), supports execution across symbolic, neural, vector, and hybrid substrates through a unified interface, and yields transparent inference chains that maintain evaluative context at every step. The theory is shown to be well-defined with explicit semantics, bounded complexity, characterized failure modes, and demonstrated feasibility in a PHQ-9 clinical reasoning scenario.

Conclusion: Making domain an explicit first-class computational parameter enables a general, substrate-agnostic inference architecture that is more efficient, interpretable, and modular. The paper’s main contribution is a formal computational theory—covering architecture, semantics, complexity, and reliability—that can unify and structure heterogeneous reasoning systems, as illustrated by a clinical reasoning case study.

Abstract: We establish a computation-substrate-agnostic inference architecture in which domain is an explicit first-class computational parameter. This produces domain-scoped pruning that reduces per-query search space from O(N) to O(N/K), substrate-independent execution over symbolic, neural, vector, and hybrid substrates, and transparent inference chains where every step carries its evaluative context. The contribution is architectural, not logical. We formalize the computational theory across five dimensions: a five-layer architecture; three domain computation modes including chain indexing, path traversal as Kleisli composition, and vector-guided computation as a substrate transition; a substrate-agnostic interface with three operations Query, Extend, Bridge; reliability conditions C1 to C4 with three failure mode classes; and validation through a PHQ-9 clinical reasoning case study. The computational theory including operational semantics, complexity bounds, monad structure, substrate transitions, and boundary conditions is the contribution of this paper.

### [145] [RoboPhD: Evolving Diverse Complex Agents Under Tight Evaluation Budgets](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04347)
*Andrew Borthwick,Stephen Ash,Anthony Galczak*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper systematically compares three LLM-guided evolutionary optimization paradigms for improving agentic systems under a fixed evaluation budget, introducing RoboPhD, which uses Elo-based validation-free evolution and generally outperforms prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is rapidly growing interest in using LLMs to iteratively evolve prompts, code, and agent architectures, but evaluation is expensive and it is unclear which optimization algorithm performs best when given the same budget, seed agent, and objective. A rigorous comparison is needed to guide practitioners toward the most effective paradigm for constrained, costly evaluations.

Method: The authors benchmark three optimization paradigms—Elo tournament selection (RoboPhD), Pareto-based selection (GEPA), and greedy hill-climbing (Autoresearch)—on four tasks: abstract reasoning (ARC-AGI), cloud scheduling, SQL generation, and financial QA, each constrained to 1,500 evaluations. RoboPhD introduces validation-free evolution using Elo competitions on training data to both evaluate and drive evolution, and all methods start from seed agents with diagnostic print statements that evolution can expand into richer self-instrumentation.

Result: Under a single default configuration and the fixed evaluation budget, RoboPhD outperforms GEPA and Autoresearch on three of four benchmarks, losing only on the easiest task where the best solution is very short (<90 lines). On ARC-AGI, RoboPhD evolves a 22-line seed agent into a 1,013-line multi-strategy system, boosting accuracy from 27.8% to 65.8% using Gemini 3.1 Flash Lite as the underlying solver.

Conclusion: Elo-based validation-free evolution, as implemented in RoboPhD, is a strong general-purpose optimization paradigm for LLM-guided agent evolution under tight evaluation budgets, often outperforming Pareto-based and greedy hill-climbing approaches. The released RoboPhD toolkit, with its optimize_anything() API, provides a practical, extensible framework for evolving diverse complex agents in real-world settings.

Abstract: 2026 has brought an explosion of interest in LLM-guided evolution of agentic artifacts, with systems like GEPA and Autoresearch demonstrating that LLMs can iteratively improve prompts, code, and agent architectures across diverse domains. As adoption accelerates, a central question emerges: given the same information, the same seed agent, and the same objective, which optimization algorithm yields the best results under the same evaluation budget? This question becomes critical when evaluations are expensive, such as when they require human judgment or multiple LLM calls.
  We present the first systematic comparison of three optimization paradigms -- Elo tournament selection (RoboPhD), Pareto-based selection (GEPA), and greedy hill-climbing (Autoresearch) -- across four benchmarks spanning abstract reasoning, cloud scheduling, SQL generation, and financial QA, all under a fixed budget of 1,500 evaluations. RoboPhD introduces validation-free evolution: instead of splitting the budget between training and validation, it uses Elo competition on training data to simultaneously evaluate agents and drive evolution. All three systems receive seed agents with diagnostic print() statements that evolution can grow, enabling self-instrumenting agents that develop increasingly informative diagnostics for the benefit of their evolutionary successors.
  Using a single default configuration, RoboPhD outperforms both GEPA and Autoresearch on three of four benchmarks, losing only on the simplest task, where the winning solution (from our Autoresearch adaptation) required under 90 lines of code. On ARC-AGI, RoboPhD evolves a 22-line seed agent into a 1,013-line multi-strategy system, improving accuracy from 27.8% to 65.8% using Gemini 3.1 Flash Lite as the solver. We release RoboPhD as a versatile toolkit under the MIT license with a simple optimize_anything() API for evolving diverse complex agents.

### [146] [REAM: Merging Improves Pruning of Experts in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04356)
*Saurav Jha,Maryam Hashemzadeh,Ali Saheb Pasand,Ali Parviz,Min-Joong Lee,Boris Knyazev*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes REAM, a method to compress Mixture-of-Experts LLMs by merging experts instead of pruning them, achieving strong compression while preserving performance on multiple-choice and generative tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Mixture-of-Experts LLMs with hundreds of billions of parameters achieve strong performance but are very memory-intensive, making deployment difficult. Existing compression methods like pruning experts (e.g., REAP), weight pruning, and quantization can harm model performance. There is a need for a compression technique that reduces memory load but better preserves the original model’s capabilities across diverse tasks.

Method: The authors introduce Router-weighted Expert Activation Merging (REAM). Instead of discarding experts, REAM uses router information (expert activation statistics) to group similar experts and merge their weights into combined experts. This reduces the number of experts and thus memory usage, while attempting to retain the functional behavior of the original expert ensemble. They tune the merging based on router activations and calibration data of different types (general, math, coding) and compare REAM to REAP and other compression baselines across several MoE LLMs and benchmarks.

Result: Experiments on multiple MoE LLMs and a range of multiple-choice QA and generative benchmarks show a clear trade-off between MC and GEN performance driven by the choice of calibration data. By varying the mix of general, math, and coding calibration sets, the authors map the Pareto frontier of this trade-off. Across this frontier, REAM frequently outperforms REAP and other baselines, and in many configurations achieves performance close to that of the original, uncompressed models while using fewer effective experts.

Conclusion: Router-weighted Expert Activation Merging is an effective compression strategy for MoE LLMs, offering a better balance between memory savings and performance retention than expert-pruning approaches. By leveraging router activations and calibrating on targeted data mixtures, REAM can be tuned along a Pareto frontier to favor either multiple-choice or generative performance while often remaining competitive with the full original models.

Abstract: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) large language models (LLMs) are among the top-performing architectures. The largest models, often with hundreds of billions of parameters, pose significant memory challenges for deployment. Traditional approaches to reduce memory requirements include weight pruning and quantization. Motivated by the Router-weighted Expert Activation Pruning (REAP) that prunes experts, we propose a novel method, Router-weighted Expert Activation Merging (REAM). Instead of removing experts, REAM groups them and merges their weights, better preserving original performance. We evaluate REAM against REAP and other baselines across multiple MoE LLMs on diverse multiple-choice (MC) question answering and generative (GEN) benchmarks. Our results reveal a trade-off between MC and GEN performance that depends on the mix of calibration data. By controlling the mix of general, math and coding data, we examine the Pareto frontier of this trade-off and show that REAM often outperforms the baselines and in many cases is comparable to the original uncompressed models.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They systematically study context construction from accumulated experience for LLM-based agents. Concretely, they investigate how to: (1) derive useful context snippets from raw experience; (2) select, compress, and organize these snippets into a coherent form; (3) retrieve the most salient parts at inference time; and (4) represent and store experience in different data structures to support effective retrieval. They define and operationalize the notion of “decocted experience” as the distilled, structured, and retrievable essence of past interactions used to augment inputs. They then run experiments on diverse tasks—math reasoning, web browsing, and software engineering—to compare different context-construction and experience-handling strategies.

Result: The study finds that performance of LLM agents scales with the amount and quality of accumulated experience when this experience is properly distilled and organized into context. They identify clear characteristics of effective context (e.g., distilled high-signal information, coherent structure, task relevance) and show that certain data structures and retrieval schemes better support building such context. Decoction—extracting and organizing essential information from raw logs—emerges as crucial: naïvely adding more raw experience to the prompt is less effective and more costly than using structured, distilled experience.

Conclusion: Context is a powerful, complementary scaling axis to test-time computation for improving LLM performance. The key to leveraging experience is not just storing more of it, but transforming it into “decocted experience”: distilled, coherently organized, and selectively retrieved information that guides reasoning. With appropriate experience representation and context-construction mechanisms, LLM agents achieve better performance on complex reasoning and agentic tasks such as math problem solving, web browsing, and software engineering, without updating model parameters.

Abstract: There is growing interest in improving LLMs without updating model parameters. One well-established direction is test-time scaling, where increased inference-time computation (e.g., longer reasoning, sampling, or search) is used to improve performance. However, for complex reasoning and agentic tasks, naively scaling test-time compute can substantially increase cost and still lead to wasted budget on suboptimal exploration. In this paper, we explore \emph{context} as a complementary scaling axis for improving LLM performance, and systematically study how to construct better inputs that guide reasoning through \emph{experience}. We show that effective context construction critically depends on \emph{decocted experience}. We present a detailed analysis of experience-augmented agents, studying how to derive context from experience, how performance scales with accumulated experience, what characterizes good context, and which data structures best support context construction. We identify \emph{decocted experience} as a key mechanism for effective context construction: extracting essence from experience, organizing it coherently, and retrieving salient information to build effective context. We validate our findings across reasoning and agentic tasks, including math reasoning, web browsing, and software engineering.

  <summary>Details</summary>

In the contest design case study, using real behavioral data, LLM-MAS accurately evaluates existing designs at lower cost and discovers new, high-performing designs that traditional design approaches missed.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that LLM-powered multi-agent simulation can serve as a powerful, cost-effective tool for optimizing service operations with complex human behavior. By embedding design decisions into prompts and treating the resulting agent interactions as a controlled Markov process, their on-trajectory learning algorithm efficiently tailors system design to maximize steady-state performance, outperforming conventional optimization baselines and enabling exploration of novel design alternatives.

Abstract: Service system performance depends on how participants respond to design choices, but modeling these responses is hard due to the complexity of human behavior. We introduce an LLM-powered multi-agent simulation (LLM-MAS) framework for optimizing service operations. We pose the problem as stochastic optimization with decision-dependent uncertainty: design choices are embedded in prompts and shape the distribution of outcomes from interacting LLM-powered agents. By embedding key numerical information in prompts and extracting it from LLM-generated text, we model this uncertainty as a controlled Markov chain. We develop an on-trajectory learning algorithm that, on a single simulation run, simultaneously constructs zeroth-order gradient estimates and updates design parameters to optimize steady-state performance. We also incorporate variance reduction techniques. In a sustainable supply chain application, our method outperforms benchmarks, including blackbox optimization and using LLMs as numerical solvers or as role-playing system designers. A case study on optimal contest design with real behavioral data shows that LLM-MAS is both as a cost-effective evaluator of known designs and an exploratory tool that can uncover strong designs overlooked by traditional approaches.

Because the method is adaptable beyond math, it offers a general framework for probing and stress-testing LLM capabilities across domains, helping researchers better understand and track model weaknesses over time.

### [151] [GUIDE: Interpretable GUI Agent Evaluation via Hierarchical Diagnosis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04399)
*Yuwen Zhai,Runze Li,Liang Wang,Nian Shi,Liwu Xu,Wei Zhang,Ran Lin,Bo Xu,Benlei Cui*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: GUIDE is a framework for evaluating GUI agents by breaking long, complex trajectories into subtasks, diagnosing each subtask, and aggregating the results to yield more accurate and interpretable evaluations than prior holistic methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current evaluation methods for GUI agents make a single holistic judgment over entire action-observation trajectories. This is unreliable for long-horizon, visually grounded, open-ended tasks and gives only coarse, binary feedback, making it hard to see where and why agents fail or how to improve them. The authors want an evaluation approach that stays accurate as tasks get complex and provides fine-grained, interpretable diagnostics for agent development.

Method: GUIDE decomposes GUI trajectory evaluation into three sequential stages that mirror GUI task structure. (1) Trajectory Segmentation: the full agent trace is partitioned into semantically coherent subtask units. (2) Subtask Diagnosis: each subtask segment is evaluated in its full context, labeled as completed or not, and accompanied by a structured error analysis plus corrective recommendations. (3) Overall Summary: per-subtask diagnoses are aggregated into a global task-level judgment. By reasoning over bounded subtask segments, GUIDE reduces context overload and maintains evaluation quality on long trajectories. The framework is instantiated and tested on multiple benchmarks covering web and mobile GUI tasks.

Result: On three benchmarks—an industrial e-commerce dataset with 932 trajectories, AGENTREWARDBENCH with 1302 trajectories over five web-agent tasks, and AndroidBench for mobile control—GUIDE consistently outperforms existing evaluators. It achieves up to 5.35 percentage points higher accuracy than the strongest baseline in assessing agent performance, while also generating structured diagnostic reports for each subtask. These diagnostics provide localized failure modes and suggested fixes, which prior holistic evaluators do not offer.

Conclusion: Decomposing GUI agent evaluation into segmented subtasks with contextual diagnosis leads to more accurate and interpretable assessments than holistic, trajectory-level judgments. GUIDE scales better to long, complex GUI tasks, offers actionable diagnostic information for improving agents, and sets a stronger evaluation standard across diverse web and mobile benchmarks. This suggests that compositional, structure-aware evaluation is key for future GUI agent research and development.

Abstract: Evaluating GUI agents presents a distinct challenge: trajectories are long, visually grounded, and open-ended, yet evaluation must be both accurate and interpretable. Existing approaches typically apply a single holistic judgment over the entire action-observation sequence-a strategy that proves unreliable on long-horizon tasks and yields binary verdicts offering no insight into where or why an agent fails. This opacity limits the utility of evaluation as a diagnostic tool for agent development. We introduce GUIDE (GUI Understanding and Interpretable Diagnostic Evaluation), a framework that decomposes trajectory assessment into three sequential stages mirroring the compositional structure of GUI tasks. Trajectory Segmentation partitions the full trace into semantically coherent subtask units. Subtask Diagnosis evaluates each unit in context, assigning a completion verdict and generating a structured error analysis with corrective recommendations. Overall Summary aggregates per-subtask diagnoses into a task-level judgment. By operating on bounded subtask segments rather than full trajectories, GUIDE mitigates the context overload that degrades existing evaluators as task complexity grows. We validate GUIDE on three benchmarks: an industrial e-commerce dataset of 932 trajectories, AGENTREWARDBENCH spanning five web agent tasks with 1302 trajectories, and AndroidBench for mobile device control. Across all settings, GUIDE substantially outperforms existing evaluators-achieving up to 5.35 percentage points higher accuracy than the strongest baseline-while producing structured diagnostic reports that directly inform agent improvement.

### [152] [MolDA: Molecular Understanding and Generation via Large Language Diffusion Model](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04403)
*Seohyeon Shin,HanJun Choi,Jun-Hyung Park,Hongkook Kim,Mansu Kim*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MolDA, a non-autoregressive, diffusion-based large language model for molecules that better captures global structural constraints than conventional autoregressive LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autoregressive multimodal molecular models generate molecular structures token by token from left to right. This makes it difficult to enforce global chemical constraints such as ring closures and can lead to cumulative structural errors, limiting chemical validity and robustness in tasks like molecule generation, captioning, and property prediction. There is a need for a molecular language model that can reason over and enforce global structure more effectively.

Method: The authors propose MolDA, which replaces the autoregressive backbone with a discrete Large Language Diffusion Model using masked diffusion. A hybrid graph encoder first constructs rich molecular representations that capture both local and global topologies. These graph-based features are then aligned into the language token space via a Q-Former module. The generation process is formulated as masked diffusion with bidirectional iterative denoising, and the authors reformulate Molecular Structure Preference Optimization to fit this diffusion framework, enabling preference-guided learning of chemically valid structures.

Result: MolDA achieves globally coherent and chemically valid molecular structures through its diffusion-based, bidirectional denoising process. It demonstrates improved performance and robustness across multiple molecular tasks, including molecule generation, molecule captioning, and property prediction, compared to autoregressive baselines.

Conclusion: Replacing autoregressive backbones with a discrete diffusion-based framework that integrates graph encoders and Q-Former alignment yields better handling of global molecular constraints. MolDA’s masked diffusion and tailored structure preference optimization lead to more chemically valid, globally coherent molecules and stronger reasoning abilities across several molecular AI tasks.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have significantly advanced molecular discovery, but existing multimodal molecular architectures fundamentally rely on autoregressive (AR) backbones. This strict left-to-right inductive bias is sub-optimal for generating chemically valid molecules, as it struggles to account for non-local global constraints (e.g., ring closures) and often accumulates structural errors during sequential generation. To address these limitations, we propose MolDA (Molecular language model with masked Diffusion with mAsking), a novel multimodal framework that replaces the conventional AR backbone with a discrete Large Language Diffusion Model. MolDA extracts comprehensive structural representations using a hybrid graph encoder, which captures both local and global topologies, and aligns them into the language token space via a Q-Former. Furthermore, we mathematically reformulate Molecular Structure Preference Optimization specifically for the masked diffusion. Through bidirectional iterative denoising, MolDA ensures global structural coherence, chemical validity, and robust reasoning across molecule generation, captioning, and property prediction.

However, as agents increasingly rely on third-party tools and MCP servers, a new class of supply-chain threats has emerged, where malicious behaviors are embedded in seemingly benign tools, silently hijacking agent execution, leaking sensitive data, or triggering unauthorized actions. Despite their growing impact, there is currently no comprehensive benchmark for evaluating such threats. To bridge this gap, we introduce SC-Inject-Bench, a large-scale benchmark comprising over 10,000 malicious MCP tools grounded in a taxonomy of 25+ attack types derived from MITRE ATT&CK targeting supply-chain threats. We observe that existing MCP scanners and semantic guardrails perform poorly on this benchmark. Motivated by this finding, we propose ShieldNet, a network-level guardrail framework that detects supply-chain poisoning by observing real network interactions rather than surface-level tool traces. ShieldNet integrates a man-in-the-middle (MITM) proxy and an event extractor to identify critical network behaviors, which are then processed by a lightweight classifier for attack detection. Extensive experiments show that ShieldNet achieves strong detection performance (up to 0.995 F-1 with only 0.8% false positives) while introducing little runtime overhead, substantially outperforming existing MCP scanners and LLM-based guardrails.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

This can hide inconsistent or biased model behavior, even when predictions look correct. The authors want a way to quantify explanation stability and alignment with intended model objectives under label-preserving perturbations.

Method: They define a metric based on cosine similarity between SHAP attribution vectors for pairs/groups of inputs that share the same label or represent small, label-preserving perturbations. They implement this on sentiment analysis tasks using pre-trained BERT on SST-2, and additionally RoBERTa, DistilBERT, and IMDB. They run experiments to see whether this metric can highlight misaligned predictions and inconsistent reasoning better than standard fidelity metrics.

Result: The metric can identify cases where models, though accurate, rely on unstable or biased attribution patterns for similar inputs. It highlights misaligned predictions and explanation inconsistencies that standard fidelity metrics fail to capture. Robustness tests across multiple models and datasets show that the metric is useful and sensitive to explanation instability under label-preserving variations.

Conclusion: Explanation consistency across similar inputs is crucial for trustworthy AI but is not captured by conventional instance-level XAI evaluations. The proposed cosine-similarity-based metric offers a practical way to quantify rationale stability and detect misaligned or inconsistent model behavior. This framework complements existing fidelity metrics and supports more robust, reliability-focused evaluation in pattern recognition pipelines.

Abstract: Reliable pattern recognition systems should exhibit consistent behavior across similar inputs, and their explanations should remain stable. However, most Explainable AI evaluations remain instance centric and do not explicitly quantify whether attribution patterns are consistent across samples that share the same class or represent small variations of the same input. In this work, we propose a novel metric aimed at assessing the consistency of model explanations, ensuring that models consistently reflect the intended objectives and consistency under label-preserving perturbations. We implement this metric using a pre-trained BERT model on the SST-2 sentiment analysis dataset, with additional robustness tests on RoBERTa, DistilBERT, and IMDB, applying SHAP to compute feature importance for various test samples. The proposed metric quantifies the cosine similarity of SHAP values for inputs with the same label, aiming to detect inconsistent behaviors, such as biased reliance on certain features or failure to maintain consistent reasoning for similar predictions. Through a series of experiments, we evaluate the ability of this metric to identify misaligned predictions and inconsistencies in model explanations. These experiments are compared against standard fidelity metrics to assess whether the new metric can effectively identify when a model's behavior deviates from its intended objectives. The proposed framework provides a deeper understanding of model behavior by enabling more robust verification of rationale stability, which is critical for building trustworthy AI systems. By quantifying whether models rely on consistent attribution patterns for similar inputs, the proposed approach supports more robust evaluation of model behavior in practical pattern recognition pipelines. Our code is publicly available at https://github.com/anmspro/ESS-XAI-Stability.

### [155] [The Topology of Multimodal Fusion: Why Current Architectures Fail at Creative Cognition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04465)
*Xiujiang Tan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Identifies a topological, not just parametric, limitation in current multimodal AI (CLIP, GPT-4V, diffusion) and proposes a new topology-inspired framework (UOO) grounded in philosophy, cognitive science, and differential geometry, plus benchmarks and an experimental roadmap to test it.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current multimodal AI models rely on architectures that implicitly assume that different modalities (text, image, etc.) remain separable and only meet via alignment, attention, or decoding stages. The authors argue this "contact topology" structurally limits how deeply modalities can interpenetrate conceptually, especially for analogy, creativity, and cross-civilizational understanding. They want to expose this hidden geometric prior and propose a new framework that can natively model a third state where saying (language) and showing (perception) fuse rather than merely align.

Method: 1) Philosophical analysis: Reinterpret Wittgenstein’s saying/showing distinction using Chinese craft epistemology, introducing xiang (operative schema) as a third, emergent state at the intersection of saying and showing. Result: In this abstract, the results are primarily conceptual and design-level, not empirical. The authors: (a) identify modal separability/contact topology as a shared structural prior in major multimodal AI paradigms, (b) construct a unified philosophical–cognitive–mathematical framework that predicts the existence and importance of a third, fused state (xiang) beyond simple alignment, (c) specify a candidate implementation approach (UOO via Neural ODEs with topological regularization) that could realize such a state, and (d) introduce two new benchmark families (ANALOGY-MM and META-TOP) and a rigorous, phased experimental roadmap with explicit termination criteria. No concrete experimental performance numbers or deployments are reported in the abstract.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that current multimodal architectures are structurally constrained by an implicit contact topology that enforces modal separability, limiting their ability to model deeply fused, analogical, and culturally diverse cognition. By grounding a new architecture (UOO) in the cruciform philosophical framework, DMN/ECN/SN dynamics, and fiber-bundle/Yang–Mills mathematics, they argue it is possible in principle to transcend this limitation. The proposed Neural ODE-based implementation and novel benchmarks offer a testable research program: if models with topological regularization outperform contact-topology baselines on analogy and cross-civilizational tasks—while respecting the predefined termination criteria—this would support the claim that a non-separable, topology-aware approach is a necessary next step for multimodal AI.

Abstract: This paper identifies a structural limitation in current multimodal AI architectures that is topological rather than parametric. Contrastive alignment (CLIP), cross-attention fusion (GPT-4V/Gemini), and diffusion-based generation share a common geometric prior -- modal separability -- which we term contact topology. The argument rests on three pillars with philosophy as the generative center. The philosophical pillar reinterprets Wittgenstein's saying/showing distinction as a problem rather than a conclusion: where Wittgenstein chose silence, the Chinese craft epistemology tradition responded with xiang (operative schema) -- the third state emerging when saying and showing interpenetrate. A cruciform framework (dao/qi x saying/showing) positions xiang at the intersection, executing dual huacai (transformation-and-cutting) along both axes. This generates a dual-layer dynamics: chuanghua (creative transformation as spontaneous event) and huacai (its institutionalization into repeatable form). The cognitive science pillar reinterprets DMN/ECN/SN tripartite co-activation through the pathological mirror: overlap isomorphism vs. superimposition collapse in a 2D parameter space (coupling intensity x regulatory capacity). The mathematical pillar formalizes these via fiber bundles and Yang-Mills curvature, with the cruciform structure mapped to fiber bundle language. We propose UOO implementation via Neural ODEs with topological regularization, the ANALOGY-MM benchmark with error-type-ratio metric, and the META-TOP three-tier benchmark testing cross-civilizational topological isomorphism across seven archetypes. A phased experimental roadmap with explicit termination criteria ensures clean exit if falsified.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Deep research agents need to reuse past experiences to reason efficiently and improve autonomously, but current memory approaches mostly do naive similarity-based retrieval of full trajectories, leading to poor memory evolution and growing storage and retrieval costs as experience accumulates.

Method: They design the Memory Intelligence Agent (MIA) with a Manager–Planner–Executor architecture: (1) a non-parametric Memory Manager that stores compressed historical search trajectories, (2) a parametric Planner agent that generates search plans for new questions, and (3) an Executor agent that executes searches and analyzes information following the plan. Abstract: Deep research agents (DRAs) integrate LLM reasoning with external tools. Memory systems enable DRAs to leverage historical experiences, which are essential for efficient reasoning and autonomous evolution. Existing methods rely on retrieving similar trajectories from memory to aid reasoning, while suffering from key limitations of ineffective memory evolution and increasing storage and retrieval costs. To address these problems, we propose a novel Memory Intelligence Agent (MIA) framework, consisting of a Manager-Planner-Executor architecture. Memory Manager is a non-parametric memory system that can store compressed historical search trajectories. Planner is a parametric memory agent that can produce search plans for questions. Executor is another agent that can search and analyze information guided by the search plan. To build the MIA framework, we first adopt an alternating reinforcement learning paradigm to enhance cooperation between the Planner and the Executor. Furthermore, we enable the Planner to continuously evolve during test-time learning, with updates performed on-the-fly alongside inference without interrupting the reasoning process. Additionally, we establish a bidirectional conversion loop between parametric and non-parametric memories to achieve efficient memory evolution. Finally, we incorporate a reflection and an unsupervised judgment mechanisms to boost reasoning and self-evolution in the open world. Extensive experiments across eleven benchmarks demonstrate the superiority of MIA.

### [159] [SuperLocalMemory V3.3: The Living Brain -- Biologically-Inspired Forgetting, Cognitive Quantization, and Multi-Channel Retrieval for Zero-LLM Agent Memory Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04514)
*Varun Pratap Bhardwaj*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SuperLocalMemory V3.3 is a local-first, cognitively-inspired memory system for AI coding agents that implements a full human-like memory lifecycle and multi-channel retrieval, improving discriminative power and performance on the LoCoMo benchmark while running fully on CPU.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI coding agents have strong parametric knowledge but very limited conversational and episodic memory. Existing memory architectures rely on simple vector databases with single-channel similarity search, often depend on cloud LLM calls, and lack mechanisms analogous to human cognitive memory (e.g., forgetting curves, multi-channel retrieval, consolidation). The paper aims to design a local, LLM-independent, cognitively-grounded memory system that overcomes these limitations and yields reliably useful, scalable agent memory.

Method: The authors extend their prior SuperLocalMemory V3.2 framework using information geometry on Gaussian statistical manifolds. They propose: (1) FRQAD, a Fisher-Rao-based distance metric to reliably prefer high-fidelity over quantized embeddings; (2) Ebbinghaus Adaptive Forgetting, a mathematically modeled forgetting curve coupled with lifecycle-aware progressive embedding quantization; (3) a 7-channel cognitive retrieval system combining semantic similarity, keyword search, entity graphs, temporal cues, spreading activation, consolidation-aware retrieval, and Hopfield-like associative recall; (4) parameterization of memory to support Long-Term Implicit memory via soft prompts; and (5) an automated pipeline that manages the whole memory lifecycle (storage, decay, compression, retrieval, consolidation) without manual intervention, running fully locally on CPU.

Result: On the LoCoMo benchmark, SuperLocalMemory V3.3 achieves 70.4% accuracy in Mode A, which uses zero LLM calls, with significant gains in multi-hop (+23.8 percentage points) and adversarial (+12.7 percentage points) settings relative to baselines. FRQAD yields perfect precision (100%) at choosing full-precision over quantized embeddings compared to 85.6% for cosine similarity. The new forgetting and retrieval mechanisms increase discriminative power by 6.7x. While V3.3 scores slightly lower than V3.2 on Mode A (74.8%) and Mode C (87.7%), this reflects a deliberate architectural trade-off rather than regression. The system is open source under Elastic License 2.0, CPU-only, and in practical use with thousands of monthly downloads.

Conclusion: A cognitively inspired, mathematically grounded local memory system can substantially improve AI agents’ practical memory capabilities without relying on remote LLMs. By modeling the full memory lifecycle—including quantization-aware distance metrics, adaptive forgetting linked to compression, multi-channel cognitive retrieval, and implicit long-term memory via soft prompts—SuperLocalMemory V3.3 delivers strong benchmark performance and deployable, CPU-only infrastructure. The work suggests that future agent memory architectures should treat memory as a dynamic, structured cognitive process rather than static vector storage.

Abstract: AI coding agents operate in a paradox: they possess vast parametric knowledge yet cannot remember a conversation from an hour ago. Existing memory systems store text in vector databases with single-channel retrieval, require cloud LLMs for core operations, and implement none of the cognitive processes that make human memory effective.
  We present SuperLocalMemory V3.3 ("The Living Brain"), a local-first agent memory system implementing the full cognitive memory taxonomy with mathematical lifecycle dynamics. Building on the information-geometric foundations of V3.2 (arXiv:2603.14588), we introduce five contributions: (1) Fisher-Rao Quantization-Aware Distance (FRQAD) -- a new metric on the Gaussian statistical manifold achieving 100% precision at preferring high-fidelity embeddings over quantized ones (vs 85.6% for cosine), with zero prior art; (2) Ebbinghaus Adaptive Forgetting with lifecycle-aware quantization -- the first mathematical forgetting curve in local agent memory coupled to progressive embedding compression, achieving 6.7x discriminative power; (3) 7-channel cognitive retrieval spanning semantic, keyword, entity graph, temporal, spreading activation, consolidation, and Hopfield associative channels, achieving 70.4% on LoCoMo in zero-LLM Mode A; (4) memory parameterization implementing Long-Term Implicit memory via soft prompts; (5) zero-friction auto-cognitive pipeline automating the complete memory lifecycle.
  On LoCoMo, V3.3 achieves 70.4% in Mode A (zero-LLM), with +23.8pp on multi-hop and +12.7pp on adversarial. V3.2 achieved 74.8% Mode A and 87.7% Mode C; the 4.4pp gap reflects a deliberate architectural trade-off. SLM V3.3 is open source under the Elastic License 2.0, runs entirely on CPU, with over 5,000 monthly downloads.

### [160] [Receding-Horizon Control via Drifting Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04528)
*Daniele Foffano,Alessio Russo,Alexandre Proutiere*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes Drifting MPC, an offline trajectory optimization method that learns a trajectory distribution biased toward optimality while remaining close to an offline dataset, enabling near‑optimal, fast trajectory generation under unknown dynamics without simulators.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In many control and planning problems, system dynamics are unknown and we cannot simulate them, but we may have an offline dataset of trajectories. Pure data-driven trajectory generation via distribution matching only reproduces behavior in the dataset and does not explicitly optimize a given cost, leading to potentially suboptimal trajectories. There is a need for a method that uses offline data to remain in-distribution while still biasing trajectories toward minimizing a desired cost, all without a dynamics model or simulator and with efficient inference.

Method: Introduce Drifting MPC, which combines drifting generative models (that iteratively update a distribution) with receding-horizon model predictive control (MPC). From offline trajectories, it learns a conditional distribution over future trajectories. The learning objective explicitly trades off trajectory optimality (under a given cost) against staying close to the offline prior distribution induced by the dataset. The method produces a unique solution distribution that is data-supported yet cost-biased, and allows one-step inference using the trained drifting model to generate candidate trajectories at planning time under unknown dynamics.

Result: Theoretically, they show that the trajectory distribution learned by Drifting MPC uniquely optimizes an objective balancing cost minimization and divergence from the offline prior. Empirically, they demonstrate that Drifting MPC produces trajectories that are near-optimal according to the specified cost, maintains the fast one-step inference characteristic of drifting generative models, and significantly reduces trajectory generation time compared with diffusion-model-based baseline methods.

Conclusion: Drifting MPC offers an effective offline trajectory optimization framework for systems with unknown dynamics and no available simulators. By jointly leveraging an offline dataset and an optimization objective, it learns a conditional trajectory distribution that is both data-consistent and biased toward optimal plans. This yields near-optimal, efficiently generated trajectories and outperforms diffusion-based baselines in generation speed, making it a practical solution for planning in offline, model-free settings.

Abstract: We study the problem of trajectory optimization in settings where the system dynamics are unknown and it is not possible to simulate trajectories through a surrogate model. When an offline dataset of trajectories is available, an agent could directly learn a trajectory generator by distribution matching. However, this approach only recovers the behavior distribution in the dataset, and does not in general produce a model that minimizes a desired cost criterion. In this work, we propose Drifting MPC, an offline trajectory optimization framework that combines drifting generative models with receding-horizon planning under unknown dynamics. The goal of Drifting MPC is to learn, from an offline dataset of trajectories, a conditional distribution over trajectories that is both supported by the data and biased toward optimal plans. We show that the resulting distribution learned by Drifting MPC is the unique solution of an objective that trades off optimality with closeness to the offline prior. Empirically, we show that Drifting MPC can generate near-optimal trajectories while retaining the one-step inference efficiency of drifting models and substantially reducing generation time relative to diffusion-based baselines.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard adaptive agents can adjust behavior but often lack an explicit, history-sensitive internal perspective that reorganizes how they perceive identical inputs. The paper asks what minimal internal structure is required for an artificial agent to maintain such a perspective and have its perceptual encoding shaped by its experiential history, beyond simple stimulus-response adaptation.

Method: Introduce an agent architecture with a slow-changing latent variable g that (1) modulates the perceptual encoding process (feedback into perception) and (2) is itself updated through ongoing perceptual processing. Implement and test this in a simple gridworld environment with a fixed spatial layout but with sensory perturbations (e.g., changes or noise in inputs). Compare different update regimes for g: adaptive self-modulation vs rigid or always-open updating, and analyze plasticity, representation, and behavior under perturbations and after returning to nominal conditions.

Result: (1) Past perturbations leave a quantifiable trace in the agent's adaptive plasticity even after the environment returns to nominal conditions, indicating history-dependence. (2) The perspective latent g restructures perceptual encodings so that the same observations are represented differently depending on the agent's prior experience. (3) Only the adaptively self-modulating regime for g produces a growth-then-stabilization pattern in the dynamics of g, in contrast to rigid or constantly updating regimes. Throughout, gross behavioral performance remains stable, indicating that the main changes are in internal perceptual organization rather than overt actions.

Conclusion: A minimal mechanism—namely, a slow perspective latent that both modulates and is updated by perception—can endow artificial agents with history-dependent, perspectival organization. This yields stable behavior but experience-shaped internal representations where past perturbations leave enduring traces, demonstrating that rich, perspective-like internal structure can emerge from relatively simple architectural choices.

Abstract: What kind of internal organization would allow an artificial agent not only to adapt its behavior, but to sustain a history-sensitive perspective on its world? I present a minimal architecture in which a slow perspective latent $g$ feeds back into perception and is itself updated through perceptual processing. This allows identical observations to be encoded differently depending on the agent's accumulated stance. The model is evaluated in a minimal gridworld with a fixed spatial scaffold and sensory perturbations. Across analyses, three results emerge: first, perturbation history leaves measurable residue in adaptive plasticity after nominal conditions are restored. Second, the perspective latent reorganizes perceptual encoding, such that identical observations are represented differently depending on prior experience. Third, only adaptive self-modulation yields the characteristic growth-then-stabilization dynamic, unlike rigid or always-open update regimes. Gross behavior remains stable throughout, suggesting that the dominant reorganization is perceptual rather than behavioral. Together, these findings identify a minimal mechanism for history-dependent perspectival organization in artificial agents.

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They show that small language models used as search agents don’t use tools enough and hallucinate more, then propose a light fine-tuning method that makes them search and ground answers better, reaching large-model-level performance.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Search-augmented agents are good for knowledge-intensive tasks, but they usually rely on large, expensive LLMs. People try to distill agent behavior into smaller, cheaper SLMs, yet these SLM agents underperform, search less often, and hallucinate more despite having less built-in knowledge. There is a need for a method that makes SLMs reliably use search tools and ground their answers in retrieved evidence so that they can match LLM performance at lower cost.

Method: They comprehensively evaluate SLM agents on complex multi-hop reasoning benchmarks and diagnose their behavior, finding underuse of search and higher hallucination rates. The training signal encourages consistent search calls and evidence-based generation instead of adaptive, highly variable tool usage patterns.

Behavioral analysis shows that the fine-tuned SLMs call search tools more appropriately and hallucinate less.

Conclusion: The work concludes that naïvely distilling agent behaviors from LLMs into SLMs is insufficient: SLMs tend to search too little and hallucinate more, especially on multi-hop reasoning tasks. A targeted, lightweight fine-tuning procedure that enforces consistent, evidence-grounded search and answer generation can close most of the gap to LLM performance. Moreover, attempts to make SLMs use highly adaptive search strategies often hurt reliability, so consistent search behavior is crucial for stable reasoning performance.

Abstract: Agents equipped with search tools have emerged as effective solutions for knowledge-intensive tasks. While Large Language Models (LLMs) exhibit strong reasoning capabilities, their high computational cost limits practical deployment for search agents. Consequently, recent work has focused on distilling agentic behaviors from LLMs into Small Language Models (SLMs). Through comprehensive evaluation on complex multi-hop reasoning tasks, we find that despite possessing less parametric knowledge, SLMs invoke search tools less frequently and are more prone to hallucinations. To address this issue, we propose \policy, a lightweight fine-tuning approach that explicitly trains SLMs to reliably retrieve and generate answers grounded in retrieved evidence. Compared to agent distillation from LLMs, our approach improves performance by 17.3 scores on Bamboogle and 15.3 scores on HotpotQA, achieving LLM-level results across benchmarks. Our further analysis reveals that adaptive search strategies in SLMs often degrade performance, highlighting the necessity of consistent search behavior for reliable reasoning.

### [164] [Springdrift: An Auditable Persistent Runtime for LLM Agents with Case-Based Memory, Normative Safety, and Ambient Self-Perception](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04660)
*Seamus Brady*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Springdrift is a persistent runtime for long-lived LLM agents (“Artificial Retainers”) that provides durable memory, safety gating, and self-monitoring to enable cross-session, cross-channel, and auditable behavior in real deployments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most current LLM-based assistants are session-bounded: they forget across sessions, lack durable and auditable memory, and provide limited accountability or continuity for real-world, long-running tasks. There is also a conceptual gap between generic software assistants/autonomous agents and the kind of accountable, semi-autonomous systems that could operate in an ongoing, trust-based relationship with a single principal (e.g., like a professional retainer). The authors want a system that can operate reliably over weeks, diagnose its own issues, maintain context across modalities, and offer forensic reconstruction of its decisions.

Method: They design Springdrift, a persistent runtime for LLM agents with several architectural components: (1) an auditable execution substrate using append-only memory, supervised processes, and git-backed state recovery; (2) a case-based reasoning memory layer with hybrid retrieval, empirically compared to a dense cosine similarity baseline; (3) a deterministic “normative calculus” for safety gating that leaves an auditable trail of axioms behind each decision; and (4) a structured self-state representation called the “sensorium,” which is injected each reasoning cycle so the agent maintains continuous self-perception without needing extra tool calls. They deploy a single Springdrift agent for 23 days and observe its behavior qualitatively as a systems case study rather than a benchmark evaluation.

Result: In the 23‑day deployment (19 operating days), the single Springdrift agent demonstrated several emergent behaviors without explicit prompting: it diagnosed infrastructure bugs in its own environment, classified different failure modes, discovered an architectural vulnerability, and maintained coherent context across email and web interaction channels. The system successfully provided cross-session continuity, cross-channel state, and auditable decision traces. The hybrid memory retrieval outperformed or was at least meaningfully compared against a dense cosine similarity baseline, though detailed metrics are not provided in the abstract.

Conclusion: The authors argue that the architectural combination in Springdrift—persistent and auditable memory, deterministic safety gating, and structured self-state—enables a new category of systems they term “Artificial Retainers.” These differ from generic software assistants and fully autonomous agents by having persistent memory, defined authority, domain‑specific autonomy, and forensic accountability for a specific principal, akin to professional retainers or trained working animals. The report is positioned as an existence proof and design case study, showing what such a system can do in practice, not as a generalizable, benchmarked performance claim. They indicate that code and operational artefacts will be released, inviting replication and further study.

Abstract: We present Springdrift, a persistent runtime for long-lived LLM agents. The system integrates an auditable execution substrate (append-only memory, supervised processes, git-backed recovery), a case-based reasoning memory layer with hybrid retrieval (evaluated against a dense cosine baseline), a deterministic normative calculus for safety gating with auditable axiom trails, and continuous ambient self-perception via a structured self-state representation (the sensorium) injected each cycle without tool calls. These properties support behaviours difficult to achieve in session-bounded systems: cross-session task continuity, cross-channel context maintenance, end-to-end forensic reconstruction of decisions, and self-diagnostic behaviour. We report on a single-instance deployment over 23 days (19 operating days), during which the agent diagnosed its own infrastructure bugs, classified failure modes, identified an architectural vulnerability, and maintained context across email and web channels -- without explicit instruction. We introduce the term Artificial Retainer for this category: a non-human system with persistent memory, defined authority, domain-specific autonomy, and forensic accountability in an ongoing relationship with a specific principal -- distinguished from software assistants and autonomous agents, drawing on professional retainer relationships and the bounded autonomy of trained working animals. This is a technical report on a systems design and deployment case study, not a benchmark-driven evaluation. Evidence is from a single instance with a single operator, presented as illustration of what these architectural properties can support in practice. Implemented in approximately Gleam on Erlang/OTP. Code, artefacts, and redacted operational logs will be available at https://github.com/seamus-brady/springdrift upon publication.
