### [165] [On the "Causality" Step in Policy Gradient Derivations: A Pedagogical Reconciliation of Full Return and Reward-to-Go](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04686)
*Nima H. Siboni*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper provides a rigorous mathematical derivation showing why, in policy gradient methods, the reward-to-go can replace the full trajectory return in the REINFORCE estimator, based on prefix trajectory distributions and the score-function identity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard expositions of policy gradients state that the reward-to-go can substitute for the full return in the REINFORCE estimator by an informal "causality" argument, but they often lack a clear, rigorous explanation of how past rewards vanish from the gradient expression. This can leave conceptual gaps for readers trying to understand the foundations of the method.

Method: The paper reformulates the policy gradient objective in terms of prefix trajectory distributions and applies the score-function (log-derivative) identity. By decomposing the objective over prefixes of trajectories, it isolates the contribution of different time steps and explicitly shows which reward terms affect the gradient and which do not.

Result: Using this prefix-trajectory decomposition and the score-function identity, the paper derives the standard reward-to-go formulation of the REINFORCE estimator, demonstrating precisely how and why the past-reward terms cancel or drop out. The final estimator is the same as the standard one used in practice, but now obtained via a fully explicit derivation.

Conclusion: Reward-to-go is not merely an unbiased, heuristic replacement for the full return; it emerges directly when the objective is decomposed over prefix trajectories and analyzed with the score-function identity. In this view, the usual "causality" explanation appears as a corollary of the formal derivation rather than as an extra principle, clarifying the conceptual foundations of policy gradient methods.

Abstract: In introductory presentations of policy gradients, one often derives the REINFORCE estimator using the full trajectory return and then states, by ``causality,'' that the full return may be replaced by the reward-to-go. Although this statement is correct, it is frequently presented at a level of rigor that leaves unclear where the past-reward terms disappear. This short paper isolates that step and gives a mathematically explicit derivation based on prefix trajectory distributions and the score-function identity. The resulting account does not change the estimator. Its contribution is conceptual: instead of presenting reward-to-go as a post hoc unbiased replacement for full return, it shows that reward-to-go arises directly once the objective is decomposed over prefix trajectories. In this formulation, the usual causality argument is recovered as a corollary of the derivation rather than as an additional heuristic principle.

### [166] [AI Assistance Reduces Persistence and Hurts Independent Performance](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04721)

  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors want to understand what this short-term optimization does to users’ motivation, persistence, and independent competence over time.

Method: The authors ran a set of randomized controlled trials with 1,222 participants interacting with AI on tasks such as math reasoning and reading comprehension. Result: AI assistance improved immediate task performance, but after even short exposure, people who had used AI performed significantly worse when later working without it and were more likely to give up. This pattern held across multiple task types, indicating reduced persistence and impaired unassisted performance as causal effects of AI help.

Conclusion: AI systems, as currently designed for instant answers, can undermine persistence and long-term skill development by conditioning users to expect effortless solutions. Future AI design should explicitly aim to scaffold learning and long-term competence—e.g., by encouraging struggle, gradual guidance, and progress tracking—instead of solely maximizing short-term task completion.

Abstract: People often optimize for long-term goals in collaboration: A mentor or companion doesn't just answer questions, but also scaffolds learning, tracks progress, and prioritizes the other person's growth over immediate results. In contrast, current AI systems are fundamentally short-sighted collaborators - optimized for providing instant and complete responses, without ever saying no (unless for safety reasons). What are the consequences of this dynamic? Here, through a series of randomized controlled trials on human-AI interactions (N = 1,222), we provide causal evidence for two key consequences of AI assistance: reduced persistence and impairment of unassisted performance. Across a variety of tasks, including mathematical reasoning and reading comprehension, we find that although AI assistance improves performance in the short-term, people perform significantly worse without AI and are more likely to give up. Notably, these effects emerge after only brief interactions with AI (approximately 10 minutes). These findings are particularly concerning because persistence is foundational to skill acquisition and is one of the strongest predictors of long-term learning. We posit that persistence is reduced because AI conditions people to expect immediate answers, thereby denying them the experience of working through challenges on their own. These results suggest the need for AI model development to prioritize scaffolding long-term competence alongside immediate task completion.

### [169] [MemMachine: A Ground-Truth-Preserving Memory System for Personalized AI Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04853)
*Shu Wang,Edwin Yu,Oscar Love,Tom Zhang,Tom Wong,Steve Scargall,Charles Fan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: MemMachine is an open-source, ground-truth-preserving memory system for LLM agents that combines episodic and profile memories with contextualized retrieval, delivering more accurate and efficient long-term personalization and reasoning than standard RAG baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors aim to build a more robust, efficient, and accurate memory system that preserves conversational ground truth, retrieves relevant information spanning multiple turns, and is cost-efficient in terms of token usage.

Method: The authors design MemMachine, a memory architecture that explicitly separates short-term, long-term episodic, and profile memory while storing full conversational episodes instead of extracted snippets. Retrieval is contextualized via nucleus-style matching that expands hits with surrounding context to capture multi-turn evidence. They perform a six-dimension ablation on LongMemEvalS, systematically varying ingestion and retrieval configurations. Optimizations include retrieval depth tuning, context formatting strategies, search prompt design, and query bias correction, as well as ingestion variants like sentence chunking. They also build a companion Retrieval Agent that adaptively chooses between direct retrieval, parallel question decomposition, and iterative chain-of-query strategies depending on the query, and evaluate across multiple long-term memory and multi-hop QA benchmarks.

Result: MemMachine achieves strong accuracy-efficiency tradeoffs: on LoCoMo, it obtains 0.9169 accuracy using gpt4.1-mini. When paired with optimized prompts, GPT-5-mini outperforms GPT-5 by 2.6%, yielding the most cost-efficient combination. Compared to Mem0, MemMachine uses about 80% fewer input tokens at similar conditions. The Retrieval Agent attains 93.2% on HotpotQA-hard and 92.6% on WikiMultiHop under randomized-noise settings.

Conclusion: Preserving episodic ground truth and enriching it with adaptive, contextualized retrieval leads to robust, efficient long-term memory for personalized LLM agents. MemMachine’s architecture, which emphasizes full-episode storage and retrieval-stage optimization, outperforms prior memory systems both in accuracy and token efficiency, while the companion Retrieval Agent shows that dynamic routing among retrieval strategies can handle complex, noisy multi-hop reasoning tasks effectively.

Abstract: Large Language Model (LLM) agents require persistent memory to maintain personalization, factual continuity, and long-horizon reasoning, yet standard context-window and retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) pipelines degrade over multi-session interactions. GPT-5-mini exceeds GPT-5 by 2.6 percent when paired with optimized prompts, making it the most cost-efficient setup. Compared to Mem0, MemMachine uses roughly 80 percent fewer input tokens under matched conditions. A companion Retrieval Agent adaptively routes queries among direct retrieval, parallel decomposition, or iterative chain-of-query strategies, achieving 93.2 percent on HotpotQA-hard and 92.6 percent on WikiMultiHop under randomized-noise conditions. ### [170] [Incompleteness of AI Safety Verification via Kolmogorov Complexity](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04876)
*Munawar Hasan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that there are fundamental information-theoretic limits to formally verifying that AI systems comply with safety and policy constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In safety-critical domains, we want strong guarantees that AI systems behave safely and comply with policies. Existing verification challenges are usually blamed on computational complexity or model expressiveness. The authors aim to understand whether there are deeper, inherent limits to what formal verification can guarantee, regardless of computational power.

Method: They formalize policy compliance as a verification problem over encoded system behaviors and analyze it using tools from Kolmogorov complexity. Considering any fixed sound computably enumerable verifier, they reason about which instances (behaviors) it can certify as policy-compliant as their descriptional complexity grows.

Result: They prove an incompleteness theorem: for every such verifier, there exists a complexity threshold beyond which there are true policy-compliant instances that the verifier can no longer certify. Thus, for instances of sufficiently high Kolmogorov complexity, some genuinely compliant cases will necessarily escape verification by that verifier.

Conclusion: No finite, sound, computably enumerable verifier can certify all policy-compliant instances of arbitrarily high complexity. This constitutes a fundamental, information-theoretic limitation of AI safety verification that does not depend on computational resources, and suggests the need for proof-carrying or instance-specific correctness guarantees instead of relying solely on a fixed global verifier.

Abstract: Ensuring that artificial intelligence (AI) systems satisfy formal safety and policy constraints is a central challenge in safety-critical domains. While limitations of verification are often attributed to combinatorial complexity and model expressiveness, we show that they arise from intrinsic information-theoretic limits. We formalize policy compliance as a verification problem over encoded system behaviors and analyze it using Kolmogorov complexity. We prove an incompleteness result: for any fixed sound computably enumerable verifier, there exists a threshold beyond which true policy-compliant instances cannot be certified once their complexity exceeds that threshold. Consequently, no finite formal verifier can certify all policy-compliant instances of arbitrarily high complexity. This reveals a fundamental limitation of AI safety verification independent of computational resources, and motivates proof-carrying approaches that provide instance-level correctness guarantees.

</details>


### [171] [Learning, Potential, and Retention: An Approach for Evaluating Adaptive AI-Enabled Medical Devices](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04878)
*Alexis Burgon,Berkman Sahiner,Nicholas A Petrick,Gene Pennello,Ravi K Samala*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors define three complementary quantitative measures: (1) learning, capturing how much a model improves on the current data after an update; (2) potential, characterizing how changes in the evaluation dataset alone would affect performance; and (3) retention, measuring how well the model preserves previously acquired knowledge across sequential modifications. They then run case studies with simulated population shifts to illustrate these metrics under different adaptation dynamics.

Result: In simulation studies with controlled population shifts, the framework shows that gradual data distribution changes allow models to achieve both strong learning and high retention, indicating stable adaptation. In contrast, rapid shifts in the data expose a trade-off: models that adapt quickly (high plasticity) may suffer reduced retention of prior knowledge, while models that preserve past knowledge may adapt less effectively to the new data.

Conclusion: The three-metric framework helps disentangle model-driven versus data-driven performance changes in adaptive AI systems for medical devices. It offers regulators and developers a structured way to track safety and effectiveness over sequential updates, highlighting when adaptation is stable versus when it risks forgetting or instability, thereby supporting more rigorous regulatory science for continuously learning medical AI.

Abstract: This work addresses challenges in evaluating adaptive artificial intelligence (AI) models for medical devices, where iterative updates to both models and evaluation datasets complicate performance assessment. We introduce a novel approach with three complementary measurements: learning (model improvement on current data), potential (dataset-driven performance shifts), and retention (knowledge preservation across modification steps), to disentangle performance changes caused by model adaptations versus dynamic environments. Case studies using simulated population shifts demonstrate the approach's utility: gradual transitions enable stable learning and retention, while rapid shifts reveal trade-offs between plasticity and stability. These measurements provide practical insights for regulatory science, enabling rigorous assessment of the safety and effectiveness of adaptive AI systems over sequential modifications.

</details>


### [172] [QED-Nano: Teaching a Tiny Model to Prove Hard Theorems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04898)
*LM-Provers,Yuxiao Qu,Amrith Setlur,Jasper Dekoninck,Edward Beeching,Jia Li,Ian Wu,Lewis Tunstall,Aviral Kumar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: QED-Nano is a 4B open model trained with SFT plus RL and a reasoning-cache scaffold to reach near-proprietary performance on Olympiad-level proofs, with full pipeline and data released.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Impressive but opaque proprietary AI systems can now solve Olympiad-level math proofs, yet they are expensive, non-reproducible, and hard to study, raising the need for small, open, and competitive reasoning models with transparent training pipelines.

Method: They build QED-Nano, a 4B model post-trained in three stages: (1) supervised fine-tuning on Olympiad-style proofs distilled from DeepSeek-Math-V2 to learn strong proof-writing style; (2) reinforcement learning using rubric-based rewards to improve proof quality; and (3) an expanded RL phase that incorporates a reasoning cache, which breaks long proofs into summarize-and-refine iterations, enabling stronger test-time reasoning without huge base models.

Result: QED-Nano, despite being only 4B parameters, surpasses larger open models like Nomos-1 and GPT-OSS-120B on proof generation for Olympiad-level problems and achieves performance close to proprietary systems such as Gemini 3 Pro, all while being much cheaper to run.

Conclusion: Small, openly trained models can reach competitive Olympiad-level proof performance when equipped with a tailored post-training pipeline combining SFT, rubric-based RL, and reasoning-cache-based scaffolding; releasing the full QED-Nano pipeline, models, datasets, and code aims to catalyze further open research on mathematical reasoning.

Abstract: Proprietary AI systems have recently demonstrated impressive capabilities on complex proof-based problems, with gold-level performance reported at the 2025 International Mathematical Olympiad (IMO). However, the training pipelines behind these systems remain largely undisclosed, and their reliance on large "internal" models and scaffolds makes them expensive to run, difficult to reproduce, and hard to study or improve upon. This raises a central question: can small, open models also be trained to achieve competitive reasoning performance on difficult Olympiad-level math? In this paper, we answer this question by building QED-Nano, a 4B model post-trained for Olympiad-level proofs. Our training recipe has three stages: (1) supervised fine-tuning to imbue good proof-writing styles by distilling from DeepSeek-Math-V2, (2) reinforcement learning (RL) with rubric-based rewards, and (3) expanding RL with a reasoning cache, which decomposes long proofs into iterative summarize-and-refine cycles and enables stronger test-time reasoning. QED-Nano surpasses the proof-generation performance of much larger open models, including Nomos-1 and GPT-OSS-120B, and approaches the performance of proprietary models like Gemini 3 Pro, at a fraction of the inference cost. To support further research on open mathematical reasoning, we release the full QED-Nano pipeline, including the QED-Nano and QED-Nano-SFT models, the FineProofs-SFT and FineProofs-RL datasets, and the training and evaluation code.

</details>
